## C. VC JOB CYCLE 

**==> picture [313 x 177] intentionally omitted <==**

## 1. Fundraise 

On the one hand, as VCs, we are on the vanguard of innovation. We invest in exciting technologies that can and will change the world.[9] However, from a different perspective, we are money managers who cling to conventions and are very, very slow to change. Raising money is the old school part of venture capital that hasn’t changed fundamentally since the industry got started over a half century ago. 

This is no surprise if we look upstream and see where the money is coming from. Pension funds and insurance companies are not exactly big risk takers. Hence, VCs set up our funds just like private equity funds were doing back in the 1950’s. VC funds are limited liability partnerships in which the VCs are the GPs (general partners) and the pension funds, banks, endowments and other investors are the LPs (limited partners). 

As the title implies, the LPs have a limited role. They will have no active participation in our partnership and will have no liability beyond the cash they’ve invested. All they do is supply cash and get cash in return. 

## Fundraising Strategy 

It can be surprising to learn that, as VCs, we pitch to our investors in much the same way as startups pitch to us. Just as startups must put together 

a fundraising strategy and pitch to numerous investors, we also go on a roadshow to present our firm as an investment opportunity for institutions. 

Both VCs and startups present a specific amount being raised and a strategy for the use of funds. While startups are presenting a series of milestones on the way to a successful exit, we pitch a particular fund size and strategy that should match the competencies of our partners. 

The main variables that determine the size of the fund are the number of partners, the number of deals each partner can handle and the stage of financing that the firm will target. Earlier stage generally will need less capital. Later stage needs more. 

Let’s say, for example, that we have three partners and we would like to invest in early stage startups, say $5-7M per startup. If we believe that each partner can handle one new deal per year for a five-year investment period, we’d be looking at a total of 15 startups in our portfolio. At $7M/startup, we’d need to raise about a $100M fund for this strategy, excluding management fees, which we’ll discuss shortly. 

**==> picture [458 x 107] intentionally omitted <==**

If we choose to alter our strategy to be a little bit later stage, which would require more capital per startup, say $12-15M per investment, we might need to raise a $175M fund. Add a partner, and we may be able to increase our size we be portfolio by 1/3, up to 20 startups. Now might looking at a $250M raise. 

As GPs, we are constantly working our LP network to gauge the temperature of the fundraising market. That is, we are asking our LPs and prospective LPs what they’d want to see. Just as startups create an MVP 

(minimum viable product) and test it with early customers, we will start floating the idea of our new fund to LPs to gauge interest. We’ll iterate our fundraising strategy based on their feedback. 

The fundraising process officially begins with our announcing that we are raising a new fund with a specific target fund size and strategic focus. Ideally, before we’ve even announced, we’d like to have a soft commitment from an anchor LP, one that will represent a large stake in the fund, perhaps in the 10-20% range. 

The fundraising process can go quickly if we already have a great track record for returns to LPs and the public markets are up, or it could take more than a year if markets are in a downturn or we are relatively new to venture capital or are trying a new strategy. 

Why does it matter if public markets are up? Isn’t this private equity? Yes, but remember that our LPs are institutions that mostly invest in traditional stocks and bonds. They will allot a relatively low percentage of their entire portfolio to crazy things like venture capital. If the markets are down, the denominator has moved, increasing their exposure to VC, and they will not have an appetite for a new investment in our VC fund. 

For example, let’s say an LP had 5% exposure to venture capital before the public markets had a significant drop, like the 30% doozy in 2008. That LP would go from 1/20 (5%) in VC to 1/14 due to the denominator dropping 30% from 20 to 14, resulting in 7% exposure. That LP would not be able to invest in more VC funds until its portfolio was rebalanced. 

Different LPs will get on board for different amounts and potentially for different reasons as they balance their portfolios. Just as a startup should be careful about choosing its equity partners (see Equity vs. Debt, p. 20), VCs need to be careful about choosing their LPs. These will be long-term relationships that will necessarily go through ups and downs. 

While fundraising, VCs pitch different levels depending on the LP and its capacity. For example, a $5B pension fund may 

not be able to commit to writing a check smaller than $20-50M as a function of its size. Only so many professionals work there to track their investments. With $5B to manage, they need to write bigger checks. Meanwhile, a $200M fund-of-funds may be able to commit $5-15M. In this example, the pension fund might be our anchor investor, putting up $25M of our $125M fund, while the fund-of-funds is in for $12.5M. Each will have a commensurate stake in our fund, 20% and 10%. 

If we have done a great job selling our story and are lucky enough to have good timing, our fund may get oversubscribed, meaning we received commitments for more capital than we targeted. This is a good problem to have, but it still can be a problem. 

Remember our strategy to invest in 15 early stage startups with our three partners? If we raise a larger fund, we will either need to get more partners, invest more per startup or work harder to put the money to work. It is a good problem, and a validation of our fund’s value proposition, but it is not the awesome situation it may appear to be on the surface. 

Top quartile VC firms, those that perform in the top 25% of the industry, regularly oversubscribe. They will generally stick pretty close to their target and simply cherry pick the LPs that they would prefer to work with, LPs that can write big checks and are not too intrusive. For the rest of the industry, it can be difficult to pass up the extra cash, and many firms will increase the fund size by 10-20% and adjust their strategy accordingly. 

Let’s keep it simple and assume we reached our fundraising goal. We are now ready to start investing in startups! Ironically, to announce that we are open for business as VCs, we must first announce that our fund is closed. The terminology is confusing. The fund is now closed to any more LPs, but it is finally open for investing in startups. 

If we have yet to reach our goal of $125M but already have a very hot prospect for a startup investment, we have a couple of options. We could close the fund at a lower level, though this is not ideal, as it will mean we will need to alter our strategy (and receive lower fees, addressed below). The more common choice, if our LPs are amenable, is to announce a partial 

close at the lower that we are still to reach our level, indicating fundraising goal. This will allow us to start making investments using capital from our current LPs. 

## Capital Calls 

After we have closed or partially closed the fund, we still don’t have any cash in our bank account. Rather, we have commitments from LPs: the promise that cash will be delivered when called upon. After we have identified a startup and are ready to invest, we put out a capital call to our LPs, who are then obligated to wire their specific percentage of the investment to us. We’ll aggregate the funding from all the LPs into a single account and wire the full investment to the startup. 

Figure: Capital call 

**==> picture [228 x 158] intentionally omitted <==**

Since different LPs will have committed different amounts to the VC fund, they will be wiring us different amounts on a capital call commensurate with their percentage stake in our fund. Our $30M anchor LP, for example, upon receiving a capital call for a $2M investment, would send 20% of $2M, or $400,000. Our 10% fund-of-funds would wire $200,000. As the VC firm, we would collect and aggregate the entire round before wiring the full $2M to the startup. 

All our LPs are motivated to honor capital calls thanks to very severe penalties in our term sheets which could result in an LP losing all of their investments to date as well as any right to continue investing in our fund. The harshness of these penalties makes sense considering the importance of capital calls. The whole system falls apart if anyone can stop wiring cash. 

Our LPs are taking quite a leap of faith about when their capital will be put to work. They are also taking a leap of faith about when it will be returned. We are not planning to return any capital until after an exit, which will be several years in the coming. 

## 10-year Fund Life 

LPs are signing on for a very interesting “we don’t know when we’ll put the money in or get it back” arrangement. To contain some of this uncertainty, the standard agreement that has not changed since the industry began is to put a ten year window on the fund. LPs are thus signing up for a ten year ride, having committed to respond to every capital call for the next decade. 

For a fund to its entire must follow a go through cycle in 10 years, it somewhat predictable pattern: new investments (Series A) for the first few years only, follow-on rounds (Series B, C, etc.) for the middle years and exits for the last few years. 

Figure: 10-year fund life 

**==> picture [364 x 164] intentionally omitted <==**

Capital calls may happen anywhere along the way, for new or followon rounds. At this point, it might cross your mind, why would an LP sign up for this? Aren’t endowment fund managers looking for consistent, predictable returns? How can an LP commit to an investment when they don’t know when the cash will go in or come out, other than they expect to be out by the end of 10 years or so? Do any of these people even have these same jobs in 10 years? 

The answer is pretty clear if we break down the risk and reward potential. Let’s start on the risk side. First, the fund manager is only putting a small percentage, less than 5%, of their entire fund into this asset class. Even if the entire amount were to be lost, it would be a relatively low percentage of the entire fund. 

Second, there is almost no chance that the entire VC investment will be lost. Each VC firm has a portfolio of startups, and while each startup is quite risky, the overall portfolio has a good chance of at least breaking even. So for LPs, the downside is quite limited. 

On the other hand, the upside can be fantastic if they happen to get into the next WhatsApp, Facebook or Google. The relatively small bet on the VC sector could actually move the needle on the overall returns for the entire endowment. 

## Early Peek at Return Potential 

Let’s do some math on an of unicorn some example return, making based on was and Kleiner guesses Google. It reported that Sequoia Capital Perkins (KPCB) invested $25M for 20% of Google in 1999.[10] 

Five years later, Google IPO’d to the tune of something like $26B. After some waffling, the stock went up, further increasing returns for VCs and LPs alike. There are a bunch of unknowns because this was private equity, and the details of the deals are not public information, but we can take a stab that the returns on that original $25M were somewhere in the $410 billion range, or half that for each venture firm. 

Figure: Google as an (extreme) example of a unicorn exit 

**==> picture [348 x 174] intentionally omitted <==**

Looking at 1999, I’m guessing Sequoia was investing out of their eighth fund (Sequoia VIII, $250M) and KPCB was on either VII or VIII ($300M or $550M respectively). No matter how we slice it, both firms returned the fund many times over. $4B to Sequoia VIII would represent 16X on the fund! [11] 

On page 26, I walk through the mathematics of a hit record and assert that from a record label’s view, there are the hits, and then there is everything else. Notice in the graphic below how the Google exit would dwarf everything else in the portfolio. This includes busts, of course, but in my image below, I also include two other “return the fund” exits! 

Figure: Hits and everything else 

**==> picture [207 x 145] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [218 x 140] intentionally omitted <==**

An LP who put $10M into Sequoia VIII could have gotten $160M back on that one deal. For fun, I looked up one of Sequoia’s LPs, Duke Endowment, which was just north of $3B back in 2004. Google alone potentially contributed 5% to Duke’s Endowment that year, an incredible bump for a little gamble. 

Good news for Duke, they were also in Kleiner Perkins! (Eugene Kleiner, BTW, was one of the Traitorous Eight, p. 10.) Other LPs included the endowments from the universities of Michigan, Chicago, California, Vanderbilt, Yale, Harvard, MIT, and Georgia Tech. These LPs may have seen their entire endowments get a bump near 10% thanks to this very small bet. Careers were made. 

## Multiple Funds 

We’ve gotten ahead of ourselves with returns here. Back to the context of fundraising, with a fund limit of 10 years you might wonder how VCs remain employed for decades. Simple (conceptually), they just have to keep raising more funds! A successful venture capital firm will raise numerous venture capital funds, which usually carry the firm name with a number attached, like Sequoia I, Sequoia II, etc. 

I’m sure it is no that both KPCB were able to surprise and Sequoia raise funds a track record that to mention many given included Google, not other winners in their portfolios. Both firms continue to be bastions of the industry, with LPs clamoring over themselves to invest. Together the two firms have raised, wait for it, 80 funds! Recent ones include KPCB XVIII (2019, $600M) and Sequoia India and SE Asia (2019, $695M). 

Figure: A VC firm needs to raise a Fund II to continue making new investments 

**==> picture [372 x 199] intentionally omitted <==**

## Mega Funds 

If you noticed that the fund sizes seem, well, sizable, you’re onto something. There has been an industry trend over the decades of fund sizes growing and growing. Firms that were raising tens of millions in the ‘80s are now raising billions. This trend slowed for a while after 2008, but in recent years, gigantic funds continue to make news. Perhaps nothing has been more impactful than SoftBank launching a $100 billion Vision Fund in 2017. Numerous venerable firms have announced $1B+ funds recently, 

including NEA’s 17[th] fund at $3.3B, Bessemer’s 10[th] fund at $1.85B and General Catalyst’s 9[th] fund at $1.375B. 

It is no small feat to put over a billion dollars to work in startups. The industry takes a lot of flak for not really investing in startups anymore, waiting for angels and other investors to bring along new companies until they are big enough to handle a $10-50M cash infusion. Nonetheless, most of these behemoth firms insist they are still early stage investors, often making the argument that the large size of the fund allows them to double down on their early winners. 

## Fee Structure 

Just as other professional fund managers charge fees, so do venture capitalists. There are two sources of income for a VC firm, the management fees and the carry. The most common fee structure, similar to private equity and hedge funds, is 2/20, meaning 2% management fees and 20% carry. 

Management fees supply the baseline cash that keeps the firm in operation. The fee is assessed annually to pay for expenses such as salaries for GPs and all other employees, rental costs for office space, travel expenses and other overhead. You might think of these fees as the keep the lights on source of income. These fees will vary, but on average are in the 2% range. 

Micro funds, thus, need to be scrappy. A $25M fund, for example, would only produce $500K in annual management fees. It is easy to see why small VC firms have little support staff. There is barely enough there to pay for office space and salaries for the partners. 

Over the life of a 10-year fund, management fees can add up: 2% over 10 years is 20% total. A $100M fund would only have net $80M to invest in startups after fees. This puts extra pressure on the lowered invested capital, as there are fewer dollars being deployed and needing to provide returns. 

An industry standard goal for a successful VC fund is to triple the fund, that is, have proceeds that are 3X of the fund size. That is a lofty goal, and losing 20% of our fund to fees makes it even loftier. With only 80% to invest in startups, we actually need to return closer to 4X of available capital in order to triple the fund. 

Looking at a $100M fund, we’d only have $80M to invest. That $80M would need to return $300M, nearly 4X. 

Some firms are able to negotiate with LPs so that they can recycle fees, meaning they take the management fees out of returned capital. This allows the GPs (VCs) to invest the entire fund amount. They argue that this is good for both GPs and LPs, both realizing higher gains due to more capital being deployed. Exactly how fees are handled is nuanced and complicated…and negotiable. A highly respected veteran firm will be able to get creative with fees, whereas a new firm will need to stick with the original tried-and-true model. 

GPs may be particularly motivated to recycle fees because it can lead to higher carry.[12] In our 2/20 fee structure, the second number is one that is more important to the long-term wealth of the general partners: the carry, or carried interest. This is basically a profit-sharing arrangement that splits any 

proceeds after the firm has returned to limited partners their entire investment. Carry can vary dramatically depending on the track record of the venture firm, but 20% is a common industry benchmark. 

General partners do not receive carry until they have returned the fund. Then they begin to split the proceeds 80/20, with 80% going to the 

limited partners and 20% distributed to GPs. 

If we look at a successful triple the fund example, we might have a $100M fund returning $300M in proceeds. The first $100M goes back to LPs to return their initial investments. The next $200M gets split 80/20, with $160M going to LPs and $40M going to GPs. 

In our Google IPO example a few pages back, we speculated that proceeds to Sequoia may have been $4B into its Sequoia VII fund, which was a $250M fund. After the first $250M was distributed to LPs, the next $3.75B was split between LPs and GPs. Sequoia already had an amazing track record, and reportedly had carry in the 30% range. That would result in the partners at Sequoia receiving over $1B. I cannot find an exact number of partners, but I would estimate 15 or so, maybe 20. Even at 20, that’s an average of $50M each, though it would not be divided evenly. [13] 

Even if my assumptions are off by an order of magnitude, the point remains the same. Things can go very, very well. 

## Corporate VC 

There is another class of venture capital that has very different fundraising needs (or lack thereof). Corporate venture capital refers to the investment arms of major corporations. No LPs nor GPs. Just the mother ship and some specialized employees. Venerable corporate venture names include Google Ventures, Intel Capital, Dell Ventures, Qualcomm Ventures and Johnson and Johnson Development Corporation. 

While all corporate VC funds depend on the corporation for funding, they run a spectrum of independence when it comes to how they make investment decisions. At one end are funds that operate entirely independently, deploying capital like traditional VC funds with a singular focus: financial returns. These entities may report directly to the CFO and 

At the other end of the spectrum are strategic investors. These corporate venture funds pick startups that have some strategic value to the 

larger company. In these cases, venture capital is used as a substitute for traditional R&D in the search for disruptive innovation.[14] 

**==> picture [400 x 104] intentionally omitted <==**

Traditional VCs often refer to these corporate investors as strategics. Further, traditional VCs sometimes view these investors with skepticism because strategic investors notoriously overpay for startups. This tension is predicable since strategic investors have the potential for two types of return: financial and strategic. Traditional VCs are financial only. In the extreme, corporate venture capital can look more like a pipeline to create deal flow for a corporation’s M&A department, and indeed there are some similarities between M&A jobs and venture capital (see Sidebar about VC vs. M&A, p. 122). 

## Fundraise Summary 

VCs are GPs who get commitments from LPs seeking a high ROI (that overuse of acronyms was intended to be funny, but also demonstrates some terms you need to remember). A VC firm aspires to raise numerous VC funds, each of which has a 10-year life and will have its own portfolio of startups. LPs do not actually invest until there is a capital call, and they don’t get any money back until there is a distribution from a liquidity event. VC funds have a fee structure that is often 2/20: 2% annual manageeent fees and 20% carry, which is the GPs share of the proceeds once the fund has been returned. A typical benchmark for successful VCs is to triple the fund. Corporate VCs act much like regular VCs with a couple of caveats: they do not need to fundraise and their investment decisions are often not purely financial, as they have a strategic interest on behalf of the corporate parent. 

**==> picture [240 x 222] intentionally omitted <==**

## Case Study: VC Razor Ventures, Fund I 

Jeff was a tech whiz who had started a social networking company that raised $30M in venture funding before being acquired for $300M. After exiting his startup, Jeff dabbled in angel investing and discovered that he really enjoyed finding and mentoring promising young entrepreneurs. He felt that he had a winning formula for angel investing, but he was frustrated that he could not participate in later rounds as his successful angel investments 

## VC Math: Fund Perspective 

In the previous VC Math section, we looked at deal algebra, specifically the math involved in determining the equity split after a venture capital investment in a startup. In this section, we’ll be looking at the algebra involved when looking at an overall venture capital fund. 

Let’s start with the fund size. As we discussed, there are several variables that will go into determining the size of a VC fund. Multiply each to get the estimated fund size. 

**==> picture [458 x 92] intentionally omitted <==**

For example, a fund with 4 GPs who each plan to invest in 5 startups averaging $10M per startup would need $200M to invest, requiring a $250M fund to include management fees (2% for 10 years is 20%). 4 x 5 x $10M x 1.25 =$250M 

Like any algebraic problem, we can turn this around if we are given the fund size but lack some other variable. We could also collapse some of the variables. For example, we might be given a target portfolio size rather than the number of partners and deals each. 

Let’s say we have a fund size of $50M and want to build a portfolio of 15 startups. What would our average investment per startup be? We’ll start by pulling out the fees so that we’ll know how much we have to invest. We could divide by 1.25, which has the same effect as subtracting 20% or multiplying by 0.80. 

**==> picture [278 x 81] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [184 x 14] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>
$50M- $10M= $40M<br>**----- End of picture text -----**<br>


## With $40M to invest and a portfolio of 15 startups, we would have: $40M ÷ 15 startups = $2.67M per startup 

A firm would likely state $2-3M per startup because a precise number such as 2.67 would falsely imply specificity in a situation in which we are making broad assumptions and looking into the (hazy) future. 

It’s worth pausing to make this point about decimal places clearer. VCs are very good at math; we are financiers. We will track equity ownership in complicated capitalization tables and report returns to their LPs in very complicated spreadsheets that follow every dollar to the 10[th] decimal point if needed. 

But that is reporting, not projecting. 

When projecting, it makes no sense to introduce precision into an environment where the variables are not precise. In fact, it is quite counter - productive and potentially misleading to do so. 

because once Counter productive you introduce 2 to 3 decimal points, you can no longer do the math in your head, and VCs often need to juggle the numbers quickly. Misleading because one can place too much confidence in numbers when the numbers appear to be precise. 

Let’s restate our target investment size to be more realistic: $40M ÷ 15 startups = $2-3M per startup 

Now let’s look at what could happen when we recycle fees. Recall that recycling fees means that we’ll have the full fund size to invest, in this case $50M. There are two ways we could invest this extra $10M: either by increasing the size of investment in each startup or by increasing the number of startups in the portfolio. Let’s start by increasing the size of investment per startup. We simple divide the total funding by the number of startups: 

**==> picture [345 x 15] intentionally omitted <==**

That’s nearly $1M more per startup (specifically, 2/3 of a million). Note that this is a somewhat significant change in strategy, from $2-3 to $34M average per startups. We can’t just hand founders an extra $1M. To deploy that extra capital, we would likely need to invest in 

startups that are slightly farther along and have higher capital needs, i.e., slightly later stage. 

Now let’s look at the same situation but assume we want to keep the strategy of $2-3M per startup. This would require that we increase the number of startups in our portfolio. 

**==> picture [408 x 33] intentionally omitted <==**

There are two simple ways to approach it. I often like to do both in my head as a double check. First, we can see that 50 is 25% more than 40, and we know that we invested in 15 startups at $40M. Increase 15 by 25% and we end up at 19 if we round (you cannot invest in 18.75 startups). 

**==> picture [334 x 14] intentionally omitted <==**

The second approach is to divide $50M by a number between $2-3M, say, $2.5M. That would result in a portfolio of 20. It is common for VC 

firms to communicate a range in their strategies, as these are all targets. This firm might say: 

## Fund Strategy: $50M fund into 18-20 startups at $2-3M per startup. 

Stop for a moment and think about the strategic implications of this scenario. With our earlier approach, we increased the amount of funding per startup, changing our investment strategy on a startup by startup basis, requiring us to deploy an extra $1M in each startup. 

In the later scenario, we are keeping the $2-3M average investment and increasing the number of startups. Something else must change. Looking at the variables that go into determining the size of the fund, what other variable(s) will have to change if the size of the portfolio increases? 

**==> picture [458 x 91] intentionally omitted <==**

The size of the portfolio is a function of the number of partners and the number of deals each will do. If the portfolio increases, we’ll either have to work harder (each partner do one extra deal) or add a partner (and split the pie one more way). Either way, the change is rather significant. Again, thinking like a VC is always connecting these dots. 

In summary, if we recycle fees, we must account for the strategic shift in one of three ways: 

1) Increase the average investment per startup; 

2) Increase the number of deals per partner; and/or 

- 3) Increase the number of partners. 

Now let’s look at what might motivate us to make these changes by recycling fees. Recall that a traditional goal of a top performing VC firm is to triple the fund. In our $50M example, we’ll need $150M to achieve that target. That is a 3X increase, from 50 to 150. 

However, if fees are not recycled, we will only have $40M to invest toward the same goal of achieving $150M in proceeds. That would require a return of 3.75X on our $40M deployed. 

**==> picture [357 x 15] intentionally omitted <==**

Let’s say we are very talented and can achieve 3.75X. With $40M, we can triple the fund. Imagine what we could do with $50M! We don’t have to imagine; let’s just do the quick math: 

**==> picture [335 x 15] intentionally omitted <==**

Pop quiz (and warning that we are heading down a rabbit hole that you don’t really need to go down unless you find this interesting): how much more carry do we (the GPs) receive in this scenario as compared to not recycling the fees? 

To answer that, let’s start with the baseline situation in which we triple the fund with regular fees. To figure out the carry, we need to first return the fund to LPs: 

**==> picture [251 x 32] intentionally omitted <==**

With $100M to split 80/20 with LPs, we will end up with $20M: 

**==> picture [246 x 15] intentionally omitted <==**

Now let’s look at recycling where we again achieve 3.75X returns, resulting in $187.5M in proceeds. First, we must take out our fees from the overall proceeds (hence the recycling): 

**==> picture [247 x 32] intentionally omitted <==**

Then we must return the fund to LPs: 

**==> picture [290 x 32] intentionally omitted <==**

Then we split the proceeds, keeping our 20% carry: 

**==> picture [323 x 15] intentionally omitted <==**

Finally, we can see the difference, an extra $5.5M in carry! 

**==> picture [254 x 15] intentionally omitted <==**

Bottom line here is that GPs can earn an additional $5.5M of carry by recycling fees on this $50M fund. That is a significant increase in carry, over 25%! The math holds up if you add a zero: GPs of a $500M fund can earn an additional $55M if we recycle fees. 

That looks great for GPs, but how well to LPs fare in this situation? Again, rabbit hole warning, you are free to skip this! 

Let’s again start with regular fees. With a total of $150M in proceeds, the GPs took $20M, leaving $130M for the LPs. It breaks down like this: start by returning the fund of $50M. Then split the remaining $100M 80/20, with $80M to LPs. 

**==> picture [223 x 13] intentionally omitted <==**

When fees are recycled, the LPs still get $50M off the top. Then we split the remaining $127.5M, and LPs receive 80%, or $102M, resulting in a total of 50+102= $152M. 

**==> picture [260 x 13] intentionally omitted <==**

That’s an increase of $22M over $130M, or about 17%. Not too shabby! No LP is going to be unhappy about an extra $22M! However, percentagewise, the GPs clearly benefit more. 

We have ignored an element that would affect the benefit to LPs. Can you think of what it is? 

The answer is time. If fees are quick recycled, that means they will come out of proceeds. Where would the proceeds be going otherwise? To the LPs. If we are recycling fees, we will necessarily be taking some of our early proceeds that would have gone to LPs and reinvesting them. 

To accurately assess the benefit to LPs of recycling fees, we’d need to tackle IRR (internal rate of return) and the cost of capital. The LPs will benefit from recycling fees, but it is a diminishing benefit due to lower early distributions in favor of larger later ones. The longer the time in between, the lower the benefit to LPs. The math would get more complicated than we will address in this book. Suffice it to say, recycling has a more clear-cut advantage for the GPs. 

not raise a ask? time is a Why just larger fund, you might Okay, this super rabit hole warning! You are strongly encouraged to skip this part, though it’ll only take a sec. 

To raise a fund in which we’d have $50M to invest, given a regular 2% fee structure over 10 years, or 20% total in fees, we’d need: 

**==> picture [200 x 13] intentionally omitted <==**

Doublechecking our math, in this scenario, GPs would collect $12.5M in management fees: 

**==> picture [234 x 13] intentionally omitted <==**

Tripling a $62.5M fund will result in proceeds of $187.5M. To determine our carry, let’s first return the fund to LPs: 

**==> picture [270 x 32] intentionally omitted <==**

Then we split the proceeds so that we receive 20%: 

**==> picture [242 x 15] intentionally omitted <==**

This $25M is quite close to the $27.5M in carry in our earlier scenario in which ew recycled fees for a $50M fund. Then, if we add in the additional management fees, we find that in both scenarios, the GPs will receive $37.5M. 

|$50M Fund with Recycled<br>Fees<br>Amount Invested:<br>$50<br>Total Proceeds:<br>$187.5<br>Management Fees:<br>$10<br>Carried Interest:<br>$27.5<br>Total Fees:<br>$37.5|$62.5M Fund with 2% Fees|
