This is practically the same thing, with a somewhat nominal difference being taxation. Management fees are taxed as income while carried interest carry, or , as it is often called when talking about taxes, is taxed as a capital gain. While that can be the difference of being taxed at a 37% rate for income versus 20% for long-term capital gains, it is not enough of a difference to drive a VC fund strategy. 

If it is basically the same thing, why does the distinction exist? The quick answer is nuanced and has to do with the alignment of LP and GP incentives. I think it is finally time for us to bail on this particular rabbit hole. For readers who want to dig further, see the footnote.[15] 

## VC Math Problems 

1. How large should a VC fund be if there are 5 partners each investing an average of $10M into 5 startups, ignoring fees. 

2. Same question, add a 2% management fee for the entire life of the fund. 

3. Back to question #1, now add a variable management fee: 2% for the first 5 years and 1% for the second 5 years. 

4. If you have a $200M fund, and you take out 2% a year for a 5-year investment period, then 1% a year for the last 5 years of the fund, how much do you have left to invest in startups? 

5. Same question, if you recycle fees, how much do you have to invest? 

6. If you wanted to end up with $200M to invest and you were planning to charge 2% fees, what size fund would you raise? 

7. If your VC firm tripled the fund in returns for a $250M fund, how much carry would the GPs get if you had a typical 2/20 fee structure? 

8. Given this scenario: 6 GPs each investing in 10 startups each for an average total investment per startup of $10M, recycled fees, 20% carry, tripled the fund. What carry would each GP expect? 

9. Same situation, don’t recycle the fees. 

10. Same as question 8, how much does the firm take in management fees over 10 years? If half goes to equal salaries to each GP, what is does a GP make per year? 

**==> picture [313 x 177] intentionally omitted <==**

## 2. Invest 

In the last chapter, we looked at the fundraising process for VC firms, whose GPs (general partners) go on the road much as startups do to pitch to investors, who will become the fund’s LPs (limited partners). Most of the capital raised by VC firms comes from institutions. Hence the definition of venture capital, institutional equity investing in high growth startups. 

Once we have secured enough commitments from LPs to reach our fundraising goal, we may close the fund, meaning that it is closed to new investors and we are ready to invest in startups. But how do we find them? 

## Deal Flow 

It is time to go unicorn hunting, a process lovingly referred to as sourcing deals. In this capacity, VCs remind me of the old-fashioned A&R reps of the big music labels. Arts and repertoire, A&R, guys (and like VCs, they were mostly men) were the talent scouts. It was their job to scour the local and regional music scenes to find the next hot band, sign them up and develop them into stars. 

VCs similarly must work our channels to find the next great startup. This is a surprise to many entrepreneurs, who fantasize that VCs are kicking back in La-Z-Boy chairs, smoking cigars and dolling out cash like the Godfather or the sharks on ABC’s Shark Tank. 

Most VCs acknowledge that venture capital is a service business. We don’t just provide capital. We also provide advice and connections and often 

play an active role in a startup’s pursuit of a unicorn exit, as we’ll discuss in the next chapter about growth. VC firms have reputations for adding value beyond the checks that we write. Our ability to attract the best startups is based on our reputations. 

As any talent scout will tell you, good acts don’t just fall in your lap. In fact, the ones that are falling into your lap in the form of unsolicited submissions are usually a waste of time. Just as A&R reps begrudge receiving hundreds of unsolicited demo tapes from wannabe bands, VCs similarly find little value in anything coming over the transom. [16] VC firms receive hundreds of random pitch decks. Bigger firms may have interns or young analysts take a quick look at these, but for the most part, time spent going through these submissions is not well spent. 

Figure: My band’s demo tape, circa early ‘90s 

VCs maintain extensive networks to create deal flow. Startup referrals can come from any of the various constituencies VCs regularly work with: serial entrepreneurs, industry experts, limited partners, startup attorneys, portfolio company employees, M&A professionals, investment bankers, angel investors and, of course, other VCs. 

**==> picture [175 x 172] intentionally omitted <==**

VCs spend a lot of time at industry events to keep these networks active. National conferences, venture show - cases and even startup competi tions, all of which are opportunities for VCs to nurture relationships with experts who might eventually be helpful for sourcing deals, performing due diligence, finding a strategic partner for a portfolio company or even exploring exit opportunities. 

## Figure: VC’s professional network 

If you ever see a VC as a guest speaker, on a panel, judging a competi tion or teaching a class, note that that they have incentives to take part in these activities beyond giving back to their alma maters and communities. VCs need to build their reputations and expand their networks. These events help VCs brand themselves as trustworthy and accessible members of the startup ecosystem. 

## Syndicate Partners 

Just like the music business, in which record labels could find themselves in a bidding war over the hottest bands, VC firms often must compete to get in the deal for the hottest startups. However, unlike the music business, VC firms often come together in a deal by syndicating, meaning having multiple VC firms participate in an investment round. 

Somewhat counter-intuitively, syndication has become a ubiquitous industry practice. It would seem like a tricky decision for a VC to bring in another VC firm on a deal. If our firm found a great startup and thought we could make a lot of money, why would we want to share that upside? 

It turns out that the benefits of syndication outweigh the downside of shared success. Perhaps the most important benefit: to get to unicorn status, the startup will ultimately need to raise more than we can likely supply ourselves, which means we’ll eventually need more investors anyway. If that is the case, it is a good idea to bring them in early so that they are part of the entire process with incentives aligned with our own.[17] 

In addition to the likelihood that we’ll need the extra cash, we could use extra help turning the startup into a unicorn. The more VC firms we have behind this startup, the greater our chances of success, one could argue. Each firm has a network of portfolio companies, serial entrepreneurs, potential acquirers and a whole range of industry experts who could be helpful. At the very least, we’ll have more people pulling for us and fewer rooting against us. 

There is another crucial benefit to bringing in a syndicate: confirmation that we aren’t crazy. Hunting unicorns is tricky business, and like many creative endeavors, there is a very blurry line between genius and crazy. If we can find another VC firm willing to join us in an investment, we have solid validation that we are onto something good. We also have a CYA 

**==> picture [150 x 79] intentionally omitted <==**

(cover-your-ass) plan to take to our LPs in case the 

whole thing blows up. (A VC once told me, “You’ll never get fired for syndicating with Sequoia.”) 

While you might deduce that VCs would want to invest alone, it turns out that they almost always syndicate at some point before a startup exits. Exactly when to bring in syndicate partners can be a source of internal conflict for a VC, whose duty is to maximize returns for LPs. One common tactic is to take an early, smaller round in its entirety with the plan to begin courting syndicates for the following, bigger rounds. 

Firms often invest different amounts in the round and may have different levels of involvement. Generally, one firm leads the deal, meaning that firm does the bulk of the due diligence and perhaps has the largest 

Often the lead investor is the firm that discovered the startup, but it is not a hard rule. It is also common for the firm that makes the largest investment to be the lead, but again, this is not set in stone. Lastly, the most prestigious firm in the syndicate will often take the lead. 

For other VC firms to be a valid source of deal flow, we must become the of VCs that others want to work with. This theme will type reappear numerous times in this book. This is a people industry built on reputations. To get into the best deals, we need a great reputation. 

## Accelerators 

Another very important development in VC deal sourcing over the last decade has been the emergence of the startup accelerator. In 2005, Y- Combinator launched the first class of what would become known as an accelerator by combining seed stage investing with a package of services intended to help early stage startups accelerate their development and growth.[18] TechStars followed a couple of years later, and today there are hundreds of accelerators, over 450 in 2015 according to Entrepreneur magazine, with Y-Combinator and Techstars remaining the leaders in the 

The accelerator model has evolved, but at its core includes a seed investment, ranging from $25,000-150,000, in exchange for a small amount of equity, usually 6-8%. Startups usually join in cohorts and spend 10-12 weeks together in an open working space. Mentors are brought in regularly to help each startup team. The theory is that the program will accelerate the development of these early stage companies. The programs usually end with a demo day attended by angel investors and VCs. 

Startups coming out of accelerators are usually still a bit too early for a multi-million-dollar Series A raise, likely needing under $1M to hit the next milestones. Though the startups are early, VCs will often attend demo days to network and start early relationships to be in a good position for a Series A should one materialize. Many accelerators have follow-on seed investment funds to further support strong graduates. 

## Due Diligence 

Once we have found a startup that we are excited about, we begin the due diligence process in which we attempt to uncover everything we can to make sure this investment will be a good one. We’ll want to see all of the financials of the startup; talk with key customers to gauge how well the startup is meeting their needs; reach out to our own industry experts, sometimes for a fee, to get their opinions about the startups offerings; and even run background checks on the founders. 

The second half of this book will go into detail about due diligence, utilizing a framework we’ll call VC Razor. The process can easily take months but is often compressed into weeks and, in the extreme, days when there is a hot deal in a frothy market (hot meaning a startup many firms are interested in; frothy describing a time when many deals are happening). During the dot com bubble, there were stories of deals closing in days, hardly any time to do minimal due diligence. 

From a startup’s perspective, shorter due diligence is preferred so that the CEO and the company can get back to focusing on growth rather than fundraising. Startups have limited resources, and any hour spent satisfying 

**==> picture [145 x 94] intentionally omitted <==**

due diligence requirements is an hour not spent on 

creating value. Hence, some VC firms can differentiate with speed. “We won’t drag you through a lengthy process,” they may be able to promise. 

This is a difficult promise for a VC to make. Due diligence is complicated and time consuming. We need to be assured as much as possible that a startup can deliver on its potential despite the odds stacked it. One VC shared in I 6 up against my class, “If had my way, we’d have months, but that’s just not realistic.” 

Investment Decision 

Most startups have a lead investor, a champion, if you will, at a VC firm, usually someone at the partner level. This VC will be responsible for getting the entire VC firm on board. This is again reminiscent of the old music business days when A&R reps would bring talent to the label and act as their champion. A band could get lost in limbo at a label if their A&R rep changed record companies after they had signed a record contract, with nobody at the label to champion their cause. 

Similarly, a startup will have a champion at a firm to guide it through the firm’s specific due diligence process, including meetings with other partners at the firm to get their support. 

Most VC firms have standing partner meetings on Monday mornings. This just happens to fit the job cycle of VCs, who spend much of the week with various activities associated with the job duties described in this book: fundraising, investing, growing and exiting. These activities include a lot of travel. Mondays tend to be the one day that all partners commit to being in town to meet. 

Different firms will have different investment decision processes. Whichever partner is leading the investment will keep other partner up to date on the due diligence progress, and ultimately there will usually be a partner vote. Some firms require a unanimous decision to invest, others might only need a majority. Many have caveats that any partner can veto a deal, or perhaps any partner can take a flyer on a deal even when other 

partners don’t agree. Firms tend to be small and driven by personalities. No two will behave exactly the same. 

## Term Sheet 

Once a firm has decided to invest, we will put down a term sheet. This is a contract that lays out the general terms of the deal we are offering the startup, with the caveat that there will be more due diligence and more specifics to be worked out before the final deal is inked. 

The venture capital term sheet is akin to the home-buying contract. When a real estate sign says “under contract,” it means that the seller and buyer have signed a contract that spells out their intentions to have a transaction, though there are still some details to be worked out. The actual sale of the home has not been executed. That happens on closing day. 

Similarly, a term sheet is a contract that is a part of the process, not the end of the process. The terms in the term sheet will be negotiated between investors and startups, and the signed document will serve as a road map for the process to follow. 

Term sheets are loaded with, wait for it, terms, many of which have far reaching implications. For a comprehensive run-down of deal terms, I highly recommend Venture Deals, Be Smarter than Your Lawyer and Venture Capitalist, by Brad Feld and Jason Mendelson of the Foundry Group. It is a very practical approach for founders to learn more about term sheets before negotiating with VCs. 

The National Venture Capital Association (NVCA) also has a number of great resources on their website, including a model term sheet.[19] 

The two most important terms on a term sheet are the investment size and the valuation, which state the amount of capital being raised and the price of the equity. As noted in an earlier, these two variables determine the percentage of the company that will be owned by the new equity partners. 

Investment Size and Valuation 

If you’ve ever seen the show Shark Tank, you’ve seen offerings like these: 

## X% of the company being sold for $Y 

## or 

For $Y we are offering X% of our company. 

This is a layman’s approach to an equity deal, and it is conceptually accurate. But in practical terms, it is misleading. In a venture capital deal, the founders do not sell some of their own shares to the new investors. Rather, new shares are created and sold by the startup, resulting in more overall shares as well as a cash infusion into the company. 

Figure: New preferred shares created for VCs 

**==> picture [418 x 109] intentionally omitted <==**

The founders will own a smaller percentage of a larger pie as a result of the pool being diluted by the new shares. The founders’ 100% ownership pre-investment will be diluted by the creation of new shares, not by selling off some of their pre-existing shares. 

Dilution is understandably a sensitive subject. Many entrepre neurs struggle with losing too much of their baby. Some, for example, want to make sure to retain at least 51% ownership to retain control. While this makes a lot of sense in a traditional business, in the super-fast growth atmosphere of venture capital, it can be self-defeating for founders. 

VCs will argue that our capital will help build a much bigger company much more quickly than traditional growth would facilitate. Founders who retain 40% of a startup that gets to be a $500M company, for example, will have much more wealth than founders who own 100% of a 

$50M enterprise. VCs will say, it’s better to have a slice of watermelon than a whole grape. 

Both sides make valid points, and there is no right answer that will fit all situations. Ultimately, “the market” decides. The market is that of capital and startups. If there are many well-funded VC firms chasing a deal, the price will go up. If there are too many startups chasing too little capital, prices go down. 

Whatever someone is willing to pay for equity is what will set the price. Startups are highly illiquid and behave as other illiquid assets, such as houses. We can’t tell what the price should be until someone actually pays a price. Until that moment, we can only estimate the price based on what others have paid for other houses. But no other house/startup is exactly the same. 

Let’s review the basic algebra that gets us from our two fundamental terms in the term sheet (investment size and valuation) to the amount of dilution a founder will suffer. First, we need to determine the post-money valuation: 

Figure: Pre-money + Investment = Post-Money 

**==> picture [244 x 65] intentionally omitted <==**

|What we agree the||The cash the||What the|
|---|---|---|---|---|
|startup is now||startup will||startup will be|
|worth||soon have in||worth directly|
|||its||after|
|||bank account||the investment|



To reiterate, VCs don’t offer deals like Sharks do, offering to buy X% for $Y. We are not buying a percentage of the pre-existing startup; we are expanding the company’s value by a specific amount (the investment), for 

which we will receive a specific number of preferred shares of new stock, thereby owning a percentage of the new, bigger entity. 

For example, a VC would not offer $1M for half of the company. Rather, we would offer a $1M investment on a $1M pre-money valuation. The investment goes into the bank account of the startup to be used to create more value (by hitting milestones). The investors do end up owning half of the company, which is worth twice as much because it has $1M in the bank. 

Figure: “1 on 1” deal, $2M post 

**==> picture [458 x 116] intentionally omitted <==**

It is important to note that while the founders have been diluted by 50% in this case, the value of their shares has not changed. Prior to the deal, founders owned 100% of a $1M enterprise. After the deal, they owned 50% of a $2M enterprise, one that has $1M in cash. The good news for both founders and VCs is that the $1M in cash that is in the bank will get converted into growth. If the founders can hit milestones with that capital, the equity of the startup will increase much more quickly than the cash will be spent. 

In the above “1 on 1” example, it is easy to see the equity split is 50/50. The algebra is quite simple so that we can determine the percentage ownership of the VCs, and hence the dilution to the founders, of any deal. 

**==> picture [193 x 80] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [198 x 75] intentionally omitted <==**

Thus, a $2M investment on a $4M pre-money would yield a $6M in 2/6 or 33% to the amount now post-money, resulting dilution, equal owned by the new investors. Similarly, a 4 on 10 pre would yield a $14M post, resulting in 4/14 or 28.5% dilution. There is a cheat sheet on p. 34 for various deal scenarios and the resulting dilution. 

I want to stress a point made a few paragraphs ago: while the founders’ percent age of ownership in the startup has been diluted, the overall value of the founders’ stock has not changed during this transaction. In the 2 on 4 deal above, founders retained 67% of the equity of the newly valued $6M startup (that has $2M in cash in the bank). The premoney of $4M is identical to 67% x $6M. 

Legal Entity 

Another way of saying this is that the share price has not changed, and founders will retain the number of shares they had prior to the investment, which brings us to another small but important point. To facilitate the issuance of shares of stock, the startup must be incorporated. 

There are several legal entities that founders may choose when getting started: sole proprietorships, partnerships (general or limited liability) and corporations (C-Corps and S-Corps). Each has its benefits. Sole proprietorships and general partnerships are extremely easy and cheap to set up. Limited liability partnerships (LLCs and LLPs) take more coordination and fees. In return, partners have some protections, limits to their personal liability should something go awry. 

Both proprietorships and partnerships have pass-through tax implications, meaning taxes are passed along to the owners who pay regular income tax on any earnings. This is very unappealing to VCs for two reasons. First, we don’t want to track and pay income tax for every penny earned by every startup in our portfolio. Secondly, and probably more importantly, we don’t want to pay regular income tax at all when we have another option. Capital gains tax on our carried interest is currently only 20% rather than 32% for regular income, and not assessed until we when and if we liquidate our investment. 

For tax reasons alone, VCs would likely require startups to incorporate. But there is another key legal incentive: C-Corps are only entity that is allowed to create preferred shares of stock. VCs require the special provisions enabled by preferred shares. Hence, a startup must be incorporated into a C-Corp prior to receiving venture capital. 

## Investment Size 

As discussed, the two most fundamental terms in the term sheet are the investment size and the pre-money valuation, often stated as an $X investment on a Y pre-money. But where do these numbers come from? Traditionally, it is the VCs who put down a term sheet. Does that mean the VCs choose these numbers? 

The answer is that both numbers are negotiated and are influenced by a variety of factors, some driven by the startup and its needs, and some by the VCs and our needs. 

Let’s start with the investment size, as it is the startup’s need for capital that has initiated this whole process. Earlier, we discussed that VC firms begin fundraising by developing a fund strategy, including the fund size. Then we go on a roadshow, pitching our fundraising goal. “We’re raising a $250M fund to invest in early stage startups, leveraging our expertise in…” 

The same is true with startups. Founders put together a growth strategy for their startup to include the specific amount of funding being raised to facilitate that growth. Founders have two countervailing influences: 1) the need for cash to grow; and 2) the desire to avoid dilution. 

These two influences motivate founders to raise only the specific amount needed to hit some value-creating milestones. Founders should want to hit milestones as quickly as possible because doing 

so will raise the valuation of the startup. The higher the valuation, the less the dilution. So founders should only raise enough (with some cushion) to hit some milestones. 

Of course, everyone knows that entrepreneurs have numerous backup plans. In addition to the strategy being pitched, founders should have plans for different levels of funding, including a backup plan for bootstrapping in case fundraising is a flop. But the pitch to VCs needs to be the optimistic, go big or go home strategy that VCs need to hear: a moonshot strategy that could return the fund for whoever is receiving the pitch. 

Here’s what not to pitch: With this investment, we will be able to reach profitability this year and never need any more funding. 

Red flag! This would demonstrate that you don’t have a clue. This is a bank pitch. They’d love for you to hit profitability right away and pay them back. They don’t care if you go to the moon because they will see none of that upside. 

But VCs want a moonshot. Unlike banks, VCs never care about don’t care about near-term profitability. In fact, we may profitability in your startup. We care about value creation. 

Isn’t that the same thing, you ask? Not necessarily, particularly in early stage tech companies. VCs need the highest valuation at exit regardless of the current profitability of the company. Sometimes these are related, but more often in early or growth stage they are not. The valuation is based on future earnings potential, or perhaps on the even more abstract concept of strategic value to an acquirer, not on current profitability. 

An extreme example is Amazon.com, which went public in 1997. For well over a decade, Amazon hardly reported any profit, having poured all incoming cash back into growth. However, Amazon’s revenue consistently and impressively continued to rise, resulting in a market cap that is nearing $1 trillion in 2018. True, Amazon could have been profitable earlier had Bezos chosen to slow down its growth. Clearly, Bezos chose growth over profitability in the larger pursuit of creating maximum shareholder value. 

Another example is Facebook’s acquisition of Instagram for $1B. There is no way to justify that purchase price as a function of Instagram’s revenue or at the time of both profitability acquisition, which were reportedly zero. Rather, Instagram represented some much larger strategic value to Facebook. 

For VCs, growth is our goal over profitability, which why we might walk away from founders pitching that they’ll hit break-even this year. We don’t want to break even; we want supercharged growth! Growth is expensive. Profitability takes a hit when your goal is growth. Rather than tying a VC investment to profitability, we need to identify the milestones we’ll have to hit on our to way hockey stick growth, the subject of the next chapter. 

Investment Size: VC Perspective 

We just spent a lot of time looking at investment size as a function of the startup’s strategy. Here’s a shocker: from the VC’s perspective, the startup’s strategy is not necessarily the main factor in our determination of the ideal investment size in a startup. It is but one of several variables. Another key variable is the amount of capital we need to put to work so that we can provide a return to our LPs. 

In the fundraising chapter, we talked about a VC firm’s strategy when raising a fund, specifically that it included an average investment for each startup in the portfolio. A $250M fund, for example, might be aiming at 1520 startups at $12-15M each. Hence, before we’ve even met any startups in which we might invest, we’ve already chosen an approximate amount we’ll invest in each. 

If we told LPs that we were investing an average of $15M per startup, we’ll have some explaining to do if we are now looking at a $500,000 investment. Further, with $250M under management, 

a $500,000 investment has very little chance of returning the fund. A 20X return on $500k would only yield $10M, only 4% of our $250M fund. 

We can certainly make some exceptions when we find exceptional startups. Our LPs trust that we will be opportunistic, but within reason. Out of the gate, our fund norms are going to be very influential as we look at new investments. We’ll want new investments to fit our fund profile. 

## Investment Size Summary 

There are numerous variables and potential conflicts associated with determining the investment size in a round of venture capital. It is not as simple as a founder pitching a specific funding need and an investor deciding whether to fill that need. Founders are balancing the need for capital to grow with the desire to limit dilution. VCs must make sure the investment fits our fund profile while also attempting to maximize returns on each investment, meanwhile ensuring that founders remain motivated to achieve a unicorn exit. 

## Valuations 

In addition to the investment size, a second and equally important term on the term sheet is the price of the equity, determined by the premoney valuation. So far, we’ve been discussing some of the considerations when negotiating the investment size. It is a very important meeting of the minds. This capital will fuel growth, and founders and investors will work together, on the same side of the table, to create value. 

However, before we are on the same side of the table, we must reach a deal, and to do so, we must agree on a price, the pre-money valuation. 

VCs like to say that coming up with a valuation is more art than science. True, there is no algebraic formula to determine valuation. But the process VCs use to determine the price looks pretty scientific to me, as defined by a “systemic knowledge of the physical or material world gained through observations and experimentation.”[20] 

I would argue that VC valuation is more science than math. There is no formula. We can’t rely solely on spreadsheet calculations to determine a valuation (much to the chagrin of many a business school student). However, there is a body of observable evidence as well as a process of experimentation that we will be our guide. 

In his book Zero to One, Peter Thiel defines valuation as the current value of the summation of all the money a company will make in the future. This is probably the least flawed conceptual framework in which to think about valuation, but it is still very challenging given all the unknowns. 

Thiel is employing a Finance 101 concept called present value, which includes a formula for which we can calculate the value today of all the value that will be created in the future. At the core of present value is the discounted cash flow, or DCF. The theory is that tomorrow’s cash is worth less than it would be if you already had it today. Hence, tomorrow’s cash needs to be discounted to give it a value today. A more practical way to say it: 

## ONE bird in the hand is worth TWO in the bush 

The two future birds in the bush have been discounted by one-half to give the present value of one bird in the hand. That’s a pretty steep discount, 50%. What determines the level of discount? Risk. For the birds in the bush, there is a high risk that you will fail to catch both. 

Discounted Cash Flow = Future Cash Flow x Discount Rate One Bird in Hand = Two Birds in the Bush x 50% 

Similarly, there is high risk that a startup will not reach the millions of dollars in revenues projected by its founders. Hence, those forecasts can be discounted to give us a present value. Here’s the rub, the amount by which we discount the future revenues is not determined by a formula. It’s a narrative. It’s a story about a number. Like the one-half we used to discount 

two birds to one bird because of the high risk associated with catching two birds, we will similarly pick a number, the discount rate, which we will find difficult to explain. 

In finance class, we are given specific future cash flows and can mathematically calculate the present value. However, in the startup world, we have much fuzzier concepts for value. We may never even have positive cash flows! Still, conceptually, we have a similar approach in which the present value is a derivative of a future valuation. 

Work Backwards from the Exit 

VC’s start with the same discounted future value framework but have a more practical bent. Future value for VCs is the exit. We ask, what do we think we can sell this company for in 5 years? Then we work backwards from that future value to figure out a present value. 

The exit valuation is the proxy for Thiel’s summation of future earnings. That will be the number from which we can reverse engineer through several rounds of investments back to today’s round. It is this reverse algebra that will guide us toward a pre-money valuation in a term sheet. 

From the VC’s perspective, the current valuation cannot be considered without thinking through the future. It is intrinsically tied to the next rounds of financing and the exit. We will come back to the science of valuations once we have had a chance to investigate rest of the VC Job Cycle. 

## Convertible Notes 

As we see above, coming up with a valuation (also called “pricing the deal”) is a sticky proposition, and it gets stickier the earlier in the life of the startup, at the seed stage. There is tremendous pressure on getting the price right. Price it too low, and we may disincentivize the founding team by diluting them too much. Price it too high, and there is the danger of a down 

round, in which the next round has a devaluation (the repercussions of which are explained in the next chapter). 

One to avoid a valuation is way negotiating seed stage by issuing a convertible note. This is basically a loan that functions a lot like an equity investment. Officially, it is debt. The startup is borrowing money from investors. There is an interest rate and a maturity date, just like any loan. 

However, the intention of the loan is different. It is not intended to get paid back in cash like a regular loan. Rather, the hope is that the startup will use the cash to hit milestones so that it can raise a Series A, its first institutional funding. When that happens, the loan will convert to equity, essentially being repaid in the form of equity. 

The convertible note allows us to invest today without pricing the round, thus avoiding a potentially messy negotiation about valuation. By the time the next equity round is needed, the startup should have hit milestones that help define a mutually agreeable range of valuations. 

On the other hand, if the startup does not make it to another round of financing, there is often nothing left with which to repay the loan. This results in a loan that acts a lot like equity, sharing in the upside (by converting to equity) or going nearly to zero on the downside. 

For the extra risk of investing early, issuers of convertible notes receive a discount on their equity once it gets priced, commonly 20%. For example, if the Series A pre-money valuation is $5M, the convertible note will convert at a price of $4M, resulting in bonus shares for the note holders. 

VCs sometimes see convertible debt as the lesser of two evils: better to deal with a convertible note as we negotiate a Series A than to deal with the fallout of a mispriced seed round. As VCs, we wouldn’t want to come in on a deal that had been priced too low by the previous investors because we’d run into the serious risk of over-diluting the 

in the seed founders. They already gave up more than they should have round. 

Conversely, if the seed round had been overpriced, we don’t want to have to impose a down round because it would create turbulence between founders, early investors and current investors. We need everyone on the same team! 

A convertible note could be a nice way to avoid bad pricing scenarios. However, over the last few years, valuation caps have come into fashion with convertible debt can act as de facto valuations. A valuation cap puts a limit on the price at which the convertible note will convert in the next round. This is to protect the debt issuers from dilution. 

For example, let’s look at a case with no discount and a $500k convertible note. If the startup goes on to raise a $1.5M Series A investment on a $3M pre-money valuation, the total post-money would be $5M. The holders of the note would have .5/5 or 10% ownership. 

In that same case, if the Series A were a $5M raise on a $10M pre-money, the resulting post-money would be $15.5M and the note would convert into about 3% ownership. The higher the valuation on the Series A, the lower the percent ownership of the issuers of the convertible note. Hence the incentive to impose a cap to limit dilution. 

There are some further complications around convertible notes, such as whether the note converts pre-money (diluting only the founders) or postmoney (diluting everyone, as in my example above). Also, interest must be charged for the note to be legally considered a loan. This can be anywhere from 2-8% annually. 

Perhaps the most significant complication is the necessary inclusion of a maturity date, that is, the date that the loan becomes due, usually set at 18-24 months. After that period, the issuers of the note could call for repayment and disrupt the operations of the startup, effectively bankrupting it. 

This would only happen if the startup were failing in the eyes of the debt holders. However, the chances of recouping the loan plus interest are minimal at best. Recall, the startup received this cash two years ago with the goal of spending it in hopes of hitting milestones to attract VCs. If the startup has not succeeded, it likely has run out of cash, meaning it cannot repay. 

Still, this provision gives the convertible note investors some assurance that they may have recourse, and it scares some founders into worrying they may be at the mercy of debt collectors. To address these risks and others, Y-Combinator came up with a replacement investment vehicle in 2013 called a SAFE, a “Simple Agreement for Future Equity.” 

A SAFE basically does the same thing as convertible debt minus the interest and maturity date, two things that favored the investor over the founders. Y-Combinator’s SAFE was soon followed by 500 Startups’ KISS, “Keep It Simple Securities,” which re-introduced interest and maturity dates in slightly different new way. 

Both KISS and SAFEs are attempts to simplify and speed up the seed investment process. From what I can tell, they’ve received mixed reviews, depending on the region. They seem to be most popular on the west coast, where speed is most important and founder friendly terms most common. In other regions, more conservative investors seem to prefer convertible debt. 

Convertible debt is generally used in one of two scenarios. Both are situations in which we would prefer not to price the round. First is seed we’ve been This is investors stage, as discussing. space dominated by angel lately. Traditional VCs took interest in convertible notes after the 2008 financial crisis. However, most ultimately concluded it did not fit their model, and most VCs today seem to prefer to go ahead and price rounds when investing. 

The second scenario where convertible debt is common occurs when a startup that has already raised a round of institutional funding (e.g., Series A) is running out of cash but has not hit the milestones needed to raise the next round (e.g., Series B). In this case, 

one of the startup’s Series A investors may provide a convertible note, called a bridge loan, intended to help the startup get to the other side and hit the milestones to have a successful next round of is necessary financing. This preferable to the alternative: a down round. 

## Option Pools 

Back to a Series A term sheet, there is one additional term that can have a major impact on how equity is split in a venture capital round. An option pool is a portion of a startup’s equity that is set aside to be granted to key employees as a recruitment and motivational tool, often in the 10-20% range. To grow at the pace required by VCs, startups need exceptional new employees at all levels, individuals willing to work very, very hard. Options help recruit and retain the best talent. 

It works because the potential upside is very real. A key executive who received 1% in options of a unicorn startup (valuation of $1B) would have equity worth $10M. One tenth of one percent would represent $1M. Even 0.01% yields $100,000 when a unicorn exits. 

A core philosophy of venture capital that we learned from the 1950’s Traitorous Eight comes back into play here: sharing equity is the best motivator. Founders already have their shares; VCs have our preferred shares. Another pool, the option pool, is set aside for key future hires, often representing 10-20% of the total shares after the investment. 

The option pool is stated in the term sheet as a percentage of the entire equity pie after the investment, but the new common shares for the option pool are created just prior to the round of venture capital, for which preferred shares will be created. The word “prior” in the previous sentence has profound implications when it comes to dilution. Remember, when new shares are created, all previous shareholders are diluted. For option pools, since they are created just prior to the VC round, they will not dilute the new investors, the VCs. 

**==> picture [342 x 88] intentionally omitted <==**

Then we must add the VC investment to see how the round works: 

**==> picture [328 x 88] intentionally omitted <==**

Because the shares are created prior to the VC investment, they are included in the pre-money valuation, thus diluting the founders, not VCs. 

**==> picture [249 x 66] intentionally omitted <==**

As an example, let’s look at a $2M investment on a $3M pre-money. VCs would own 2/5 (investment ÷ post) or 40% after the deal. In our earlier calculations with no options pool, this leaves founders with 60%. However, if a 20% option pool were created as a part of the round, founders would end up with 40%. 

We’ll look at the math at the end of the chapter. For now, note that founders take this dilution hit. Despite trends toward founder friendly terms over the last decade, this remains a non-negotiable standard term in the industry. Even the NVCA model term sheet, which highlights negotiable terms, shows no negotiable option here. 

However, the size of the option pool is negotiable, and given the fact that the founders are penalized, the size of the option pool can be contentious. It adds a layer of complication on top of the pre-money valuation and investment size, as it effectively lowers the value of the shares owned by founders. This can be offset by a higher pre-money valuation. a founder is insistent on a Conversely, if certain pre-money, a larger option pool could be something VCs might negotiate. 

Still, there is a basic principle on which both sides can agree: the size of the option pool will depend on the needs of the startup. If we have a seasoned team of serial entrepreneurs, the option pool could be as low as 5%, recognizing that the key players are in place. On the other hand, with a couple of first-time entrepreneurs right out of college, we may need a large option pool, say 20-25%, to attract important new team members. 

## Other Deal Terms 

Investment size, valuation and option pool are the most fundamental terms on the term sheet, specifically spelling out how equity will be split and at what cost. These three terms are negotiated for every deal on a caseby-case basis. However, there are other terms that can have an effect on ownership and behavior and can create a contentious term sheet negotiation. 

I think it is fair at this point to remind ourselves just how revolutionary venture capital was and is. Traditionally in capitalism, those with the capital dictated the terms, and they historically abused that position to get the lion’s share of the benefits. This was partly a function of the lowskilled labor needed (to build railroads or make steel, for example). 

Conversely, venture capital was born from and benefits from a strategy of sharing risk and rewards with high-skilled labor. Since the Traitorous Eight started a new venture-backed company, VCs have seen the value in attracting the top talent, who share the risk with their considerable opportunity cost. Together, the capital and the talent create new value and share in the upside. 

That is the conceptual backdrop of the term sheet, where the rubber meets the road in these arrangements. VCs do employ a strategy of shared equity. However, we must also return capital to our LPs, or we’ll go out of business. LPs would never be able to take financial risk on startups without the structure and professionalism of the venture capital industry. 

VCs and founders have an unusual circumstance in which we sit across the table from one another while negotiating term sheets. We must hammer out a long-term contract in the face of extreme ambiguity. Then, once the deal is signed, we sit together on the same side of the table with a singular mission to grow the company, hoping that the legal contract that binds us will continue to serve both of our interests. 

The first two terms we have already discussed, investment size and valuation, would be pretty much all that we would need if we could guarantee that everything would go well. If we knew the startup would have a successful exit, we could all plan to divide the pie per our term sheet agreement, and we would all be pretty happy! 

However, there are other scenarios to consider. The rest of the terms on the term sheet are protective measures intended, in one way or another, to protect the investors. They tend to fall into one of three categories: upside protection, downside protection or control. 

It should be noted that all the terms below are theoretically negotiable for observation is that VCs are conformists for most any deal. However, my of these terms and are hesitant to deviate with whatever very they perceive to be the current convention. This is a case where “the market” seems to dictate. VCs know what our have peers been doing, and they tend to follow those trends very closely. 

Further, the conventions at any given time have more to do with macroeconomic conditions than the merits of a single deal. In the last decade, for example, as a result of more capital being available, deal terms have tended to favor the founders (“founder friendly”) more than they did, after the internet bubble had burst and there was say, just very little capital 

available in the early 2000s. Even the hottest startup in 2003 likely had terms in its term sheet that would be considered draconian today. 

While the terms below are theoretically negotiable on a startup-bystartup basis, they are often practically set in stone, dictated by the time and place (by the market), and will not change until macroeconomic conditions change. 

## Board Seats 

This is a control right, granting investors a specific number of seats on the board of directors. This term also designates the number of directors on the board. Boards for early-stage startups should be small, usually 3 or 5 members, consisting of one or two founders, one or two investors and an independent member mutually acceptable to founders and investors. There should always be an odd number so that there is no possibility of a tie during a pivotal vote. 

While boards of directors don’t get involved with the daily running of the company, they have an important oversight role in any corporation. In startups, boards will often have dramatic strategic influence, as members will have extensive industry experience and connections. 

CEOs will deliver regular updates at board meetings, sometimes as often as monthly. Boards vote on key issues, such as hiring and firing CEOs, entering into strategic partnerships and perhaps most important of all, choosing when to exit, such as how to respond to an offer of acquisition. 

## Pro Rata Rights 

The right to participate pro rata in future rounds is probably the most important and least contentious on the term sheet. It is most important because it guarantees the VCs the right to continue to invest in a startup as it grows. In betting parlance, you might think of this as doubling down on our winners to prevent dilution. Pro rata refers to maintaining the same level of 

ownership. If we have 10% coming out of the Series A, we have the right to invest in future rounds to maintain our 10%. 

As we’ll explore in more detail in future chapters, it is hard to overstress the importance of avoiding dilution in our winners, which can be thought of as upside protection. Our hit-driven thesis requires that we maximize returns from the few hits we are hoping to have in our portfolio. If we have a unicorn on our hands, we want to ride it for all it is worth! A 1% drop in ownership from a unicorn exit of $1B would result in a loss of $10M. Being diluted from 30% to 20% would represent a loss of $100M. 

This participation right is the not particularly contentious during term sheet negotiations because founders are quite happy to assume their Series A investors will be long-term partners and continue to support their growth all the to exit. There is little downside to founders unless way they have some reason to believe their relationship will go sour, which is not usually what we’re anticipating when negotiating a term sheet. 

## Liquidation Preference 

This special right gives investors first dibs on some of the proceeds at exit, intended to be downside protection to protect the investors if there is a mediocre exit. It allows investors to get our money back first before any proceeds are split. 

Frankly, this seems relatively fair and common in our culture. It’s easy to picture an investor of any project saying, “Hey, if this thing goes sideways, I get my money back first,” as a condition of the investment. In VC parlance, this is called a liquidation preference. 

The liquidation preference is stated as an “X” multiplier of the initial VC investment (usually plus dividends). A 1X liquidation preference is most common these days in the U.S., with higher liquidation preferences currently considered unfriendly to founders. 

Liquidation preferences are considered a downside protection because they are only relevant when things go poorly. They become irrelevant when there is a successful exit. For example, if we have a 20X exit, the extra 1X provided by a liquidation preference would not really move the needle. However, if our proceeds are going to be closer to 2-3X, getting 1X off the top would be a big deal. 

For example, let’s take a VC investment of $10M into a startup for 20% ownership. Let’s look at a rather tragic situation: the startup exits at a valuation of $20M. This is tragic because the post-money valuation after our $10M investment was $50M ($10M/20%). An exit of $20M would be a significant loss of value, more than half. Something tragic must have happened either within the startup or with macroeconomic conditions. 

With no liquidation preference, the VC firm would normally expect to receive 20% of the proceeds, or $4M. This would represent a loss of $6M from our $10M investment. Our LPs would not be happy. 

However, with a 1X liquidation preference, VCs would get the original investment of $10M off the top, leaving $10M to be split, of which we’d get $2M, for a total of $12M going to the VCs. 

$10M + $2M = $12M (liquidation preference) (20% of $10M) total proceeds 

Instead of losing money in this downside scenario, VCs (and our LPs) come out unscathed. It’s not a home run, by any means, but the loss has been mitigated. 

Of course, this mitigation came at the expense of the common shareholders, i.e., the founders and key employees who held options. A positive spin of this would be that it creates an incentive for everyone at the startup to perform as well as possible to achieve a better exit. 

Still, this can be a bitter pill for founders to swallow when term sheets are being negotiated. So VCs came up with a way to take a little bit of the sting out of liquidation preferences by adding a non-participating clause. In our example above, the VCs took the liquidation preference and also in the distribution of the was an participated remaining proceeds. That example of a 1X participating liquidation preference. 

If the term were non-participating, the VCs would have received only the liquidation preference to end up with $10M in proceeds. No loss, but no gain either. Downside protection. The rest would go to common shareholders, as the VCs would not participate in the equity split 

The legalese is a bit confusing, but the effect of a non-participating clause is to give VCs the option: either take the liquidation preference or participate in the distribution of proceeds based on the equity split. For a low exit, VCs will choose the former, thus being somewhat protected from the downside. For a big exit, the latter, no effect on the upside. In the earlier example, if the startup gets to a $500M exit, investors would much prefer the 20% split to receive $100M rather than get the original $10M back. 

Per the legal language of these terms, the way it actually works is that VCs would have to convert preferred shares to common shares to participate in the equity split. In converting, we lose all preferences, including liquidation preferences. 

There is another middle road option that VCs sometimes put in the terms: a cap on participation. This is also expressed as a multiple, such as 2X or 3X, and will limit the VCs participation in the distribution of proceeds if we choose to exercise the liquidation preference. As in the nonparticipating option, we may forego this option, convert to common stock, and participate per usual. 

Here are three typical options for a liquidation preference: 

- 1X participating: investors will receive money back and then pro rata distribution of proceeds. 

- 1X non-participating: investors will either receive up to 1X or will choose to convert to common stock to participate in distribution. 

- 1X participating with a 3X cap: investors will either receive up to 1X, then split proceeds up to 3X, or will choose to convert to common stock to participate in distribution. 

All three have the similar effect to protect the VCs from a poor to mediocre exit. Where we run into some differentiation is when a startup has 

a decent but not spectacular exit. We’ll look at a few scenarios in the math section at the end of this chapter. 

## Dividends 

This preferred right is a straight-up sweetener for investors that looks to me like boilerplate handed down from our private equity roots. Dividends simply give investors an extra percentage of return on their investment, conventionally in the 6-8% range in the U.S. Conceptually, dividends represent a semi-guaranteed rate of return. 

Dividends make a lot of sense for tradi tional private equity investments and even to later stage VC, where more dollars are put to work in each deal, and lower multiples are expected. Those types of firms have more consistent rate of return across their portfolios. If we expect all the companies in our portfolio to return 10-20% annually, adding a 6-8% dividend to each is quite a sweetener. 

However, for early stage VCs, dividends lose much of their relevance. Our investment thesis is hit-driven. We will have wildly differing rates of returns, from 0% to over 1000% (10X). Of course, a zero return will also yield zero dividends. At the other end of the spectrum, when we have a hit with a high multiple, dividends are almost negligible. 

If we invest $10M and get a homerun 20X return in 5 years, we’ll have $200M coming back to us. The extra 6-8% of annual dividends on our original $10M after 5 years would be something like $3-4M, perhaps 2% of our total returns. 

Regional VC firms care more about dividends, as the regional portfolio is more likely filled with doubles and triples rather than homeruns and busts. If we invest the same $10M above into a 3X winner, we’ll be looking at proceeds of $30M. Adding $3-4M looks a little more relevant in that scenario, though it still only represents perhaps 10% of proceeds. 

Dividends make the biggest difference on the downside, when minimal proceeds are produced at the exit. In those cases, dividends can add up. In the same 5-year horizon above, if the exit returned only 1X to 

investors, that is, our money back, the extra 30-40% in dividends would make a big difference in terms of providing a return on that one deal. However, in the context of our entire portfolio, unless we have a lot of startups go sideways like this, dividends will be non-material. 

Dividends have several negotiable points around whether they accumulate and when and how they are paid. Depending on the firm, particularly how much its strategy is swinging for the fences, dividends could be a term insisted as boilerplate (“that’s just the way we do it”) or a non-issue. 

## Anti-Dilution 

This is another downside protection included in the term sheet to mitigate a problem, in this case, a down round, which occurs when a round of funding is priced lower than the previous round. For example, a Series B valuation is lower than a Series A pre-money post-money valuation. In a down round, value has decreased, possibly from the startup missing milestones, or perhaps a macroeconomic hiccup (like a stock market crash). 

As we’ll discover in more detail in the next chapter, dilution is a serious risk for VCs, and down rounds can be killers when it comes to dilution (see Down Rounds, p.108). An anti-dilution clause will allow the Series A investor to reprice our old shares, thus gaining new shares to compensate for dilution. Who takes the hit when this happens? Again, the common shareholders (founders). 

The most typical way to do this lately is called weighted-average, which gets mathematically complicated but effectively shares some of the dilution between the founders and Series A investors. 

## Cap Table 

Between founders, investors and options, the allocation of the equity can get complicated fast. The capitalization table is the spreadsheet that indicates who owns what. Again, we don’t want to delve too deeply into the mathematics yet, but I want you to see an example of a cap table for a Series A (below). 

**==> picture [458 x 166] intentionally omitted <==**

This cap table above represents a 4 on 4 deal: a negotiated pre-money valuation of $4M and a negotiated total Series A round of $4M, resulting in an $8M post money valuation. There was also a negotiated option pool just over 15%. You can see in this cap chart that the option pool was created by adding shares “Pre-A,” thus diluting the common shareholders and not the new preferred shareholders. You can also see that the round was syndicated, [21] 

## Invest Summary 

Not surprisingly, this has been a busy chapter! The venture capital investment includes process sourcing deals, performing due diligence, building syndicates and putting down term sheets. 

The two fundamental terms on the term sheet are the investment size and the valuation, which “price the deal” and determine how much equity founders are giving up to investors. An option pool is often added (if it does not already exist) to aid in recruiting top talent and also plays a role in diluting founders. 

Both valuations and investment sizes may be the subject of complex negotiations that balance various motivations for both the investors and the founders. In early stage deals (usually pre-institutional), convertible debt may be used to avoid conflict over the valuation. 

Other terms on the term sheet explain the preferences VCs received with our preferred shares, including liquidation preferences, board seats, dividends, pro rata rights and an anti-dilution provision.

## Case Study: VC Razor Ventures Series A 

Jeff and Mark are nearing the end of their third year of their first fund, VCIC I, a $50M 

are a micro-fund. Things going very well, maybe little too well. They had targeted 10-12 portfolio companies with a total of $4M for each. They have already invested in 8 seed rounds averaging $500,000. Two of those startup have gone on to raise Series A rounds at $3M and $4M. 

VC Razor I was the lead investor in both Series A rounds, with $1M and $2M investments, respectively. So far, they’ve had capital calls totaling $7M. Another $25M is reserved as dry powder for follow-on rounds for these 8 portfolio companies. 

Today, they are really excited about a ninth company, brought to them by one of their syndicate partners on another deal. This one is a little unusual in that it is a Series A for a startup for which they did not participate in the seed round. The other firm will lead the Series A. VC Razor I is being invited to piggyback on the round as a follower. 

Having already invested in 8 seed rounds, Jeff and Mark are feeling the burn of juggling so many early stage companies at once. It seems that everybody needs help at the same time. Coming in as a follower for this Series A may be a good way to deploy some capital without depleting more of their human resources. 

Meanwhile, they are nearly out of available funds for new investments. All 8 investments are ongoing, and all are (knock on wood) still very promising. This is great news, but it also means that all of the funds reserved for those investments needs to remain reserved. That leaves room for only two more investments. Should this Series A opportunity be one of those last two? 

It is becoming increasingly clear that they need to raise a new fund, but it’s early. Being only the third of their have no exits to year fund, they tout. The Series A rounds indicate a little increase in valuation for two of the early investments, but those are unrealized gains. They have executed their plan to the tee, but it is still unknown whether the plan was a good one. LPs have received no proceeds to date, and there are none on the horizon. 

TO PONDER: If you were an LP, what would you want to hear from Jeff and Mark so that you would be comfortable allocating a portion of your portfolio to their new fund? 

## � VC Math: Investing 

Investment Size: Startup Perspective 

We discussed that founders have an incentive to wait to raise capital: a higher valuation that would result in less dilution. Let’s look at the math. 

If an early-stage startup raised a $500K round at a $1M valuation, the result would be 33% dilution, equal to the amount owned by the VCs: 

$500K VC Investment Ownership/ = = 33% $1.5M PostDilution Money 

However, if the founders we’re able to raise only $100K at the same pre-money, perhaps from friends and family, then raise the remaining $400K at a $2M valuation, they would only be diluted 26% (9% + 17%): 

## $100K 

1[st] Round Investment = = 9% Dilution $1.1M PostMoney 

**==> picture [241 x 69] intentionally omitted <==**

The resulting 26% represents 7 percentage points not lost as compared to the 33% suffered if they raised the entire amount on the lower valuation. If this startup went on to exit at $50M, the founders would receive an additional $3.5M ($37M instead of $33.5M). 

Even better, what if they were able to hustle and bootstrap to achieve a $2M pre-money valuation without raising any outside capital? 

**==> picture [41 x 14] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [230 x 50] intentionally omitted <==**

Proceeds for a $50M exit would be $40M rather than $33.5M, ignoring any other special rights such as liquidation preferences and dividends. 

## Option Pools 

For the purposes of this book, with a focus on strategy instead of finance, I have opted not to deal with the issuance of shares. Rather, we will look at ownership like a pie, with each party receiving a slice of equity. Dealing with the actual shares adds another layer of algebra, and while it is not particularly complicated, it is also not particularly relevant in terms of strategic impact. 

To add an option pool to a round of venture financing, we start with the regular pre + investment to determine the percentage ownership as if there were no option pool: 

**==> picture [418 x 103] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [197 x 74] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [197 x 83] intentionally omitted <==**

If we have a pre-money of $6M and an investment of $4M, that would yield 60% ownership to the founders excluding any option pool: 

**==> picture [414 x 105] intentionally omitted <==**

Then, to add an option pool, we simply carve out a portion of the founders’ slice. Adding A 10% option pool in this case would bring the founders’ down to 50%, worth $5M out of the total $10M post-money. 

We can then back-engineer to see the effect on the pre-money valuation, which is now split $5M to founders and $1M to the option pool. 

**==> picture [458 x 104] intentionally omitted <==**

Scenario 2: $2M pre-money with a $1M investment. Two equal cofounders. Post-money is $3M. In this case, with no option pool, the founders and the VCs each owns 33% at the end of the deal. 

**==> picture [458 x 118] intentionally omitted <==**

If a 20% option pool is created, it will come out of the founders’ percentage. In this case, since the founders are equal, each cofounder would lose 10% to the option pool. 

**==> picture [258 x 103] intentionally omitted <==**

The post-money valuation is $3M, 20% of which is $600K. Each cofounder lost $300K of value to the option pool. 

**==> picture [260 x 109] intentionally omitted <==**

Now that we have the equity split at post, we can work backwards to see the value of the option pool in the pre-money valuation. 

**==> picture [458 x 101] intentionally omitted <==**

We can see that in this example, the addition of a 20% option pool has devalued the founders’ portion of the pre-money by 30%, from $1M each to $700K each. Conceptually, this is like the founders participating in the round, each contributing $300K toward future growth of the company. 

Scenario 3: $5M investment on a $20M pre-money. Note that the here is 10X our second pre-money previous example. Stated differently, the startup has created more value prior to raising this round. This can come from two places: either they’ve lowered the risk by hitting more milestones or and are they identified pursuing a larger opportunity, which would result in a larger exit. 

Though not always true, there is often a reverse correlation between valuation and option pool size: low valuation potentially implying we will need a be the result of more large option pool. A higher valuation might maturity already on the team and a thus lead to a lower need for options to recruit talent. 

In this example, let’s go with a 10% option pool. That gives us a “5 on 20 pre” with a 10% option pool. What is the resulting value of the founders’ equity? With no option pool, founders would own 20/25 or 80%. 

**==> picture [414 x 105] intentionally omitted <==**

Since the 10% option pool comes directly out of the founders’ equity, the founders stake is lowered to 70%, resulting in the founders ownership value decreasing: 70% x 25 = $17.5M. Hence the $20M pre-money is split $17.5M founders and $2.5M options.
