## Liquidation Preference 

Let’s look at the three different scenarios discussed in the section above and how they affect the split of proceeds in different exit situations. Let’s go with: 

- 1X participating preferred 

- 1X non-participating preferred 

- 1X with participation up to 3X 

Let’s startup with a downside scenario since this is the main function of a liquidation preference. 

Scenario 1 : $10M investment, 33% ownership, $40M exit. First, let’s note that this is not an ideal exit. The post-money valuation implied by this scenario is $30M. Exiting at $40M is not great, but at least some value was created after the last round. 

With 1X participating, first we get our $10M back, then we split the remaining $30M: 

$10M + $10M = $20M (liquidation preference) (33% of $30M) total proceeds 

With 1X non-participating: we have the option to take 1X (and not or the so that we participate) forego liquidation preference can participate along with common shareholders: 

$10M + $0 = $10M (liquidation preference) (non-participation) total proceeds or $0 + $13.3M = $13.3M 

(33% of $40M) 

(liquidation preference) 

total proceeds 

We’d pick the latter. To do so, we’d convert to common shares and lose or liquidation preference. Lastly, with 1X participating up to 3X, we can see above that the proceeds to participate will only get to $20M, or 2X. We’d do that. 

Scenario 2: same as above with $500M exit. This is an excellent exit, the startup having increased in value nearly 17X after our investment (from $30M post to $500M exit). 

With 1X participating, first we get our $10M back, then we split the remaining $490M: 

$10M + $163M = $173M (liquidation preference) (33% of $490M) total proceeds 

With 1X non-participating: we have the option to take 1X or the 33%: 

$10M + $0 = $10M (liquidation preference) (non-participation) total proceeds or $0 + $167M = $167M (liquidation preference) (33% of $500M) total proceeds 

Lastly, with 1X participating up to 3X, we can see above that the proceeds to participate will be far above 3X and we will convert to common to receive $167M. 

Scenario 2: same as above with $100M exit. This is a mediocre exit, the startup having increased in value just over 3X (from $30M post to $100M exit). 

With 1X participating, first we get our $10M back, then we split the remaining $90M: 

$10M + $30M = $40M (liquidation preference) (33% of $90M) total proceeds 

With 1X non-participating: we have the option to take 1X or the 33%. It’s clear that $33M is more than $10M, so we convert to common. Lastly, with 1X participating up to 3X, we have a closer call. 

$10M + $30 = $40M (liquidation preference) (participate up to 3X) total proceeds 

or $0 + $33M = $33M (liquidation preference) (33% of $100M) total proceeds 

In this case, we do better to exercise our preferred rights rather than to convert to common and participate in pro rata distribution. 

## Dividends 

Dividends are another downside protection for VCs. They are usually less of a factor than liquidation preferences, but they can add up. Because they can accumulate annually, we need to add the element of time. If we look at the scenario from the very last section, with a $10M investment for 33% ownership, let’s say the $100M exit takes 5 years. Cumulative dividends of 7% over 5 years would be: 

7% x $10M x 5 = $3.5M (dividend) (investment) (years) total 

## dividends 

If we have a $500M exit and proceeds in excess of $170M, we won’t particularly care about this extra amount. However, $3.5M could make a material difference in the other scenarios with low to medium exits with proceeds in the $10-20M range. 

## VC Math Problems 

1. What percentage of ownership do founders retain after a $2M investment on a $3M pre-money valuation: 

   - a. With no option pool? 

   - b. With a 10% option pool? 

   - c. With a 15% option pool? 

2. What percent do the VCs retain in all three of the above scenarios? 

3. Same situation, assume no future rounds of investing and an exit in 5 years at a valuation of $50M. What proceeds do the founders receive: 

   - a. If there were a 10% option pool and no liquidation preference? 

   - b. With a 10% option pool and a 1X participating liquidation preference? 

   - c. With a 20% option pool and 1X non-participating liquidation preference? 

4. What percentage ownership do VCs receive if the invest $6M on a $12M pre-money valuation? 

5. What percentage would the founders retain if there were a 10% option pool? 

6. What proceeds would VCs and founders receive if there were a $54M exit, including the 10% option pool but no liquidation preference? 

7. How much is left over and who gets it? 

8. What do all three receive in the same scenario if there is a 2X liquidation preference? 

9. The founders received $60M of a $100M exit having raised one round of $10M with a 7% option pool (no other terms). What was the pre-money valuation? 

Same deal, what are proceeds for VCs? 

**==> picture [313 x 177] intentionally omitted <==**

## 3. Grow 

This aspect of the VC’s job, helping startups grow, is perhaps least appreciated and yet most important. Most VCs I’ve worked with consider themselves company builders, not simply investors. We don’t just create a portfolio and kick up our heels. For many, this is the favorite part of the job, rolling up our sleeves to help startups succeed. 

A startup is a unique type of investment going through a remarkable metamorphosis. The months following a venture capital investment are tumultuous for a startup and everyone who works there. Hopefully, it is a constructive tumult, a controlled mayhem, a chaotic but value-creating process. 

There are many parallels here again with the music business. Our early startup is like a talented young band. Handing them $1M without any supervision is not a recipe for success. They need the guidance of industry pros who have been there, done that. 

Record labels don’t create a roster and walk away from it. They must work the roster. True, they need to attract top talent into the roster, but it is what they do with that talent that separates the top labels (and VC firms). 

This flies in the face of the mythology of the discovered artist, who is often sitting innocently in a coffee shop when a talent agent stumbles by. In reality, thousands of artists are “discovered” and never become famous. They get cast in a movie or sent into a recording studio, and magic does not ensue. They fall flat. Happens all the time. When I was pursuing a record 

deal, the industry was rife with stories about bands with record deals doing nothing. Went into the studio. Cut a record. Went nowhere. 

When “discovered talent” does eventually become famous, the myth is that the success was in the pre-ordained and obvious, just sitting there But it was no more the hit than it was for coffee shop! pre-determined for the talented flops. What is much more likely to determine who becomes a hit is work ethic, often a superhuman work ethic. How many “overnight successes” have spent years and years honing their craft? 

## Working the Portfolio 

Record labels and VC firms have entire bullpens full of potential. They constantly work the bullpen, keeping an eye on everyone’s performance, looking for clues, any little hint that could indicate who might be the next breakout performer. 

Back in the day, when a new band started getting some regional radio airplay, the label would pour resources toward making a bigger success out of that small win. All of a sudden, that band would be booked on talk shows, the song would be placed in movies and promoters around the country would be hired to advocate for more radio play. A life-size full-color display would be set up at, I’m going to say it, record stores! Display ads would appear in, wait for it, magazines! The band would go on tour as the opening act for a big national band. It is no accident that when a song is a hit, you here it everywhere. 

All these things are very expensive. Record labels cannot commit these resources to every act on the roster. They have to work the bullpen and make tough decisions about the next act that will get the benefit of the machine that is a major record label. You and I cannot call Steven Spielberg and ask him to put our hot new band in his next movie. David Geffen can. 

VCs similarly cannot commit all our resources to every startup in our portfolio. We work the roster in our own way, similarly looking for traction, and similarly looking to push the gas pedal when something starts to take 

I would argue that the true core competency of venture capitalists is the craft of working the portfolio, choosing when to pour on resources and when to withhold. It is the competency of grooming and preparing portfolio companies so that they may be in the position, if they work their asses off, to become unicorns. 

Early stage investments are called seed funding for good reason. VCs plant a lot of seeds to see which ones will flourish. The seedlings that sprout get a whole lot of attention: meetings with potential strategic partners, leads for big sales calls, more funding… The process is more like unicorn raising than hunting. 

Just as record labels make stars out of raw talent, VCs know how to make unicorns out of startups. Nobody knows more about what it is like to grow a venture from 3 to 300 employees than VCs, who have done it time and time again. Not many humans have experienced the roller coaster ride of growing startups. 

Through this process, VCs and record labels share a moral hazard that individuals may not want to emulate. The bullpen model requires that some of the talent in the bullpen will inevitably be neglected. This should be the result of underperformance, but it is often a product of circumstance. 

What it almost never is…an easy decision. VCs tell me that these are the hardest decisions: deciding how to divide time and resources amongst portfolio companies; when to limit the time devoted to an underperforming startup; whether a startup even is underperforming or might take off with a little more help. The fading potential starlet, discovered in the coffee shop, has been cast in a few minor roles that never took off but still has the potential to be a big star if only we could figure out the right role for her! 

## Active Participation 

Recall from our definition of venture capital that one of the differentiators is that we actively participate in our investments. We are not simply picking stocks. This can go well beyond simply serving on the board of directors. 

After we make an investment, we often roll up our sleeves to help our startups. Our expertise and vast networks are invaluable for connecting startups with potential partners and customers. Just as a record label can a in a movie or a new band on tour a place song big put opening for bigger act, VCs can make significant connections for startups. 

The same networks that we utilize in and due fundraising diligence are re-employed toward helping startups in the portfolio. 

**==> picture [196 x 195] intentionally omitted <==**

We have relationships with decisionmakers at big companies who have partnered with, been customers of, or perhaps even acquired other startups in our portfolio. These decisionmakers will take our calls and meet with our people, knowing that we’re putting our credibility on the line. 

## Figure: VC’s professional network (again) 

Compare that to the responses unfunded startups get while cold calling. The same is true across our network. Other VCs, our LPs, serial entrepreneurs…their networks are a part of our network. We stake our 

reputations by reaching out to our colleagues when we have something exciting to share. We also make ourselves available to them when they have something exciting for the same reasons. 

The more we crate value in the network, the more highly we are regarded and the more quickly calls are answered, favors are granted and, most importantly, we get included on a hot deal that is otherwise impossible to get in on. 

On the previous page, I asserted that working the bullpen is a VC core competency. Let me revise that statement to include working the entire network: the bullpen, the bench, the coaching staff, the refs, the sky boxes. Our competency is creating value in this complicated ecosystem. 

Because we create value for our portfolio companies beyond supplying cash, venture capital is considered a service business. We are, in service to our based on how we effect, in startups. VC firms differentiate service our portfolios. Some firms are famously deeply involved, perhaps to a fault. Others are more laid back, preferring to let the firm’s reputation speak for itself (and open doors for anyone associated with it). 

Some firms go so far as to bring on support staff to do work on behalf of all their portfolio companies, helping them handle challenges common to all startups. Hiring new people, for example, is part and parcel with growth. Some VC firms have talent coordinators to leverage the VC firm’s network and help portfolio companies with recruitment. PR is another need shared by all startups. Many firms have PR staff to help promote the entire portfolio. 

Another thing that portfolio companies need…more funding. 

## Follow-on Rounds 

True, VCs are often active investors creating value beyond the dollars invested. However, VCs’ main tool for growing companies is capital. The Series A was the a just beginning. If startup gains traction, much more capital will be needed to reach unicorn-like growth levels. 

As we’ve discussed, VCs are planning on several rounds of investing before exit. The first institutional round, when VCs enter the picture, is the Series A, and the plan is to have one or more follow-on rounds, called Series B, Series C, etc., on the way to a successful (big) exit. 

Figure: Rounds of equity financing 

**==> picture [378 x 231] intentionally omitted <==**

Let’s recall that this happens within the framework of a 10-year fund, in which VCs can only make new investments for the first handful of years: 

**==> picture [362 x 118] intentionally omitted <==**

Figure: VC fund 10-year life 

Below is an example of a startup that received three rounds of VC financing before reaching an exit. Between each round, the startup performed well, hitting milestones and earning a higher valuation and larger investment in later rounds. In this case, it was about 5 years from the Series A to exit, year 3 to year 8. 

Figure: Example of a successful exit 

**==> picture [398 x 188] intentionally omitted <==**

This pattern repeats itself throughout the fund’s portfolio. For example, a typical mid-size fund might invest in somewhere between 15-25 startups, perhaps averaging 2-3 rounds of financing each. That’s potentially 75 deals that have to be negotiated! You start to get the idea about why VCs have severe time constraints. 

Figure: Portfolio of 10-20 investments 

**==> picture [402 x 194] intentionally omitted <==**

The above graphical representation looks like a pretty healthy portfolio. However, graphically speaking, I don’t see any unicorns in there. Below is a graphic that shows how large follow-on rounds can get when a startup is 

taking off. These are not usual results. This graph is the investment size only. No valuations are included. 

Figure: Example follow-on rounds for unicorns (valuation not included) 

**==> picture [378 x 195] intentionally omitted <==**

## Reserve Capital 

Every time our VC fund invests in a Series A, we make a note to save a certain amount of funding, called reserve capital or dry powder, to participate in follow-on rounds in that same startup. That is the plan. Several rounds. 

The reserved funding is somewhat conceptual. There is no real money put aside, no escrow account created, no capital call to our LPs funds are at our is on today. However, the earmarked, and everyone firm notice that some of our fund is reserved for this startup. 

VCs often have a baseline plan to reserve 2-3X of the Series A investment for follow-on rounds. You might ask, why not just write a bigger check up front when the valuation is lower? Splitting the investment across multiple rounds is helpful in two ways: risk mitigation and opportunism. 

We’ve already discussed how we can mitigate capital risk by investing less up front, giving us the ability to cut bait if the startup doesn’t gain 

traction after a Series A. “Cut bait” in this case means to free up the funds that had been reserved for follow-on rounds. Those newly available funds can then be redeployed into our winners. 

The risk is not the we mitigation having deployed all capital thought we might into a startup that has faltered. The opportunism is redeploying that into our like the record label was capital winners, just doing when investing to make a hit song show up everywhere. The dollars going into supporting a hit provide the best returns. VCs need to make quick tough decisions about failing startups so that we may redeploy much needed resources (time and capital) to the winners. 

In addition to the benefits that VCs receive, founders also gain a significant advantage from breaking investments across several rounds: they are able to retain more equity. If entrepreneurs had to take all the funding ever need in one sum at the their they’d lump beginning of venture, they would lose an enormous amount of ownership. 

For example, let’s estimate that a startup needs $20M to get to exit. If the founders accepted a term sheet to receive all $20M in a Series A with on a valuation would result in founders pre-money of, say, $5M, it being diluted from 100% ownership to 20% (founder ownership = pre/post = $5M/$25M = 20%). 

However, in the same scenario, if only $3M is raised on the $5M premoney valuation, founders are only diluted to 62.5% (5/8). If the next two rounds were a “6 on 16” and an “11 on 30” (I am hypothesizing to result in the same $20M total investment, 3+6+11), founders would end up with about 33% ownership rather than the 20% above. We’ll walk through this math at the end of the chapter, but here is a chart to summarize: 

Table: Founder dilution example with total $20M needed to exit: 

one round (20%) vs.three rounds (33%) 

**==> picture [172 x 65] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [145 x 50] intentionally omitted <==**

This has proven to be a pretty good trade-off: VCs spread out our capital risk and entrepreneurs retain a little more equity. However, guess 

what can happen if the markets are down and startups are having a very hard time getting funding? In those situations, VCs sit in the driver seat, and norms begin to shift to favor the investors. Such was the case in the mid2000s when tranching came into vogue. 

## Tranching 

A tranche is simply a division of something larger, and “to tranche” in VC lingo is to divide a round so that only a portion of the capital is given to the startup at one time. Some of the round is withheld, to be distributed when specific goals are met. 

In the example above, if the $20M were distributed over three tranches rather than three rounds, the VCs would price the round ($5M premoney) and commit to a $3M investment today with the rest contingent on milestones. 

If that sounds like VCs getting the benefit of lower capital risk without founders getting the benefit of retaining more equity, bingo! That is exactly what it was. Tranching is not founder friendly and was only common for a handful of years. Since 2010, the VC market has been much more competitive, meaning that VC firms have had to compete to get deals. In a competitive environment, founder friendly terms become the norm, and tranching is often a non-starter. 

Tranching also suffers because it does not accurately reflect the underlying economic realities. If milestones are hit, valuations should increase. Tranching a round implies that we have the ability to pre-price those future valuation increases today into a fair market pre-money valuation for the current round. 

Most that it makes more sense to fund a some agree startup to hit milestones, and then let the market decide what that should be worth. “Let the market decide” is VC lingo for raising a round and letting other VCs price it (see Outside Lead Investor below). Early investors will be rewarded with good returns in an up round, and perhaps more importantly, we’ll have a guaranteed seat at the table for the next round. In VC investing, one of the biggest challenges is getting in on the hot deals. 

## Dilution 

Just as founders get diluted when new shares are created and sold, so do early investors. Each new round of funding dilutes all pre-existing equity shareholders. As new shares are issued and sold, the percent ownership of the earlier equity owners goes down. 

**==> picture [418 x 154] intentionally omitted <==**

Conceptually, you can think of it as: 

**==> picture [418 x 144] intentionally omitted <==**

Founders, for example, start with 100% ownership of their startup. After receiving a couple of angel investments, they might be down to something like 80%. Then a Series A could bring them down to 60%; then after a Series B, say 45%, and so on. 

All along, if things go well and milestones are hit, the valuation will rise. The smaller (diluted) percentage of equity will be worth more because of the new higher valuation. Another way to think of this is that the share price increases. 

You may recall the grape and watermelon metaphor in the last chapter. Better to own a slice of watermelon than the whole grape. 

Founders are hopeful that they’re on the way to a unicorn exit, of which they may still own 15-30%.[22] Google and Facebook founders, for example, still held a very impressive 25-30% of the equity at the IPO. Not bad considering they were valued at exit at $26B and $104B, respectively, making Larry and Sergey nearly $4B and Zuckerberg over $25B. 

## Pro Rata 

Early stage VCs carry a similar risk of dilution as the founders, with a significant difference. We have a mechanism to mitigate dilution: participation in future rounds. Typically, early stage VCs will attempt to maintain a pro rata stake in the startup. That is, we plan to maintain roughly the same equity position (% ownership) that we received in the Series A through future rounds, all the way to exit, if possible. 

To see how this will work, let’s start with a simple example of dilution, a Series A investment of $4M on a $6M pre-money valuation. That results in 40% ownership for the Series A investors and 60% for founders, with a post-money valuation of $10M. 

Figure: Series A “4 on 6” deal 

**==> picture [314 x 81] intentionally omitted <==**

Now let’s add a Series B of $10M. If this $10M is added to the $10M post-money from the Series A, then the earlier shareholders are diluted by 50%. This might look like: 

Figure: Series B “10 on 10” after a Series A “4 on 6” 

**==> picture [406 x 126] intentionally omitted <==**

The algebra in this example case is very easy: 50% dilution results in halving the early percentages. In this case, the Series A investors have been diluted from 40% to 20%. If the Series A investors want to maintain pro rata, we would need to take the same percentage of the Series B as we held at of the startup after the A round, in this case, 40%. 

The result would be Series A investors taking $4M of the B round. That $4M would result in 20% ownership post B ($4M of $20M post). Added to the Series A 20%, we would maintain pro rata of 40%. We’ll dive more deeply into the math at the end of the chapter. 

**==> picture [106 x 167] intentionally omitted <==**

Let’s stop for a moment and think about the strategic implications of the numbers here. Remember, VCs are always connecting the dots between numbers and strategy. In this case, we have made a bad assumption with our numbers. Take a look at the pre-money valuation of the Series B and compare it to the post-money valuation of the Series A. It’s the same. That’s a problem. 

The Series A post-money valuation should not be projected to be equal to the Series B pre-money valuation. That implies no value was created between the rounds, and it would be called a flat round. 

Figure: Flat round 

True, this could happen, but we certainly are not projecting that it will happen. When we project the future, as the optimistic contrarians we are, we always project up rounds. 

## Up Rounds 

Remember, there is something important happening between each round of investment. The startup is supposed to be hitting milestones! (See the arrow between the rounds.) 

**==> picture [414 x 134] intentionally omitted <==**

More specifically, the Series A investment is the cash to be used to hit milestones, which creates value and result in a higher valuation. When the pre-money valuation of the B Round is higher than the post-money valuation of the A Round, it is called up round. 

Let’s assume our startup hit a big milestone, resulting in a doubling in valuation. The pre-money for Series B would then be $20M (after the Series A post-money valuation of $10M). Infusing $10M on a $20M pre would dilute by 10/30, or 1/3. 

**==> picture [120 x 251] intentionally omitted <==**

Figure: Up round from $10M post-A to $10M pre-B 

So, let’s look at the dilution that Series A investors would suffer in this up round. Because the Series B investors receive 33% (10/30), Series A investors are diluted by 33%, from 40% to 27% (40*.67). Since our plan is to maintain pro rata ownership, we would need to plan to invest in the Series B to maintain our 40%. Again, this would require Series A investors to take $4M of the Series B. (More about the math at the end of the chapter.) 

Figure: Series B “10 on 20” after a Series A “4 on 6” 

**==> picture [371 x 115] intentionally omitted <==**

The money we invest in the Series B comes from the dry powder we had reserved for follow-on rounds for this startup. Our exposure for this deal would now be a total of $8M across two rounds ($4M in Series A and $4M 

in Series B). Our total ownership after Series B would be 40% of the total equity. We would own two classes of preferred shares, Series A and Series B, each of which may have different rights. 

This situation begs the question: if we are only putting in 40% of the Series B, who puts in the other 60%? The answer is our syndicate partners. Since our plan all along was to maintain a pro rata position, we would have already been courting syndicate partners to be ready to take most of the Series B. 

In fact, we may have already brought a key syndicate partner into the Series A, particularly if we think this startup will have a lot of potential and need a lot of funding and other help to get there. 

We will attempt to follow this pattern through future follow-on rounds, Series C, Series D, etc., all the way to exit. 

## Outside Lead Investor 

Each time shares are issued (each round), the new investors are motivated to negotiate the lowest possible share price, i.e., the lowest possible pre-money valuation, for that round. Their return will be based on the increase in valuation after that round. The lower the pre-money valuation, the higher the potential return for them. Buy low, sell high. 

However, the founders and early investors are motivated to negotiate the highest possible pre-money valuation to minimize the dilutive effect of the round. When Series A investors are participating in the Series B, we have conflicting interests. We want our Series A to have appreciated as much as possible, and we concurrently want the Series B to be as cheap as possible. 

Often at this point, the Series A investors will seek a new VC firm to come in lead the round, including pricing. This achieves a couple of things. First, it alleviates our conflict. We now have the market deciding the price of the next round. Second, it brings new credibility to the startup. If a round is made up entirely of insiders (firms who have already invested), it could be a 

signal that new investors are not interested. This could limit the potential of the startup. 

## Down Rounds 

**==> picture [193 x 166] intentionally omitted <==**

When we are due performing diligence on a startup and envisaging fast growth all the way to exit, we will always project up rounds. However, bad things can and do happen, both internally and externally. A startup may run out of cash before hitting a milestone, or the public markets might crash just as we were planning a major expansion. 

## Figure: Influences on valuation after Series A 

We won’t always be able to control when a startup needs to raise capital, and if it is happening at the wrong time, we might encounter one of the biggest fears of VCs and founders alike: the dreaded down round. 

Down rounds are dreaded for a simple reason: dilution. The algebraic outcome of adding new shares when the startup has been devalued creates a significant disadvantage for early shareholders. 

In our earlier example, with a $4M Series A investment on a $6M pre-money, we saw the difference in dilution between a flat round vs. an up round. With the up-round, the pre-money for Series B was 2X the postmoney of Series A (increased from $10M to $20M). Series A investors were diluted from 40% to 27%. 

In the flat-round scenario, the pre-money valuation remained the same as the post-money from the Series A, at $10M. Series A investors went from 40% to 20% ownership. 

Now let’s look at a down round. Dilution becomes a very serious risk when things go awry. Let’s say we dropped from $10M post-money Series A to $5M pre-money Series B. If the Series B investment remains $10M, Series A and founders will be diluted by 67%, 2/3. Our Series A stake of 40% is lowered by 2/3 to 13.3%! 

Figure: Dilution effects of up, flat and down rounds with a $10M Series B 

|UP ROUND:<br>33% dilution|FLAT<br>ROUND:<br>50%<br>dilution|DOWN<br>ROUND:<br>67%<br>dilution|
|---|---|---|



In this down round scenario, the valuation is cut in half for the Series B, meaning our original $4M investment has decreased in value by 50%, to $2M. At the end of Series B, we own 13.33% of a $15M post-money, again, $2M. 

A scary thought for Series A investors: down rounds can happen even when a startup is performing well. Macro events can move the whole market downward at the most inopportune times, such as after the great recession of 2008. 

It is difficult to overstate how destructive down rounds are to the VC investment process, but Series A investors do have a couple of protections. First, we have the ability to participate in the Series B, taking advantage of the lower share prices to purchase more equity. 

Series A investors will also usually insist on an “anti-dilution” clause as a special right that comes with preferred shares. In the event of a down round, an anti-dilution clause protects the Series A investors by issuing extra shares in the Series B to minimize dilution. 

Startups: More or Less Money Now? 

As a rule, founders should not want to take any more money than is necessary to hit a significant milestone because hitting that milestone will increase valuation. When valuation goes up, the share price goes up, meaning you can sell fewer shares for the same amount of money, minimizing dilution. The plan should be for a Series A to hit certain milestones, followed by a Series B at a higher valuation once the milestones are hit. 

Of course, it is not certain that a founder will hit the milestones nor be able to raise another round. Those are big ifs, and most entrepreneurs are survivalists. There is an incentive to get as much cash as possible when you can get it. More cash now means more wiggle room to recover from any potential problems down the road. 

Fundraising can also take time and energy away from growing the business. It is distracting for a CEO to have to be on the road fundraising all the time rather than running the company. This is a powerful incentive to raise more money now, even though it is more expensive, i.e., results in more dilution, to do so. 

So there is an internal conflict on the founders’ side: get as much funding as possible versus waiting until milestones are hit and dilution won’t be as big an issue. 

## VCs: More or Less Money Now? 

VCs also have an internal conflict when it comes to the investment size of a given round. If we are bullish on a startup, we may want to put more money to work, thereby getting more equity for lower dollars and increasing the potential return on this round’s investment. 

Contrarily, VCs have an incentive to limit our exposure by only risking a little bit of capital now, knowing we’ll have the opportunity to invest again if things go well. Yes, it will be more expensive after a startup has hit milestones, but it will be worth it because it will be less risky. 

We should also remember one of the key lessons from the brief history of venture capital discussed in the first chapter: sharing equity helps align interests. VCs know how hard it is to grow a company. Further, we know the benefits of motivating founders through shared ownership. Growing a startup into a billion-dollar company requires an immense amount of work. VCs have to balance a desire to own as much 

of the equity as possible with the need to keep the founders motivated to work an incredible number of hours to achieve massive growth. 

## Option Pool Reset 

The biggest potential losers in the down round are the founders and other common shareholders, who have little to no ability to participate in the Series B and no anti-dilution protections. It can be a sincere challenge to reorganize the cap table to make sure there is enough equity to incentivize management. 

Enter options pools again. With each round of financing, the option pool may get reset, meaning new shares can be created and allocated to the option pool. This can be a particularly useful tool if there are cofounders on the cap table who are no longer contributing to the startup. They will be diluted with no opportunity to earn new options. 

Current and future management, who will be responsible for our success, will get new options. It may not make up for all of the dilution 

suffered from a down round, but it could be enough to keep the wheels from coming off. 

For example, according to Re/Code Magazine, Jawbone had a down round in January of 2016, with its valuation dropping by half from $3B to $1.5B. Re/Code called it “a restructuring of the cap table, diluting investors not in the new round.” to participating In an attempt hang onto talent, “a larger pool of equity for employees has apparently eliminated losses in value of their shares.” [23] 

## Grow Summary 

We learned in this chapter that a venture capitalist’s job does not end with finding and investing in high-potential startups. Many VCs’ favorite part of the job is rolling up their sleeves to help startups succeed. How a VC firm manages its portfolio can differentiate it from other firms. 

We can help our portfolio companies in many ways, beginning by plugging them into our vast networks of key players in the industry. We have relationships with potential strategic partners, customers, acquirers, advisors and others. 

However, the most tangible contribution we make to the growth of our portfolio is our participation in future rounds of funding. When VCs negotiate a Series A, we are already thinking about the Series B, C…all the way to exit. We reserve 2-3X of our Series A investment to participate in follow-on rounds in an attempt to thwart dilution and maintain pro rata. 

**==> picture [202 x 238] intentionally omitted <==**

## Chapter Questions 

1. Why do VCs plan on several rounds of investing before an exit? 

2. What happens to valuations as milestones are hit or missed? 

3. How are rounds of investing related to milestones? 

4. What can happen when a startup misses key milestones? 

5. In one word, explain what is terrible about a down round? 

6. Why might a founder have mixed feelings about how much money to raise? 

7. Why might a venture capitalist be conflicted about how much money to invest? 

8. How do Series A investors mitigate dilution? 

9. How is tranching not reflecting underlying economic realities? 

10. How is a VC fund’s portfolio like a record company’s roster in terms of developing and investing in talent? Explain the “bullpen” metaphor. 

11. If a 2 on 4 round is followed by a 3 on 4 round, is the second round an up, down or flat round? 

## Case Study: VC Razor Fund II 

Jeff and Mark began testing the waters for their second fund, VC Razor II, at the end of the third year of VC RAZOR I, right as they were exploring joining a Series A lead by another VC firm. Timing was good. LPs seemed eager to put more money into this asset class, and their LPs appreciated that VC RAZOR I was performing 

surprisingly close to its original strategy. It looked like a winning formula. True, there were no exits and Mark were able to demonstrate yet, but Jeff value creation through milestones that the startups had hit, including the two Series A rounds. 

The LPs were the particularly happy about syndicate partners Jeff and Mark were working with, some very well-known VC firms with great track records. This enthusiasm for syndicates helped Jeff and Mark quickly pull the trigger and join the Series A they had been deliberating. 

Because of the hot fundraising environment, Jeff and Mark decided to move quickly on fund II. To keep it simple, they pitched a profile very similar to VC Razor I, with a relatively slight increase in size from $50M to $60M. This allowed them to close on VC Razor II in weeks rather than months, with most of their LPs re-upping. 

Part of the reason they kept the fund small was because their current strategy seemed to be working, getting in at the seed stage and helping early companies position for a Series A. They liked that sweet spot and did not want to increase the amount of funding per company. 

Another reason to the fund small was keep that they had not found an individual that they might bring in as a third partner on VC Razor II. They were not actively searching, but it was always in the back of their minds as a potential growth strategy for the firm. If they didn’t want to invest more need to invest in more per startup, they’d startups. To invest in more startups, they’d need help. 

The two of them were already nearing their limit in terms of how many portfolio companies they could manage. Thanks to the influx of 

management fees for the new fund (2% of $60M is $1.2M/year), they could afford to invest in infrastructure and get some help, including hiring an analyst and an administrative assistant. The new help would increase their bandwidth so that they would be able to handle the increased workload of a second fund on top of the first fund. 

Their fund strategy was to focus on early stage. They still had a lot of work to do to get six of their VC Razor I portfolio companies positioned for a Series A. After that, the strategy was not to lead future rounds and to focus their on energies new investments in VC Razor II. 

TO PONDER: What can you imagine Fund III might look like? Should Jeff and Mark start looking for another partner with more expertise in later-stage investing? 

## � VC Math: Growing 

Venture-backed startups grow by raising venture capital and using that funding to hit mile stones. Each infusion of capital comes at a price: the addition of new shareholders who will be there to further split the pie upon exit. Dilution becomes the main mathematical principle to understand when thinking about growth. 

Every time a round of financing occurs, all earlier shareholders are diluted by the amount of ownership purchased by the new investors: 

New VCs’ Dilution = % = Ownership 

**==> picture [103 x 75] intentionally omitted <==**

Over a series of rounds, dilution is cumulative. When new equity rounds are added to the pie, the pie grows, but the percentages get smaller. 

As a side note, each round of equity can have different preferred rights (liquidation preference, dividends, etc.). Those rights are “stacked” on top of one another with the latest investors getting priority. 

**==> picture [418 x 174] intentionally omitted <==**

For simplicity, let’s look at an example in which each round of equity is exactly doubling the number of pre-existing shares. This is not realistic because it would cause too much dilution, 50% per round, but this simplified example will illustrate the cumulative effects of dilution across several rounds. 

Our Series A is a “2 on 2,” meaning a $2M investment on a $2M pre-money valuation. The result is a $4M post-money valuation, with new investors receiving 50% of the equity and the founders diluted by 50%. 

The Series B again dilutes by 50% with a $6M investment on a $6M pre. Series B investors receive 50% owner ship, and previous shareholders are diluted by 50%, taking founders and Series A shareholders to 25% each. The pattern repeats one last time, with Series C investors taking 50% with a $20M investment on a $20M pre. 

**==> picture [418 x 171] intentionally omitted <==**

Not including special rights, such as liquidity preferences and dividends, the proceeds at exit will be split according to the equity split after the last round of financing, the C round in the example above. 

If this were a unicorn exit at exactly $1B, the dilution suffered by the Series A investors would amount to a loss of $375M, having dropped from 50% to 12.5% (37.5% *$1B=$375M). 

Given our hit-driven model, in which we are targeting only a few home runs in our portfolio, we need to receive as much as possible from any hits we may have. When you’re hunting unicorns, you will fight like anything to avoid dilution. 

The above example is conveniently set at 50% dilution per round. As we look at more realistic examples, we’ll likely need a calculator, with a caveat. Note that we are projecting future rounds. It doesn’t make sense to carry too many decimal points when we are taking such liberties with guesswork about the future. VCs do a lot of rounding when projecting, but they also know when and how to account for every penny when reporting to LPs for example. Again, these are projections. Rounding is okay, and in fact, even encouraged because it helps us juggle numerous scenarios in our heads. Back to the math. 

To determine a previous shareholders stake after the investment, we simply remove the amount diluted: 

New Diluted Ownership = Old Ownership x (1 – dilution) 

Below is a slightly more realistic (and complicated) example, in which dilution is occurring to varying degrees with each round. In this scenario, Series A receives 2/5 ownership, or 40%, diluting founders from 100% to 60%. Series B receives 5/15, or 33%. Series C is 10/40, or 25%. 

For each constituent in each round, we have to go one at a time and 

calculate new ownership. After the Series B dilution of 33% we have: 

- Series B Investors: 33% (preferred) 

- Series A: 40% * (1 - 33%) = 27% (preferred) Founders: 60% * (1 - 33%) = 40% (common) 

Similarly, after the C round we have: 

- Series C: 25% 

- Series B: 33% * 75% = 25% Series A: 27% * 75% = 20% Founders: 40% * 75% = 30% 

These numbers would hold through the exit if there are no more rounds after the Series C. 

**==> picture [418 x 177] intentionally omitted <==**

If this startup was acquired for $200M, the proceeds would be split as follows (ignoring preferences): 

- Series C: 25% of $200M = $50M 

- Series B: 25% of $200M = $50M Series A: 20% of $200M = $40M Founders: 30% of $200M = $60M 

Note that all of these rounds are UP rounds, with the pre-money of one round higher than the post-money of the previous round. 

- A post to B pre: $5M to $10M = 2X 

- B post to C pre: $15M to $40M = 2.7X From C post to exit: $40M to $200M = 5X Total appreciation from A to exit: 27X 

**==> picture [417 x 178] intentionally omitted <==**

However, what if there were a blip in the road, and the B round ended up being a down round? How would that change dilution? I want to reiterate that VCs would never be projecting a down round, but let’s walk through an example to show the effects of down rounds. In this case, we’ll make the B round a down round of 4 on 4. 

**==> picture [140 x 91] intentionally omitted <==**

In this new scenario, we still have the 2 on 3 Series A, resulting in 40% ownership. Series A investors will then be diluted 50% in the 4 on 4 B 

## round: 

- Series B Investors: 4/8 = 50% 

- Series A: 40% * (1 - 50%) = 20% Founders: 60% * (1 – 50%) = 30% 

We’ll keep the Series C the same, including a pre-money valuation of $30M, meaning this startup had a remarkable turnaround and increased in value nearly 4X from the B to the C. 

- Series C: 25% 

- Series B: 50% * 75% = 37.5% Series A: 20% * 75% = 15% Founders: 30% * 75% = 22.5% 

**==> picture [418 x 172] intentionally omitted <==**

- With a $200M exit, the resulting distribution would be: Series C: 25% of $200M = $50M Series B: 37.5% of $200M = $75M Series A: 15% of $200M = $30M Founders: 22.5% of $200M = $45M 

Since it was the B round that was the killer, with 50% dilution, the founders and Series A investors took a big hit, losing a cumulative $25M to Series B investors as compared to the earlier UP round example. 

**==> picture [417 x 171] intentionally omitted <==**

The last thing I want to note is that we know that the Series A shareholders will typically be reserving 2-3X capital (dry powder) to participate in follow-on rounds with the goal of maintaining a pro rata stake in this startup. In this case, Series A investors would reserve up to an extra $6M (3 times the $2M A round) to invest across the next two rounds. In the graphic below, A[B] and A[C] represent the Series A investors’ participation in later rounds to maintain pro rata ownership. 

**==> picture [418 x 178] intentionally omitted <==**

We’ll dive into this math when we discuss Fund Fit in Part II.
