Principle User is Payer 

Another issue to consider in our revenue model is the risk associated with creating value for customers who do not make purchasing decisions. For example, if a startup is targeting me, a professor, they’d likely have to sell through my institution, a university. Creating value for a professor may not equate to creating value for an institution. Even though I may desire to 

purchase the product, I likely have to wait for the university to make that decision. 

Similarly, targeting teenagers as consumers can be a challenge for items that their parents will be paying for. Ideally, we hope to have your users in a position to make the purchasing decisions. Back to me as an example, the startup might price its offerings within an amount that many professors can cover with their discretionary spending accounts. Then the end user is also the payer. 

Revenue Model Summary 

Of course, not every startup even needs a price or a revenue model. If we have near zero incremental costs, we may be able to go years without knowing for certain how we will harvest the value we are creating. 

We also do not necessarily care about revenue, pricing or margins if we have an exit strategy in which an acquirer will be purchasing our users and/or technology. They may want our users so that they can sell them their technology, or they may want our technology so that they’ll be differentiated in their market. In either case, our pricing strategy may not have relevance in their value network. 

If we are trying to build a company to go public, we will very much care about revenues and margins. We will need to reach significant levels of income, nearing $100M a year, with a consistent upwards trajectory, to be a candidate for an IPO. 

Lastly, if we see tough times ahead, we should be thinking about a revenue model as a source for the potential funding, as equity markets may not be available to us. If for some reason we think an acquisition is going to take a very long time or that public markets may be hitting a down cycle, we may need some income just to survive. 

## Business Model Summary 

Generally, you don’t hear much of the phrase business model until the markets are in a down cycle, and everyone all of a sudden has a come-toJesus moment to start focusing on traditional business fundamentals. However, it is not really our job as VCs to build sustainable businesses unless it is required for the best exit. It is our job to return capital to our investors via our unique hit-driven equity-growth investment thesis. 

Hence, as VCs, we have slightly different fundamentals than traditional investors. Our focus is on scalability in high margin businesses with a clear path to exit. A revenue model may have little significance unless it is necessary to get to exit. 

**==> picture [222 x 237] intentionally omitted <==**

## Chapter Questions 

1. Why are growth and profitability negatively correlated? 

2. Is profitability in an early stage startup a primary concern of VCs? Why or why not? 

3. What are two different approaches to thinking about LTV? 

4. What factors go into calculating CAC? 

6. Create a service vs. product scorecard and rate the following types of businesses: sharing economy, social network, e-commerce, tech-enabled on-demand services, software-as-a-service. 

7. In what situations is a revenue model irrelevant? Why? 

8. Why are recurring revenues attractive? 

9. What are some factors that make services businesses harder to scale? 

10. Thinking like a VC, would you consider Facebook a service or a product? 

11. Do you think Uber’s business model is disrupting the taxi industry? Why or why not? 

Due Diligence Advice for Students: Business Model Focus on identifying and removing barriers to scale, both current and future. Use numbers when appropriate but focus on the narrative. Do not count on founders to understand our exitdriven strategy, meaning be strategic and assertive about the kind of growth-oriented business model a VC fund needs to see (whether or not the founders are pitching that model). Reconnect with product/market fit, a major determiner of customer adoption, as well as competitive threats. 

**==> picture [458 x 234] intentionally omitted <==**

## C. FUND FIT 

We’ve explored the market and industry aspects of the value network. Now it is time to look at the mechanics of how an investment in a startup would fit into our fund. This is what VCs are referring to with the question, “Can we make the math work?” 

**==> picture [328 x 153] intentionally omitted <==**

In Product/Market Fit, we addressed the question of value creation, or more generically, does the idea work? In Team/Industry Fit, we looked at the feasibility of creating the organization through which the idea could grow. The third area of inquiry is one of happiness, really: is it worth our investment? Can we achieve a vision for success? 

**==> picture [278 x 86] intentionally omitted <==**

Any future entrepreneur reading this should be free to include any metric here: financial, social, personal…whatever might make you happy. However, VCs have only one metric for happiness: financial return. It is our only legal definition of success (our personal lives be damned!). We have the fiduciary duty on behalf of our LPs to maximize returns. Our “happiness” is predicated on the math working. 

VC Razor is a due diligence framework, which means we are looking at a startup as an investment opportunity, from today forward. Due diligence has two aspects: one is corroborating that the startup can actually do what the founders had asserted they can do. This is akin to an inspection during a house purchase, aimed to make sure the house is as represented. 

The second aspect of due diligence is predicting the future. What is the potential of a startup? A big part of that analysis is a shared vision between the founders and the investors, including a future of more rounds of financing and a liquidity event (exit). 

Of course, we don’t know for sure how things will go, but based on our experience as investors in other startups, there are some familiar patterns. I want to stress that we don’t necessarily have a crystal-clear vision of the future. However, we must be able to envision a positive outcome, even if it is in very broad strokes. 

The Fund Fit section is an exercise in taking our earlier knowledge about the context of the VC Job Cycle (fundraise, invest, grow and exit) and superimposing it on a present-day investment opportunity. We want to explore how the Job Cycle influences our investment decisions. 

A quick review, recall that VCs are professional money managers investing from a fund made up of commitments from limited partners, LPs. Our fund has a specific size and a specific strategy that will determine the size of checks we write across the portfolio. For example, we might have a $200M fund from which we invest an average of $10-15M into each startup. 

We also plan to invest across several rounds in each startup. Our $1015M per startup might start with an initial investment of $3-5M with 2-3X reserved as dry powder for follow-on rounds. 

**==> picture [410 x 194] intentionally omitted <==**

We are reserving dry powder to participate in future rounds to minimize the effects of dilution. With our hit-driven investment thesis, we don’t expect all our startups to succeed. The few that make it big are going to drive the performance of our fund. If we are lucky/talented enough to have a unicorn exit at exactly $1B in our fund, for example, dilution of 10% would result in the loss of proceeds of $100M. Hence, we want to participate as much as possible in our winners. 

**==> picture [458 x 227] intentionally omitted <==**

Something to keep in mind as we explore Fund Fit is that these chapters are even more future-focused than other sections of the book. This 

aspect of due diligence has less to do with validating the founders’ assertions and is all about assessing the potential of the startup. 

In all our due diligence efforts, we’ve had an eye to the future. In product/market fit, we wanted to understand the fit for the current target market as well as future segments. On the team, we assessed today’s leadership and also considered who we might bring on board. When we looked at competition, we looked at today’s threats as well as how competitors may react tomorrow. 

However, Fund Fit is all about the future, and not just any future. It is a specific new future for a startup that is made possible by our investment. This is a future that we will help create, and in fact, we often have more experience with this type of future than our founders do. While the founders know their startup better than anyone on earth, we are the experts on how startups grow and exit. When considering investing in a startup, venture capitalists must have a vision for how to make this happen with this startup, becoming both strategist and prognosticator. 

This is where the numbers and the story come together. Yes, the numbers must work, but equally true, the story has to work. The numbers are but an abstraction of a shared vision for success. We need to stop for a moment to chew on both of these words, “shared” and “vision.” 

VCs need to be visionaries. We can’t expect our founders to carry the entire load of creating a new future with our money. It’s a collaboration. Founders must design the detailed roadmap and be engaged in day-to-day operations, creating the culture in which the startup can grow. However, our comes with conditions and further considerations. The cash we money invest in a startup is part of a bigger picture, one that includes a portfolio and limited partners. 

This section, Fund Fit, is about merging our fund’s needs with each founder’s vision for success. Founders are going to have to agree with our strategies to accept our investments. We necessarily must Impose the needs of a structure created in part thanks to the Traitorous 

Eight back in Part 1. Without this vehicle called a venture capital firm, there would be no funding for startups. 

Given the level of uncertainty in predicting the future, different VCs will have different approaches and will often disagree. This level of analysis does not lend itself to right and wrong answers. More relevant are compelling narratives, weaving numbers and story into a vision for the future. We will never know what was right or wrong. We will know, with the help of time, if the path chosen worked or not, but we’ll never know what might have worked. 

Our shared vision for the future has several elements. One is the money in: how our investment will be spent by the startup, called the use of funds. We want a roadmap of value-creating milestones to be achieved with our dollars. Further, we’ll consider future funding rounds and the milestones they will fund. 

Our vision also includes the money out: an exit strategy. If we’ll be creating a larger, stable company, perhaps we can take it public. More often we will make a plan to be acquired. We want to identify potential acquirers and what they are looking for. 

Lastly, we must make the math work. The money in and money out must fit our hit-driven investment thesis and move the needle on our fund size. 

- In summary, we’ll be investigating three elements of Fund Fit: The story about the number: money in. GROWTH MILESTONES The story about the number: money out. EXIT STRATEGY Make the math work. RETURN ANALYSIS 

**==> picture [458 x 85] intentionally omitted <==**

## 7. Growth Milestones 

The Story about the Number: Money In. 

## Milestones and Risk 

Perhaps the most important strategic concept for maximizing financial returns in a venture capital investment is milestones. Any time a milestone is hit, the underlying valuation of the startup increases, just as a public company’s stock price rises when the company hits its numbers. Identifying, funding and achieving milestones are imperative for creating value in a venture. 

Each milestone is tied to one or more key risks. Hitting a milestone results in demonstratively lower risk, which translates into higher valuation. Though there are some shared characteristics across all startups, every industry has its own set of inherent risks. Biotech companies, for example, will have vastly different milestones than software startups. 

**==> picture [159 x 310] intentionally omitted <==**

The challenge for venture capitalists is to identify key risks in a startup and determine whether there are milestones the startup could achieve to mitigate those risks. Then we must make some estimations about the feasibility of funding activities required to hit the milestones. 

For example, a software startup may have beta testing, new feature releases and/or strategic partnerships to forge. Biotech companies have regulatory and approval processes. Hardware companies build prototypes and release products. Young founding teams need to recruit seasoned executives; experienced founders may need to bring in young, energetic talent. Most startups will benefit from many of the achievements listed, and it will be the job of the venture capitalists to figure out which of the activities should be funded with a round of venture financing. 

## Use of Funds 

Most startup pitches will include a “use of funds” section, which describes how the founders intend to spend the cash they are raising. Unfortunately, their explanation is often tactical rather than strategic. That is, founders often share their hiring plan or marketing spend. What they often forget to share is the outcome anticipated by those expenditures, the milestones they are planning to hit. VCs might ask, “Where does that get us?” That can be translated into, “What milestones will we achieve with this investment?” 

As we discussed in the VC Job Grow chapter, VCs pre-identify a series of milestones and estimate the amount of funding that will be needed for each. The amount being raised in a round of financing should only be as much as is necessary to hit specific milestones. The goal is to maximize the value of their equity from round to round. 

Breaking up the funding needed into a series of rounds separated by milestones is advantageous for VCs and for founders. VCs reduce capital risk by deploying less capital up front and reserving dry powder for followon rounds. If milestones are missed in a significant way, a VC firm may choose not to participate in later rounds, freeing up the reserved capital to invest in other opportunities. 

Founders also benefit from the multi-round pattern by lowering dilution and thus retaining more equity. This is a result of raising only the amount needed to get to the next milestone. After hitting the milestone, the valuation increases, and future funding is not as dilutive. If founders had to raise more capital at the lower valuation, they would lose a higher percentage of their startup. 

## Figure: Typical example of multiple rounds of funding. 

**==> picture [376 x 129] intentionally omitted <==**

Most people, including entrepreneurs, tend to think on a cash basis. We see money coming in and money going out, and we calculate that the difference is our profit (or loss). But it turns out that in a startup, where there is little or no income to balance out expenses, this pursuit of profitability can be a distraction from the real goal, which is creating the most value. Naïve entrepreneurs are racing to profitability or break-even rather than pursuing maximum growth potential. 

## Building Equity 

Cash is a liquid asset. That means we can spend it easily and quickly. You might say fluidly. We can go to the store with cash and exchange it for milk. Having a lot of cash is a type of wealth that we can all understand. We have money and we can buy what we want. 

However, individuals may be wealthy and still have no cash. As an example, let’s say we are poor and our grandparents leave us a beach house worth a million dollars. All of a sudden, we are much richer. We have a very big asset, but it is illiquid. We cannot take our house to the store to buy bread. Our wealth is tied up in equity in the house. Or stated differently, most of our net worth is equity, not cash. This creates a challenge, as we need cash to get by. 

Similarly, startups are often equity rich and cash poor. Much of the value we can create with a venture capital investment will yield increases in equity, not cash. As we saw in the previous chapter, we may not even be building the capability to generate cash, depending on our exit strategy. For example, we might be close to developing a cure for cancer, which everyone would agree holds a lot of value as an illiquid asset. However, the startup 

could still be cash poor, and in fact has the serious risk of running out of cash before hitting the next milestone. 

Another example, let’s say we have two successful restaurants. If we open a third restaurant this year, the startup expenses we incur may overwhelm our profits (cash) from our other two restaurants. On a short-term cash basis, we appear to be unprofitable, and we have negatively impacted any break-even analysis. However, in the longterm, we are creating more value in our venture because we are growing our overall business and thus increasing the value of our equity. 

## Hit Movie Example 

**==> picture [106 x 269] intentionally omitted <==**

The process of a producing a blockbuster movie is a good case study of how milestones create equity value. It starts when someone has a good story idea (which creates some value). Then that gets put on paper as a treatment (a little more value). Then an award-winning writer is found to write a screenplay (even more value). Spielberg reads the 

script and gets on board to direct (a lot more value). Clooney likes what he sees and signs up to be the lead (starting to sound like a hit!). 

## Figure: Increasing equity value in a film project. 

This movie concept has hit numerous value-creating milestones, and we haven’t even started filming, not to mention editing, scoring, marketing, etc. During each step along the way, risks are being mitigated and value is being created, even though no income has yet been derived from the movie. 

The last point is an important one and deserves further investigation. Notice that value is being created long before there are any customers or revenues. How is this possible? The answer is the difference between cash and equity. Understanding that difference is often the key to maximizing 

## Airbnb Example 

**==> picture [263 x 137] intentionally omitted <==**

In Part I, we looked at follow-on rounds for a few unicorns. Let’s dig a little deeper into one of them, Airbnb, to see how the milestones are tied to funding. The figure below shows the Series A, B and C of Airbnb. There have been several subsequent rounds, but we’ll focus on the early stage funding. 

Figure: Example follow-on rounds for three unicorns. 

In 2008, after failing to raise $150,000 from VCs or angels, the founders of AirBedandBreakfast, as it was originally named, famously got scrappy and sold cereal at the presidential political party conventions to raise some seed money. That scrappiness helped get them into Y- Combinator in January of ’09. 

The “guys” made a big impression on Y-Combinator co-founder Paul Graham, who went to bat to help them get funding. “We had big doubts about this idea, but they vanished on meeting the guys,” Paul said in an email to VC Fred Wilson. Fred passed on (declined) the investment and went on to regret it.[43] The company, renamed Airbnb, secured a $600,000 seed round from Sequoia and a couple of others in April of 2009. 

One lesson for other young entrepre neurs: if you demonstrate a fanatical drive to succeed in your startup, a lot of investor doubt (but not all) can be erased. It seems that in the early days, investors were much more 

impressed with the team’s drive and problem-solving 

**==> picture [131 x 90] intentionally omitted <==**

creativity in the face of scarce resources (selling cereal to raise $30,000), not so much with the idea (dudes on couches?). 

After the seed round, Airbnb reportedly was only making a couple hundred dollars a week. One major obstacle that the team identified was the poor quality of photographs for listings. They used part of their funding to overcome this risk, offering free professional photography for individuals who wanted to list their property. It worked and revenues took off.[44] 

Before closing the A round about 18 months later, Airbnb had hit milestones of 700,000 nights booked in 8,000 cities in 166 countries. Estimating an average of $100 per booking, that’s $70M in transactions, of which Airbnb takes a flat 10%, or around $7M in revenue. 

The Series A was reportedly a $7.2M investment on a pre-money of $100M. This is worth chewing on: how could the company have a valuation that was 15 times higher than all the money it had ever earned? The double answer is growth trajectory and market size. On trajectory, the team had implemented improvements, such as professional photography, that had 

dramatically increased the growth curve. They had removed barriers to scale, and in so doing had proven that with very little money ($620,000 so far), they could create a lot of value. 

As importantly, there was still plenty of room to grow, if you defined the opportunity as part of the much larger hospitality industry. It was at this point that my aforementioned VC friend passed on the investment (see p. 167), believing it was still “dudes on couches.” The Series A investors believed in the bigger market. 

Note that part of the calculation for a VC considering joining the A round is price. The Series A is priced at a very impressive $100M. Usually Series A is more in the $10-30M range, and even lower if you are not in California. A VC would need that the team was onto to strongly believe something big to think there was the potential for a 10X return with a current valuation of $100M. In fact, it would have to become a unicorn, as the definition of a unicorn is a $1B valuation (10 x $100M =  $1B). 

It took another seven months for it to become a unicorn. The only Series B was an investment of $112M on a pre-money valuation of $1.2B. This release us an idea of the metrics press gives they were using to track their success: 

- “Airbnb has experienced explosive growth over the past year, doubling the benchmark of 1 million nights booked in just four months. The company now has over 2 million nights booked, receives over 30 million page views per month and has seen the number of Airbnb Social Connections triple to 54 million since launching the feature in May. As the company poises itself to meet the growing demand in international markets like Germany, the United Kingdom, France and Brazil, this investment will help position [45] 

- Airbnb as the leader in the broader vacation rental market in 2012.” 

Guess who got on board for the Series B? Yes, my VC friend finally came around and concluded this was bigger than “dudes on couches.” This is a classic example of why VCs split investments into rounds, each specifically designed to give enough capital to hit the next milestone, thereby decreasing risk and attracting more capital (at a higher price). 

This Series B came relatively quickly, just seven months after the A round. We can assume that the milestones expected by the Series A 

investors were hit so early, thereby “de-risking the deal,” so that investors were eager to “put more money to work” to take advantage of and continue to fuel the remarkable growth. Remember our earlier lesson about working the portfolio like a record label does. When a label has an artist getting some traction, next thing you know that artist is everywhere, performing on late TV their in new tour dates night shows, placing songs movies, booking in larger venues. All of those things represent significant investment of resources by the label. 

Similarly, the VCs in this case moved very quickly to increase the magnitude of the opportunity. They knew they had a hit on their hands. Now it was time to shoot for a mega-hit. The $112M B round on the heels of the $7.2M Series A was intended to fuel growth to take this unicorn (already $1B valuation for the Series B) to decacorn status ($10B valuation). 

My friend’s firm had to pay up for the Series B, with a pre-money valuation over 10 times higher than that A round, but new investors received the benefit of milestones that had been hit, significantly lowering the risk. In the table below, I’ve added two more rounds, the C and D. Take a look at those later rounds and think about what the numbers tell us. 

Table: Detail about Airbnb rounds of funding through Series D. 

|Date<br>Jan.<br>2009<br>Apr.<br>2009<br>Nov.<br>2010 <br>Jul.<br>2011 <br>Oct.<br>2012 <br>Apr.<br>2014|Round<br>Seed<br>Seed<br> Series A<br> Series B<br> Series C<br> Series D|Amount<br>$20K<br>$600K<br>$7.2M<br>$112M<br>$200M<br>$475M|Pre-<br>Money<br>~$300K<br>$1.5M<br>$100M<br>$1.2B<br>$2.3B<br>$9.5B|Up Rounds<br>-<br>0.32  1.5 =<br>5X<br>2.1  100 =<br>50X<br>107<br>1.2B<br>= 11X<br>1.3B  2.3B<br>= 2X<br>2.5B  9.5B<br>= 4X|
|---|---|---|---|---|



The C round came 15 months after the B round and doubled both the investment and the valuation. This is a significant deceleration of value growth as compared to the 10X uptick in 7 months from A to B. However, it is still an impressive increase in valuation of $1B. Value is being created. Milestones are being hit, but the curve has flattened a bit, which is normal as a startup matures. Later rounds generally have a lower X multiple with a higher valuation increase in terms of net dollars. 

The D round ticks back up impressively, with the valuation up nearly 4X, representing an increase in valuation of $7.7B. What does this tell us about milestones? Something big happened. Coming into April of 2014, Airbnb must have done something that was perceived by investors to have significantly lowered risk. 

I could not find any press releases specifically explaining the round, but my guess is that the big valuation bump was related to regulatory concerns. Airbnb had been operating illegally in many markets. There was a risk that the enforcement of those laws or the enactment of new ones might significantly curtail Airbnb’s growth. 

According to Wired magazine, “That changed around 2014, when Airbnb began striking deals with officials in select cities to collect and deliver taxes from its hosts.”[46] My guess is that the D investors had a lot of optimism based on a milestone related to local laws. The regulatory filing for the Series D says that funds were to be used in part “to navigate the regulatory landscape.” 

Just as hiring professional photographers had helped overcome the obstacle of unattractive photos, signing early agreements with cities perhaps mitigated regulatory concerns, one of the last barriers to growth for Airbnb. The milestone for the former was likely a goal of increased performance for properties that had professional photographs. This might have been, “We want to see a 3X increase in conversion from site visitors on select properties.” 

For the regulatory risk, the milestone have included in select cities may achieving agreements and starting negotiations in others. Coming up with the specific milestone(s) that will convince future investors that risk is diminished is again an endeavor of intuition and analysis, requiring a mix of narrative and data. 

We can look back over all of Airbnb rounds of investment and conclude that significant milestones were being hit with each. From Seed to Series A, Airbnb’s valuation went up 50X, representing nearly $100M in value creation. The next round, from A to B, the milestones that were achieved increased the valuation by $1B. As the real dollar numbers get higher, the multiple increase becomes impossible to maintain. However, immense value is still being created, to the tune of nearly $8B between the C and D rounds. 

I’ve chosen not to take this through the E and F rounds of Airbnb funding because the later rounds really look more like private equity than venture capital. The company went on to raise a Series E of $1.5B, debt of $1B and most recently a Series F of $1B. The valuation after the F round was reportedly $31B. It was while raising the F round that Airbnb announced it had turned profitable, which can be an important milestone for a company planning to go public. 

## Negotiated and Time Limited 

When working with VC numbers, it is important to remember that many of the variables are both negotiated and time limited. What is the value of hitting a milestone? How much is needed to hit the next milestone? Both the pre-money valuation (which includes the value previously hit milestones) and the amount being invested (which is tied to future milestones) are moving targets. 

Selling a home is a good comparison—another transaction involving an illiquid asset. When you put your house up for sale, what is it worth? It is hard to say. There are a number of factors that can affect its valuation: 

typical price per square foot in your neighborhood, sales prices of other comparable homes, the value of your recent renovations, trends in your market, a crash in the world economy, etc. 

VCs would value a house by looking at the exit and the amount of funding required to hit the milestones needed to get to that exit. Then they would back into the current price necessary to achieve a certain return. 

**==> picture [181 x 160] intentionally omitted <==**

The reality is that nobody knows the price until you sell it. Then you have a price, for a moment, equal to exactly what someone was willing to pay for it. That number is only good for that moment. Just after the house is sold, as after a round of VC investing, circum stances can change quickly, and the valuation will change accordingly. 

Figure: effects on home values directly after the house is sold 

That night, there could be a nearby natural disaster. The following weekend there could be a feature in the New York Times about how great the neighbor hood is. The house next door might sell for a surprisingly high or low amount. A nearby big company with high paying jobs might be closing (or opening). 

Any of these events will affect the value of the underlying illiquid asset, but we can only guess as to how much. There will not be another true price until we choose to put the house on the market, go through the process of showing it, negotiate a new price and ultimately consummate the transaction. Only then will we know the true valuation, and once again, only for a moment. Then the cycle continues. 

**==> picture [202 x 174] intentionally omitted <==**

The same happens with startups. The valuation was negotiated under a specific set of circumstances, and all parties know (and are planning on the fact) that things will change. A product will launch. A big new customer will sign. A beta will flop. The top engineer will leave. The stock market will go up. Then down. 

## Figure: influences on valuation directly after a round of funding. 

All of these things, some internal, some external, will conceptually affect the valuation, but we will not know exactly what the new valuation is until there is another transaction, when we will sit down across the table again and negotiate a new price for another round of funding. 

A quick insertion of mechanics: if this were a public company, we would refer to valuation changes as the share price fluctuating. It is the same thing with a startup, share prices are going up and down with valuations. The difference is in liquidity and transparency. Public stocks are traded every minute, meaning their prices are publicly negotiated all day long. A startup’s share price will only be negotiated in the next equity round or in a liquidity event (exit), and the information will not be public unless the parties choose to disclose the details in a press release. 

The startup market is much less liquid and less transparent than homes. The transactions happen much less frequently, and when they happen, the details are often undisclosed. VCs have an informational advantage because through our networks we know the particulars of many private deals. 

## Dilution and Participation 

Much of the discussion above is about narrative, the story of milestones. It’s time to return to VC math to dig into some of the numbers behind the story. The key concept here is dilution. We manage this risk by participating in future rounds. 

(In case you’d like to review, we are going to build on the math we started in VC Math Growing, p. 114.) 

Let’s start easy, with a Series A investment of $3M on a $5M premoney valuation. The startup has two founders, each of whom owned 50% prior to the round. What percent ownership would each founder have after the A round? 

- Step 1: determine the amount of dilution: 3/8 = 37.5% 

- Step 2: calculate new ownership. Each founder started with 50%. Multiply that by (1 - 37.5%) to get: 

**==> picture [79 x 176] intentionally omitted <==**

50% x (1-37.5%) = 50% * 62.5% = 31.25% 

A simplified cap chart might look like this: Series A Investors: 37.5% (preferred) Founder 1: 31% (common) Founder 2: 31% (common) 

Because VC shares are preferred, they are often represented as being on top. As new preferred shares are added, their rights are “stacked” on top. Each round could have different rights depending on the negotiations. 

**==> picture [77 x 164] intentionally omitted <==**

For an early stage startup with a pre-money valuation of $5M, it is VERY likely that the VCs will want to create an option pool. Such a young startup has a lot of growing to do, which means we will need to hire key talent. Let’s put in a 10% option pool to attract that talent. Luckily for us the investors (and sad for the founders), the math is very easy because the option pool always comes out of the founders’ side. In this case, each founder will then lose 5% to the new options. 

Let’s take this to a B round with a $12M investment on a $24M pre-money valuation. The B investors will have 12/36 = 33.3% ownership after the round. A new cap chart would look like this: 

**==> picture [154 x 196] intentionally omitted <==**

Series B: 12/36 = 33% Series A: 37.5% * (1 - 33%) = 25% Options: 10% * (1 - 33%) = 6.7% Founder 2: 26% * (1 - 33%) = 17% Founder 1: 26% * (1 - 33%) = 17% 

Our as Series A investors is that we lost a third of our stake. problem just We dropped from 37.5% to 25%. If this startup were to exit after the B round at a rather modest $100M, we would stand to lose $12.5M. 

I call the $100M rather modest because the post-money of the Series B is $36M. A $100M exit would represent a healthy, but not unicorn-like, 3X value jump. With this modest exit, we still lost $12.5M, which is quadruple our original investment of $4M. If the startup were to go all the way to a $1B unicorn exit, the dilution would cost us $125M. 

**==> picture [91 x 302] intentionally omitted <==**

We avoid this dilution by participating in the B round. To hold pro rata exactly, we participate in the next round by taking the exact percentage of that round that we already own. In this case, it is 37.5%. Multiply 37.5% * $12M to get our piece of the B round: $4.5M (noted as A/B below). Syndicate partners would take the rest of the B round, $7.5M. 

Series B only: 7.5 / 36 = 21% Series A/B: 4.5 / 36 = 12.5% Series A: 37.5% * .67 = 25% 

Options: 10% * .67 = 6.7% Founder 2: 26% * .67 = 17% Founder 1: 26% * .67 = 17% 

When we add our diluted A stake at 25% to our new 12.5% in the B round, we are back to a pro rata 37.5%. 

**==> picture [127 x 91] intentionally omitted <==**

Let’s look at a different scenario, this one a Series B investment that starts with one founder at 40%, another founder at 30%, an option pool of 10% and Series A investors at 20%. 

**==> picture [121 x 108] intentionally omitted <==**

If the Series B is a 20 on 60 round ($20M investment on 

a $60M pre-money), each party will be diluted by 25% (20/80): 

Series B Investors: 25% Series A Investors: 20% * .75 = 15% Options: 10% * .75 = 7.5% Founder 2: 30% * .75 = 22.5% Founder 1: 40% * .75 = 30% 

Once again, for the Series A investors to maintain pro rata at 20%, we will need to take that same percentage of the Series B. Since the B round is $20M, we’ll need to take $4M of it (20%*20). That $4M will be worth 4/80 = 5% (noted as A/B in the table). Our syndicate partners invested the rest of the B round, $16M, to receive 16/80 = 20%. 

B only: 16/80 = 20% 

A/B: 4/18 = 5% 

**==> picture [88 x 279] intentionally omitted <==**

A only: 20% * .75 = 15% Options: 10% * .75 = 7.5% Founder 2: 30% * .75 = 22.5% Founder 1: 40% * .75 = 30% 

Again, if we add A/B and A we are whole again at pro rata 20%. Note that we own two distinct classes of preferred stock: A and B. Since each was negotiated at a different time under different circumstances, each may have as different preferred rights, such board seats, liquidation preferences, dividends, voting rights, anti-dilution, etc. 

Let’s start a new example and project several rounds of investing. We’ll focus on the Series A investors (us), and our goal is to maintain pro rata. 

- Series A: 5 on 15[47] 

- Series B: 20 on 60 Series C: 100 on 400 Exit: $2B 

**==> picture [418 x 131] intentionally omitted <==**

The first step is to calculate the ownership for each round: 

- Series A: 5 on 15 :: 5/20 = 25% 

- Series B: 20 on 60 :: 20/80 = 25% Series C: 100 on 400 :: 100/500 = 20% 

The Series A investors will first be diluted by 25% and then by 20%. 

- After B: 25% * (1-25%) = 19%. After C: 19% * (1-20%) = 15% 

Eeks, we’ve lost 10 percentage points, from 25% to 15%. That would be $200M out of our pockets with the given exit of $2B! Let’s get it back. 

We’ll need to take 25% of the B round, $20M * .25 = $5M. That $5M investment yields 5/80 = 6%, bumping our diluted 19% back up to 25% after the round. With our new 25% ownership going into the C round, we would be diluted: 

After C: 25% * (1-20%) = 20% 

We’ll avoid this by taking 25% of the C round, $100M * .25 = $25M, which yields 25/500 = 5%. We are back up to 25% and have avoided losing $200M. Our proceeds from a $2B exit would be $500M. 

## Series A Golden Rule 

As we discussed in the are of a fundraising chapter, we investing out fund that we raised from LPs with a specific strategy, which includes an 

average total amount invested in each startup. When we are performing due diligence on a Series A deal, we don’t know exactly what the future rounds will be, but we do know that we will have a limited amount of funding available for that startup. 

Most VC firms reserve 2-3X of the Series A investment to be used to minimize dilution in future rounds. Recall that this is somewhat theoretical, in that we don’t actually have any cash put in a separate account. Rather, when a Series A investment is made, the firm will make an internal note that a certain portion of the fund is reserved for follow-on rounds for that startup and cannot be used for other purposes, like a Series A in another startup. 

The goal is to maintain pro rata as much as possible on our winners, we are still some dilution. In the on understanding likely to suffer example the preceding page, we were able to maintain pro rata by following the $5M Series A investment with another $30M in follow-on funding. This would not likely be feasible, as our future funding would be limited by the funds we reserved for this startup, 2-3X of $5M, or $10-15M. 

**==> picture [418 x 135] intentionally omitted <==**

Given the 2-3X follow-on limitation, let’s revisit the problem and see how we are affected by dilution. Looking at the previous page, we invested $5M in the Series B to maintain a 25% position. This is still feasible, as it doubles our Series A investment, leaving another $5-10M for the C. 

In the C round we run out of available funding and would not be able to invest the $25M required to maintain pro rata. If we invested the maximum we reserved, the last $10M, in the C round, with a post of $500M, we would earn 10/500 or 2% ownership. Our previous stake would have been diluted to 20%, as calculated on the previous page. This new 2% would bring us back to 22%. 

**==> picture [418 x 130] intentionally omitted <==**

In this example, our 2-3X golden rule is not enough to prevent all dilution. However, it does a pretty good job. With no follow-on rounds, we would have been knocked down to 15%. By reserving 2-3X of our Series A investment, we were able to maintain 22% out of the 25% coming out of the A round. 

## SERIES A GOLDEN RULE: 

## Reserve 2-3 times the Series A investment to participate in later rounds to minimize the effects of dilution (and attempt to maintain pro rata). 

## Growth Milestones Summary 

The concept of funding milestones is critical to understanding how to think like a VC. Milestones are the building blocks of value creation. We are in the business of creating unicorns, which means tackling BIG ideas and creating enormous value. Milestones are the roadmap to achieving those big goals. 

As we perform due diligence on a Series A investment, the use of funds should be tied to specific milestones to reduce risk and increase the valuation for the next round of funding on the way to a successful exit. Our biggest enemy in future funding is dilution. The golden rule of Series A investing is to reserve 2-3X of our investment for follow-on rounds in an attempt to maintain pro rata as best we can given the limitations of our fund strategy. 

## Due Diligence Advice for Students: Growth Milestones 

From the perspective of a Series A investor, we are looking for future milestones, the ones we hope a startup can achieve with our investment. Any previous milestones should be noted under Traction and will have increased the pre-money valuation of this round. Milestones noted here will be achieved with the “use of funds” of our investment. This will be our “money in” component of the return analysis. 

Our founders may (again) not be the best authorities on the growth trajectory we need to achieve on our way to exit. That means VCs often must come up with their own plan here for milestones. These should be directly tied back to any risks or opportunities you noted in your analysis in earlier sections. If you identified a new compelling market in product/market fit, you need to create milestones to prove out that market. If you noted a technology lead in your team/industry analysis, you likely need to create a milestone for maintaining that lead. 

Some milestones may be for the current round. Others should be planned for future rounds. Be assertive: you might pick a different amount of funding to hit fewer or more milestones each round than the founders pitched. 

As we are looking at a startup from the VC fund perspective, it may not be surprising that many founders have not deeply considered this point of view. It is imperative that the milestones you choose here are in sync with your analysis above, not necessarily with the founders, though you do need to gauge whether the founders could 

get on board with the direction you are suggesting they take. Remember we are in the Fund Fit section. We have to be able to make the math work. However, the calculus we employ to do so, along with the changes in strategy our new numbers may imply, those also have to work for the founders. It’s a dance. 

Lastly, be prepared to revisit this section after have worked on Return you Analysis. You may need to adjust your investment size, which implies you may be going after different milestones. Be prepared to iterate! 

**==> picture [458 x 84] intentionally omitted <==**

## 8. Exit Strategy 

The Story about the Number: Money Out. 

## Startups are like 9-Volt Batteries 

As we’ve noted many times before, venture capitalists never make an investment without some vision of how to exit that invest ment. We are not buy-and-hold investors. Our strategy is to buy, grow and sell. The majority of the growth is locked in equity growth which comes in the form of increased valuations (share prices) of privately held companies. 

To use an electrical analogy, equity growth is like voltage, also known as potential. Voltage in and of itself doesn’t do anything. A 9-volt battery just sits there if it is not connected to anything, but it has the potential to deliver nine volts when connected to a circuit. If you fly a kite into a high voltage utility wire, that circuit might go right through you to the ground. The high voltage line has the potential to kill you. 

When we build up equity value in a startup, we similarly create potential that requires a catalyst to unlock. For startups, this comes in the form of an exit, or liquidity event. Without an exit, all the value is locked away like a 9-volt battery. 

Our limited partners, LPs, supplied the cash that we invested to build all that value, and in return, they expect cash back. They’re not interested in a junk drawer full of batteries. 

To extend the metaphor, the exit strategy is the design of the final circuit to realize the full potential of the startup. You wouldn’t plug a 9-volt battery into your household circuit and expect it to run your refrigerator. Nor would you plug the high-voltage line straight into a kid’s toy. 

Different startups will have different exit opportunities, as we first explored back in the VC Job Cycle: Exit chapter. If we have a unicorn on our hands that will achieve steady revenues north of $100M, we may be 

considering an IPO. If we have another unicorn on our hands that generates no revenue, we will want to identify the best acquisition candidates. 

The Exit Strategy for a VC investment in a startup is the plan for the end that includes a whole host of how much value can we create questions: with venture funding, how big should we try to get before exiting, when is the best time to exit, can we go public, who might acquire us and why? 

## Beach House Example 

In the we were previous chapter, when discussing building equity (p. 259), I mentioned a scenario in which we inherit a million-dollar beach house, thereby making us “equity rich.” Let’s take that same scenario and think about a few different exit strategies and the motivations that might lead us to choose one strategy over another. 

The we consider is the first strategy might quickest path to liquidity (cash wealth). For that, we might put the house on the market immediately for a reduced price, say $800,000. That might get us a buyer within a week or so. 

If we are not in need of immediate cash, we would likely be rewarded for being a little more patient. We could enlist a real estate agent and start the normal home a going through selling process, which could easily take few months. This may require a few thousand dollars up front for some minor repairs, for which we’d have a good shot at getting the full million dollars or a maybe even more if a bidding war ensued. 

However, it is not guaranteed. While this scenario would likely provide a higher return, it comes with the trade-offs of upfront negative cash flow (to fix a few things), a longer payback period and a little more uncertainty. 

One last scenario, in looking at other properties in the neighborhood, we might conclude that our house has the potential to be worth $1.5 million if we make some significant upgrades. We estimate it would cost $100,000 and take an extra three months. Of course, home improvement projects are rife with risks. There could be delays and cost overruns. Also, with an extra delay to the exit, there is a higher chance of some unexpected disaster. Still, for this extra risk, we would be rewarded with extra proceeds of perhaps $400,000. 

Table: Comparing exit strategies for inherited beach house 

|Exit Strategy|Investment<br>Time<br>$0<br>0-1 month<br>$5k<br>2-3<br>months<br>$100K<br>5-8<br>months|Proceeds|Uncertainty|
|---|---|---|---|
|<br>1. Quick Sale<br>2. Regular<br>Sale<br>3. Fixer<br>Upper||$800K<br>$1M<br>$1.5M|~10%<br>~20%<br>~30%|



Which exit strategy is best? Of course, it depends. There are several factors to consider, including our need for immediate cash, our appetite for uncertainty, our willingness to oversee a project, our access to cash to fund a project, our interest in making more money and our confidence that the real estate market will remain stable. 

My guess is that most of us would choose option #2 and go through the normal home selling process. Seems like a good balance between risk and reward, and likely fits the risk profile of most people. 

But if our sole goal was to maximize financial return, we’d find a way to raise the $100K so that we could get the extra $500,000. There are not many opportunities out there to earn half a million dollars on a $100,000 investment in a few months. Think of the things you could accomplish with an extra $400,000 lying around! 

VCs, of course, are out to maximize financial returns for our limited partners. If a VC were involved in the transaction above, the first thought would likely be, how much more could the house be worth if we put more money to work?! (If this were the game of Monopoly, we’d tear the house down and build a hotel!) 

## Should we add a Swimming Pool? 

VCs would ask, what other milestones could we hit with more investment that would significantly increase the ultimate purchase price? Add a swimming pool? Milestone. New mother-in-law apart ment? Milestone. Five-car garage? Milestone. 

The exit strategy is the plan to maximize financial returns by investing in milestones that generate the highest sale price. In essence, we are estimating the cumulative value of all the milestones. 

Just like a beach house may not need a swimming pool, a startup may not need to go after a new market to prove its value to an acquirer. We may not need a Series C to achieve a higher level of revenue if we have an acquirer that wants our technology. 

In formulating an exit strategy, we are making educated guesses as to how potential acquirers will value the mile stones we are funding. 

I should stop for an important point about vision and ambiguity. I do not mean to imply here that VCs have crystal clear vision of the exit for every investment, any more than real estate agents know for sure that adding a swimming pool is going to be worth the investment. 

However, it is an important part of a VC’s job to have insights about value creation in a particular industry, and more specifically, how that value will be priced by acquirers or the public markets. The future may well be fuzzy, but we must have at least some blurry vision about the value that future acquirers will perceive we have built. 

Part of this comes through our constant study of M&A markets, keeping track of who is acquiring what and why. If a big tech company is on an acquisition buying spree, we absolutely need to know that. We might even be making some Series A investments largely predicated on that fact. 

Conversely, if we know that no acquirers are looking for hardware manufacturers, we might not invest in that space unless we believe we have the potential to build a public company, because otherwise there is no end game. Or we might make the investment despite the poor outlook in the acquisitions markets because we believe that the immense amount of value 

that will be created will disrupt that trend. Don’t forget, we are optimistic contrarians. We need to know the rules to know when we want to be exceptional. 

## Exit Valuation 

**==> picture [116 x 113] intentionally omitted <==**

If multi-millionaires are not paying more for beach houses with swimming pools, we’re not going to add a swimming pool. It is an important part of our job to know what our buyers want to spend money on. In the startup world, this metaphorical swimming pool can be translated into three fundamental things that acquirers are looking for, often in combination: revenue, customers and capabilities. 

## Revenue 

Revenue is the most straightforward of the three and the easiest to price. Most industries have revenue multiple ranges that are relatively easy to attain and commonly accepted. By achieving a revenue milestone, assuming revenues are the primary driver of an acquisition or that we are pursuing an IPO, we can estimate an exit valuation. 

Revenue multiples go up and down rather dramatically depending on macro market conditions. Back after the dotcom bubble had burst, for example, it was important to understand the acronym EBITDA (earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization), which is basically net income for a venture-backed startup because tech startups can’t borrow (no interest) are not profitable (no taxes) and don’t make capital investments (no depreciation or amortization). 

Back in the early 2000s, when the exit horizon was were decade rocky, revenue multiples normally applied to EBITDA. A later, things had changed considerably. Since the early 2010’s, markets have been up and multiplies are now more commonly attached to gross income. There are exceptions to every rule, but multiples for venture-backed companies often fall in the 5-10X range. 

It is to revenue are important keep multiples in perspective. First, we talking about the exit, when we have hit numerous milestones and built an impressive company. We should not be using a revenue multiple to come up with a pre-money valuation for a Series A startup. 

Second, we are talking about a circumstance in which revenue is the driving factor for the acquiring company, that is, the acquirer is making largely a financial transaction rather than a strategic one. 

When those two points are present, a revenue multiple can be a reason able method of roughly estimat ing exit valuation, akin to an average price per square foot for a house in a particular area. Those real estate numbers are distilled from numerous properties that have at least one thing in common: proximity. 

Similarly, revenue multiples are a distillation of numerous company valuations in a particular industry. With a multiple, we’ll get a number, but we’ll still need a story about the number. 

Some houses are going to deserve a higher-than-average price per square foot. Perhaps they have higher-end fixtures or maybe even a swimming pool. Similarly, every startup is unique, and we need to be careful about putting too much faith in a number derived from averages. 

One important factor that should be considered when applying a multiple is growth trajectory. Not every company in an industry has the same potential to grow. If we expect our startup to double revenue each year 

for the next several years, we’d expect a higher multiple than a more mature company that might be doing well to grow 10% year over year. 

Another factor that can affect multiples is profitability, or more technically, margins. This one is a little trickier. If our multiple is being applied to EBITDA, then it is already incorporating margins because we are taking out our costs before we apply the multiple. 

However, if we are using a gross revenue multiple, margins should have an influence. Within a given industry, there are going to be some companies with the ability to charge premium prices or that have cost structure advantages. These companies will ultimately generate more profits, and their multiples should reflect this advantage. 

The points above apply to startups that may be acquired for largely financial reasons or that could go public. There is another consideration for a startup that might get acquired: strategy. When a startup is acquired, it is often for numerous reasons, and applying a multiple in that situation can be is a mistake often made media after a misleading. This by the big acquisition for a high price. This is news, look at that multiple! 

A was the good example acquisition of AppDynamics by Cisco in 2017 for $3.7B. At the time, AppDynamics had revenues nearing $200M, close to a 20X multiple of topline revenue and well above the norm. at a Coincidentally, AppDynamics had been preparing to go public valuation below $2B, meaning the financial markets had valued AppDynamics under 10X of revenue. Cisco swooped in at the last minute and paid nearly twice as much. 

The difference in the price set by the financial community and the price paid by Cisco can be answered in one word: strategy. AppDynamics had some sort of metaphorical swimming pool that was valued by Cisco and not by bankers. Sure enough, an article two years later discussed how Cisco was into its “central for incorporating AppDynamics nervous system” [48] . enterprise computing 

Clearly Cisco was going to leverage more than the revenue from AppDynamics. It also had plans to leverage its customers and capabilities. 

## Customers and Capabilities 

In 2012, Facebook paid $1B for a two-year-old startup that had zero revenue. Of course, no multiple could explain that price. However, Instagram had about 30 million users and was a serious threat to a core Facebook value proposition: photo sharing. 

Though this example is extreme, it illustrates the value of nonfinancial elements of an acquisition target. Facebook did not want to wait for Instagram to develop a business model and start generating revenue. Facebook wasn’t necessarily after the (short-term) revenue. Rather, they had a complex calculus that included a different (better?) way to share photos, 30 million users and a talented team. 

Another example of a strategic acquisition is the “acquihire,” a new term coined because it started to become somewhat commonplace for bigger tech companies to acquire startups largely to get access to its talent. You usually can’t ask cofounders to quit their job to come work for you. They have employees and customers that they don’t want to let down. If you want the benefit of their talents at your sitting-on-alot-of-cash tech company, you can acquire their startup. 

Of the revenue must materialize in some course, ultimately way. The bottom line for corporations in the U.S. is financial, and acquisitions must ultimately make financial sense. The point for us as investors in early stage startups is that we may be able to build a startup that satisfies a strategic need, and hence not need to take the additional risk and expense of developing a sustainable business. 

With no sustainable business, multiples don’t make any sense. It’s like putting a price per square foot on a vacant lot. The price per square foot assumes there is a house there that shares some 

characteristics of the other houses from which we derived the index. need revenue and assume that revenue is the Similarly, multiples key driver of value. 

The pharmaceutical industry is another good example. Over the last several decades, big pharma companies have lowered their R&D (research and development) budgets and increased their M&A activity, allowing biotech startups and the VCs that back them to take the early risk of developing new drugs. 

When a biotech startup has a potential new drug that performs well in clinical trials, a pharma company may swoop in and acquire the company. These cases are and is pre-revenue pre-customer. The acquirer specifically going after capabilities, ones that likely have very strong intellectual property protection. 

Given these strategic influences, it is no surprise that using multiples is often a poor methodology for estimating exit valuations. They may be a starting point, but unless we’ve got a startup going public or the unusual circumstance of a purely financial acquisition, we need other tools to help us. 

## Comps 

Once again, we take some guidance from real estate, another industry that deals with illiquid assets. The best tool for estimating exit valuations is identifying comps, or comparables: other startups that have already exited. 

VCs have inside information about the private deals that have occurred across our network. Somewhere in all the deals that we know about, there are some that share characteristics with the startup that is in front of us today. It is through that knowledge that we create a story about a number to estimate an exit valuation. 

There are two challenges to using comps. One is finding them in the first place. Secondly, we need to know their valuation to be useful in our estimates. 

To identify comps, we should keep in mind that there are several ways a could be considered to the comp comparable startup we’re looking at. Variables to consider include: the problem/solution, level of disruption, team, competitive landscape, scalability and pretty much every other section of the VC Razor. Sometimes a from another era when comp may come another was be industry going through similar changes. No comp will perfect, but the more variables there are in common, the more confident we may be that our startup could perform similarly. 

When with student teams who are new working to an industry, I recommend some identifying industry keywords and conducting an intensive internet search to try to find comps. Once we’ve identified some candidates, we need to know everything we can about their funding: how much raised and at what valuations. VCs already have this information or know who to call to find out. For anyone new to the industry, it can be quite a challenge to find numbers for these private deals (see due diligence advice for students at the end of the chapter). 

Through comps and perhaps adding multiples we can create a story that helps explain the number we choose for an exit valuation estimation. Like market sizing, it helps to have numerous sources and data points, all tied together with a narrative to explain a particular estimation. 

## Exit Strategy Summary 

The exit strategy is our success story, a vision toward which we will be steering this ship. Or maybe it’s a raft and we’re paddling in white water. We don’t necessarily have a ton of control, but we do need confidence that we are headed in the right direction. 

For VCs, success is measured in one way: financial returns. Our exit strategy will include a best guess of the most likely liquidity event. If we think we have a startup that could reach $50-100M in steady, predictable revenue, then it may be suitable for an IPO, and we can use a revenue multiple to estimate an exit value. 

If not, we need to identify what acquirers would be most interested and what metrics use for a we consider might they purchase price. As potential acquirers, we need to revisit our growth milestones from the to make sure we are kind of previous chapter building the company our acquirers would want to pay (the most) for. 

In addition to our story about an exit, we need a number. Our exit strategy should have an estimate of an exit valuation based on comps or multiples or both. This number is the “money out” element of our Fund Fit, whereas our Growth Milestones described our “money in.” In the next chapter, we take both of those numbers (and stories) to determine whether we can make the math work. 

**==> picture [238 x 161] intentionally omitted <==**

## Chapter Questions 

1. What does it take for a startup to be a good candidate for an IPO? 

2. Prior to exit, why is (illiquid) equity more important to a VC when growing a startup than (liquid) cash? 

3. What is the minimum return required for a VC investment? 

4. Why is it important to combine narrative and numbers when defining an exit strategy? 

5. What is flawed about DCF as a method of determining return potential? 

6. Why are milestones important as we look toward an exit? 

7. Give an example of the three-step process for calculating return potential. 

## Due Diligence Advice for Students: Exit Strategy 

Our founders may again not be the best authorities on the exit strategy. VCs must do their own diligence here. We need two things: 1) named potential acquirers; and 2) an estimate of an exit valuation. Like Market Size, we hope to create a story about a number here with multiple data points. This will be the “money out” aspect of the Return Analysis. 

To identify potential acquirers: get specific and name the companies you think might acquire this startup. Identify big companies in the industry. Research their M&A activity. Google the company name along with “acquires” and “acquisition” and “M&A” to see if you can learn their acquisition trends. Is there a range of acquisition prices? Is there a common strategic goal? Speculate as to what kind of company we’d need to build for them (milestones we need to hit) and what range of price they might pay. Revisit the previous chapter’s analysis using the new information you’ve gathered here, adjusting your growth milestones and funding to match your exit strategy. 

To identify comps: we have two challenges: finding startups that could be comps and then finding the valuations to estimate an exit valuation. Google industry keywords to try to identify other companies/startups that could be comps. Once you have a startup name, find out everything you can about its funding. How much did it raise? At what valuations? How much was it acquired for. Much of this information may be unobtainable, but you may get lucky if you look it up on Crunchbase. See if your university subscribes to Pitchbook, 

Capital IQ or Thomson ONE. Google the company name along with “acquired” or “acquisition.” 

**==> picture [458 x 84] intentionally omitted <==**

## 9. Return Analysis Can we make the math work? 

In this section we’re going to build on everything we’ve learned, both strategically and mathematically. We’ll take the “money in,” the number described by the story of our Growth Milestones, and the “money out,” the number explained by our Exit Strategy. Here, we will try to make the math work. 

Let’s do this with a seed investment out of our hypothetical $75M VC Razor Fund II. 

## VC Razor Fund II, First Investment 

Jeff and Mark were excited to announce they had closed Fund II, oversubscribed at $75M. The new fund had the same investment strategy as VC Razor I: 10-12 early stage deals, typically starting with seed investments. The difference this time is that they could average closer to $6M per startup rather than the $4-5M in Fund I. 

They have found a startup they are very excited about. A couple of Carnegie-Mellon undergraduate engineering students have dropped out of school to create a co-working productivity tool. This is right in the sweet spot for VC Razor Ventures: young, smart, tech-savvy entrepreneurs who already have a product in the marketplace gaining traction, but who could really use the guidance of some investors who have been there 

before. Jeff and Mark already have some great ideas about how to gain market share quickly. 

The young founders, we’ll call them YFs, have pitched that they need $100,000 to get to profitability. They say that, with their current user base and the additional customers a new marketing campaign would bring in, they would never need additional funding. 

Jeff and Mark don’t exactly disagree, but they have a bigger vision. True, the YFs only need about $100,000 right now to do some marketing and add some product features. However, if that investment works, VC Razor Ventures would want to raise a $1M Series A round to start going to the next level with new product features that would appeal to a larger market. 

Assuming the YFs hit the Series A milestones, Jeff and Mark think there is a potential to after a market with another go bigger segment round of financing with the goals of reaching 100,000 paying users, 3 specific new product features and at least one strategic partnership with a major tech company. Building a company that could handle that growth would require a Series B in the $5-10M range. 

At that point, Jeff and Mark could see things going a few different ways. The strategic partner mentioned above could be interested in acquiring the startup, probably in the $75-100M range, or a C round could position it well for a $200-300M exit. Another possibility is that more strategic partners may come on board, bringing the idea of an IPO into consideration. 

VC Razor Ventures coached the YFs about this possible bigger trajectory, including multiple rounds of VC financing, as opposed to the $100K 

to profitability strategy the YFs had pitched. VC Razor Ventures projected, assuming milestones were hit and the didn’t the economy implode, that startup could raise several rounds of funding. On the back of a napkin, they sketched out this potential scenario: 

Seed round: $100K investment on a $1M pre-money valuation 

Series A: $1M on $2M Series B: $5M on $10M Series C: $20M on $40M Exit: $200M 

Adding all the rounds of investment above, the total raised would be approximately $26M. If they could find a buyer in the $200M-$300M range, it would be a terrific success for both the founders and VC Razor Ventures. They estimated that the founders might retain 20% of the equity for exit, worth around $40-60M at exit. 

## Breaking it Down 

As we’ve said many times already, when VCs first look at a startup, we are thinking holistically about the entire life of the investment, not just the Series A. We will map out in our heads a best guess as to every round of investment on the way to exit. 

In Mark and Jeff’s scenario, we start with a $100,000 seed round, followed by a Series A, B and C. Each follow-on round dilutes the ownership of earlier shareholders. In this case, each follow-on round happens to be diluting by 33% (1 on 2, 5 on 10 and 20 on 40). This is just a coincidence that falls within the 20-40% norm. 

We’ll walk through the math in a bit, but here is an overview of dilution from each round: 

**==> picture [408 x 222] intentionally omitted <==**

Every round projected is an up round, of course, as we are forecasting success. The seed investment of $100,000 on a $1M pre-money yields a post-money of $1.1M. The Series A has a pre-money of $2M. This implies that milestones must have been hit, as the value has increased approximately 1.8X. 

**==> picture [186 x 185] intentionally omitted <==**

The seed investors’ $100,000 invest ment is now worth $180,000 (on paper), and the $1M worth of stock owned by the founders, as determined by the seed round pre-money valuation, is now worth $1.8M. The image to the left shows the pre-money breakdown; the $1M investment is not in there yet. (Series A is a 1 on 2 round.) 

Similarly, between the A and B rounds, we are projecting an up round from the Series A post-money of $3M to the Series B pre-money of $10M, or a 3.3X increase. Finally, from Series B to Series C we see another increase of 2.7X, from a post of $15M to a pre of $40M. 

**==> picture [418 x 296] intentionally omitted <==**

With the $200M exit projected in this case, the splitting of proceeds is represented in this pie chart. 

**==> picture [238 x 241] intentionally omitted <==**

The seed investors and founders, while suffering dilution, benefit from all the up rounds. Multiply them all (1.8 x 3.3 x 2.7 x 3.3) and you get just over 54X on the way to a $200M exit. Series A enjoys three bumps, 3.3 x 2.7 x 3.3, or 30X. Series B has two jumps, 2.7 x 3.3, or 9X. 

**==> picture [360 x 226] intentionally omitted <==**

It’s clear why a VC firm would want to participate in the early rounds, with 55X and 30X returns for the seed and Series A. However, very little capital was put to work. If VC Razor II took all the seed and Series A but did not participate in later rounds, we would not have returned even half of 

their $75M fund. Total proceeds for seed and A are $5.4M + $30M = $35.4M. 

After the Series A, VC Razor Ventures owned 39% (6% from the seed and 33% for Series A). If they were able to maintain pro rata through the B and C rounds, holding at 39% at exit, they would indeed return the fund with this investment: 39% of $200M = $78M. 

We will now dive into the weeds of the math. The algebra gets pretty complicated, and we’ll use a cap table to keep up with all the moving parts. However, I will attempt to keep it all in perspective so that we don’t fall too deep into a spreadsheet! 

## Seed Round 

In the seed round, this particular startup had low capital needs ($100,000) to hit the next milestones. Some VC firms would not bother with an early stage investment like this. They would pass and ask the startup to come back when they were ready for their first institutional round. 

**==> picture [52 x 115] intentionally omitted <==**

However, VC Razor Ventures’ strategy is to find early stage deals like this. The purpose of the seed investment is to get an early seat at the table for the Series A, as the returns from seed investing will not likely move the needle on the fund (see the previous section: total returns on the seed investment were $5.4M). 

For the seed round, VC Razor Ventures invested $100,000 on a $1M pre-money valuation, resulting in a 9% ownership stake. 

VC % ownership = investment/post 

$100,000/$1,100,000 = 9% 

On a cap table, the seed round would look like this:[49] 

Figure: Capitalization table of seed round 

**==> picture [454 x 145] intentionally omitted <==**

The cap table shows all the detail of creating and pricing shares. This will be very important once we’ve made the investment. We’ll want to track things very accurately. However, at the projections stage when we are trying to make a determination about the level of investment and pre-money valuation for an early round, I would not recommend getting to the level of detail of a projected cap table. As you’ll see at the end of this chapter, it is overkill for the purposes of an exit scenario analysis. 

Let me repeat, the cap table is absolutely necessary post-investment. It is a critical tool for tracking equity. Where it is less helpful is when we are simply making back of the napkin projections as we decide about a current early stage investment. There is so much prognostication that it renders accuracy pointless, even dangerous, lest we start to have too much faith in the numbers we’ve concocted. That fancy cap table spreadsheet looks right, don’t you think? The complicated formulas are working; so the numbers must be right! 

## Series A 

Looking forward to the Series A, VC Razor Ventures is planning to take the entire round, projecting a 1 on 2, meaning a negotiated $1M investment on a negotiated $2M pre-money valuation. (Of course, these things could change. We’re guessing about future rounds.) 

**==> picture [100 x 153] intentionally omitted <==**

Series A Ownership: $1M / $3M = 33% 

If the Series A goes as planned, it would create 33% new equity, diluting all pre-Series A shareholders by 33%. 

Though I have one for you below, we don’t need a cap table to do this math. It’s preferable to keep it simple so that we can do the math with some degree of accuracy in our heads. Taking 1/3 off the founders’ 91% ownership puts them at about 61%; a third off of VC Razor Ventures’ seed stake of 9% puts it at 6% post-A. 

New Diluted Ownership = Old Ownership x (1 - % dilution) 

**==> picture [110 x 12] intentionally omitted <==**

Dilution hurts, but there is good news in that our diluted percentage is now worth more due to the higher valuation. Our original $100,000 is now worth around $180,000 (6% of $3M post-money). 

**==> picture [458 x 164] intentionally omitted <==**

Digging into a cap table for this round, note that the new share price is $1.82, up from $1 in the seed round. The new share price is calculated by 

taking the new pre-money valuation from the upcoming round and dividing it by the number of pre-existing shares: 

New Share Price = Pre-Money Valuation ÷ Total Shares after Previous Round 

$2M ÷ 1.1M shares = $1.82 

To determine the number of shares that will be issued to the Series A investors, we need to take the investment, $1M, and divide it by the new share price: 

Number of New Shares Issued = New Investment ÷ Share Price 

$1M ÷ $1.82 = 550,000 Series A shares issued 

You can see in the cap table that VC Razor Ventures owns a total of 650,000 shares: 100,000 from the seed round and 550,000 from the newly issued Series A. While it is imperative to know how this works if you are going to become a VC, it is actually a bit overkill and distracting from the big picture of return analysis. 

**==> picture [55 x 136] intentionally omitted <==**

In other words, we don’t really need to know that we own 550,000 shares. Instead, we can simply add up our percent ownership: 6% for seed and 33% for the Series A, totaling 39% post Series A. 

As we are not planning to cash out yet, it really doesn’t matter what the shares are worth at the very moment. That said, we can calculate that 39% of the post-money of $3M is just under $1.2M, which makes sense: it’s our $1M Series A investment plus the near doubling of our $100K seed investment. 

Coming out of the Series A, we have the approximate percentage of ownership that we’d like to maintain by reserving capital (2-3X of our Series A) for follow-on rounds. 

## Series B 

Going into Series B, we anticipate a 5 on 10 round. This implies that the startup made significant progress, increasing in valuation from Series A post of $3M to Series B pre-money of $10M, representing more than a 3X jump. 

**==> picture [241 x 343] intentionally omitted <==**

With a 5 on 10 round, the Series B investors will own 33% of the equity after the round: 

Series B Ownership: $5M / $15M = 33% 

Our previous ownership stake of 39% will get diluted by 33%: 

39% x (1 – 33%) = 26% 

**==> picture [94 x 105] intentionally omitted <==**

To maintain pro rata, VC Razor Ventures will participate in the B round. If we were trying to maintain exactly 39%, we’d take exactly 39% of the new round. But we’re VCs. Let’s go with 40% of the $5M round, or $2M for VC Razor Ventures. That will leave $3M for syndicate partners. We will split the new 33% equity 60/40. That’ll be 20% for them and 13% for us. 

**==> picture [142 x 314] intentionally omitted <==**

Our total ownership stake after the Series B will be our diluted 26% from Series A post plus our newly purchased Series B 13%, totaling 39%. So far, 

we’ve invested $100,000 + $1M + $2M. Total in: $3.1M. 

Again, we can see all the details of the shares being issued in a cap table. Our 1,650,000 shares from the previous round carry forward. The new premoney valuation of $10M puts the share price at $6.06. At that price, the Series B investors receive a total of 825,000 shares, bringing the total share pool up to 2,475,000, Post B. 

**==> picture [458 x 204] intentionally omitted <==**

## Series C 

**==> picture [140 x 413] intentionally omitted <==**

Ding, ding, round 4. VC Razor Ventures has modelled a 20 on 40 round for Series C. This once again means that the startup has hit some milestones and increased valuation, another up round, this time from $15M post-B to $40M pre-money for C, nearly 3X. 

**==> picture [100 x 231] intentionally omitted <==**

With a 20 on 40 round, the Series C investors will own 33% of the equity after the round: 

Series C Ownership: 

$20M / $60M = 33% 

**==> picture [114 x 381] intentionally omitted <==**

Our previous ownership. stake of 39% will get diluted by 33%: 

**==> picture [124 x 12] intentionally omitted <==**

To maintain ~40% pro rata position through the Series C, we would need to take 40% of the $20M. However, 40% of $20M is $8M, and we cannot 

## Fund Fit Alert!
