In this $75M fund, it stated above that we average $6M per startup in our portfolio. We’ve already invested $3.1M through the Series B. Another $8M would put us at over $11M, way too much exposure to one startup in the portfolio. 

Let’s stick with our game plan of $6M per startup and reserve $3M for this $20M Series C. Yes, we will take a hit on dilution, but VC Razor II was not intended to be a later stage fund. We did not sell our LPs on later stage investing. 

**==> picture [86 x 129] intentionally omitted <==**

Going with $3M for us and $17M for our syndicate partners, we would end up with 5% new equity (3/60), with the rest of the syndicate getting the other 28% (17/60). 

If we add 5% to the 26% we had Post B, we are now at 31%. We did not entirely avoid dilution, but we hung in there pretty well. 

Once again, a cap table will show us all the details of adding a 20 on 40 Series C: 

**==> picture [368 x 648] intentionally omitted <==**

## To the Exit 

VC Razor Ventures participated in every round, from seed through Series C. We took 100% of the seed and A rounds; we had syndicate partners for the B and C rounds. VC Razor (VCR) shares are in black. 

**==> picture [458 x 321] intentionally omitted <==**

With a $200M project exit, our new equity split will look like this, with all our slices in black: 

**==> picture [271 x 274] intentionally omitted <==**

We can break down our investments, equity and proceeds in a table: 

|Round|Investment|Equity at<br>Exit|Proceeds|Multiple|
|---|---|---|---|---|
|Seed|$100,000|2.7%|$5.4M|54X|
|Series A|$1M|15%|$30M|30X|
|Series B|$2M|9%|$18M|9X|
|Series C|$3M|5%|$10M|3X|
|Total|$6.1M|31.7%|$63.4M|10X|



Ultimately, after all of this hard work going round by round and accurately calculating the dilution, we are projecting a 10X return on an average investment for our fund ($6M), that comes close to returning our $75M fund. 

## Back to the Forest 

We don’t need to delve into all of the technicalities above to make some guesses for exit scenarios. Remember, we are doing this return analysis to inform our decision on the intial institutional round, specifically, 

on negotiating the investment size and the pre-money valuation for the Series A. 

Rules of thumb can serve us well. First rule, split your overall investment over several rounds. VC Razor II was designed with an average of $6M per startup. We modeled this exit scenario on that average. 

Second, reserve 2-3X for follow-on rounds, which can be restated as, do not invest more than 1/3 of the total investment in the Series A. In this case, we only invested 1/6 in the Series A. As we look back on this analysis, we might want to increase that to get a higher percent ownership out of the gate. 

For example, had we invested $1.5M on the $2M pre-money in the Series A, we would have received about 43% equity in that round rather than 33%. The “1.5 on 2” round would have diltued our seed stake by 43%, from 9% to about 5%. Add that 5% to our new Series A 43%, and we come out of Series A with 48%. 

Would the founders balk at this extra diltuion in the Series A? Perhaps, but we haven’t changed their pre-money valuation of $2M. They do not lose any value of their shares, still worth $1M post-A. Plus, they get the benefit of an extra $500,000 to put toward growing their business. This might be what we have to do to make the math work. 

Another consideration is the age of the founders and the fact that they are first-timers, having never raised venture capital, let alone taken a company to a successful exit. Given their relative inexperience and the arguments from the previous paragraph, we may be able to negotiate to increase the investment size, keeping the pre-money at $2M, resulting in a higher percent ownership for VC Razor Ventures. 

It is important to think about the strategic implications of this extra $500K. The startup will have considerably more cash. What are they going to do with it? Will hit even they bigger milestones, thereby increasing the valuation by a larger factor, an even bigger up round to the Series B? Or will it simply give them some comfort that they won’t run out of cash, aiming at the same goals? Depending on our answers to those questions, we may need to adjust our projections. For example, we may want to increase or projected valuation on the B 5 we pre-money round. Originally a on 10, perhaps should now project a 5 on 15 round. 

We also have to consider what the extra investment does to the math of our return analysis. Coming out of the A round, the post money will be $3.5M rather than $3M. For the startup to achieve 20X returns, it will need to achieve at least a $70M valuation rather than $60M. The higher the postmoney valuation coming out of a round of financing, the higher the exit will need to be to achieve the same returns. 

These are the are as strategic questions VCs mulling over they consider a Series A investment. These are the reasons that they have shortcuts so that they can iterate their projections quickly. 

As we iterate our return analysis, in this case considering a $1.5M Series A investment rather than $1M, we do not want to go through the entire process of the previous 10 pages. VCs have shortcuts that allow quick iterations of various scenarios. 

I apologize for the repetition, but I must say it again: it is imperative that VCs understand the math behind the last 10 pages. It is an important aspect of the job. However, as this book is focused on strategy, and more specifically the decision-making process for new opporunities, we will focus on the shortcuts that VCs employ as they iterate projection in return analysis. 

These shortcuts are a product of industry rules of thumb, what we’ll call norms. 

## Series A Norms 

When VCs that the math has to make sense mean that say , they they need to be able to chart out on the back of an envelope how this startup might render an acceptable (or terrific) return after considering multiple rounds of financing. 

Clearly, this forward-looking calculation is fraught with broad assumptions, many of which have become norms. Frustratingly for newcomers, these norms are largely set by and known only by VCs, though the Internet and accelerators are changing that information gap for the startup world, much as the Internet did for real estate in a previous generation. 

20-40% Ownership Norm 

An example of a typical norm is that VCs usually take between 2040% of the equity in a tech startup in a Series A. This is a product of the underlying principle that the team is the most important component of a successful startup. Attracting amazing talent is a top priority, and sharing the equity is the proven tactic. 

Given that norm, a “3 on 3” Series A ($3M investment on a $3M premoney) would be unusual. The VCs in that situation would be taking 50% of the startup. Let’s understand that this may happen. Venture capital is an industry often defined by exceptions. As I’ve said before, it is important to know the rules so that you’ll know which ones you want to break. 

What exceptional circumstances might lead to a 50% equity deal in a Series A? Let’s start with the problem that necessitates the rule. We don’t usually take 50% because we do not want to over-dilute the founders, and we are anticipating future rounds (further dilution). If we have a startup that may not need future rounds, it is possible that we might reconsider the rule. 

Other possibilities include anything that might bring extra risk to the table, such as an extremely inexperienced team, exceedingly unstable technology or perhaps the lack of traction. 

Another possibility, and frankly the most pressing one, the startup has no other term sheet options. No other firms are interested. If there is no other firm to bid the price up, a startup might have to take the only term sheet offered, depending on their need for cash to survive. If the startup can survive without cash, it is better to forego the bad term sheet, hit more milestones and come back to market with a more fully baked cake, if you will. 

$3-15M Pre-Money Norm 

Another norm is that tech startups in a Series A are usually valued between $3-15M, and investment sizes are usually between $3-15M. The high side of the range (and higher) represents Silicon Valley. The low side is the rest of the industry. One study reports the average Series A is up from around $4M in 2008 to over $11M in 2018.[50] There are definitely exceptions to any norm, but having a baseline provides an opportunity to strategize about fund management. 

## Up Rounds Norm 

Another norm is the projection that future rounds will go up, in both the amount invested and the valuations. The goal is for startups to use the VCs’ cash to hit milestones, and in so doing, create value. As a startup gets larger, more and more capital will be required to fuel growth. 

## Reserve 2-3X for Follow-Ons Norm 

One more norm is the practice of reserving 2-3X of the Series A investment to maintain pro rata through future rounds. Not all firms have this exact strategy but understanding the purpose of this norm helps us appreciate how and why some firms choose to deviate from it. Some firms for example have a “spray and pray” philosophy, investing in far more early stage startups than they could ever expect to follow to exit. Clearly, the strategy supposes many more will fail than in an average VC fund. 

Other more conservative funds may only reserve 1X, as they expect more doubles and triples in their portfolio rather than a few home runs and many duds. 

There are two other norms we need to consider: the fund fit norm that comes from our managing a portfolio of at least a dozen startups, implying that we would very rarely invest more than perhaps 10-15% in one company. 

10X Return Norm 

Lastly, we have our hit thesis norm, in which we expect a few of our investments to far outperform the others. The classic California-style metric here is to return the fund (10-20X), or we may have a regional strategy in the 5-7X range. 

**==> picture [266 x 242] intentionally omitted <==**

## Return Analysis Summary 

Estimating our return analysis is simple in concept (money out ÷ money in), but very challenging justifying the underlying strategic assumptions. In other words, the numbers are easy, the story is hard. As slight aspects of the story change, the numbers can change significantly. VCs need to be able to iterate the math quickly so that we can test different strategies and assumptions. 

Using the norms listed above, VCs can run Series A candidates through numerous return analysis scenarios to ensure that the investment 

For example, suppose we have a $150M VC fund and there is a startup in front of us seeking $2M in funding at a pre-money valuation of $6M. Off the top of our heads, we can determine this would imply 25% ownership for us (2 on 6, post of 8). We’d typically reserve another 2-3X for follow-on rounds to maintain pro rata. We might plan on a total of $8M invested for around 25% at exit, depending on the average investment per startup we are targeting with our fund. 

Then we must figure out what the exit needs to look like. If we want to potentially return the fund, we’ll need $150M at exit. If we own 25%, the exit valuation would need to be $600M. 

The question becomes, do we believe that this startup can become a half billion-dollar from this If we have a vision for company starting point? how to make that we think the chances of are happen, and getting there reasonable, it’s a go. 

Maybe we think the exit will be closer to $450M. In that case, we’d need to own 33% to return our fund of $150M. Perhaps we offer $2M investment at a pre-money of $4M. Notice the current pre-money valuation changed based on our assessment of the startup’s exit potential, not due to any calculated value of the current business. Valuations of startups are all about potential, and that means we have to have a vision for the finish line. Foundry Group’s Brad Feld put it th is way an excellent blog entry about deal algebra: 

For early-stage companies, venture investors are normally interested in owning a particular fraction of the company for an appropriate investment. The valuation is actually a derived number and does not [51] really mean anything about what the business is “worth.” 

Back to our startup, perhaps we think it could become a $600M company, but we believe to get there it would need to go faster and bring in new senior level talent. We might offer to take the lead and build a syndicate for a $6M Series A on an $8M pre, adding a 20% option pool to help attract new chief marketing and technology officers. 

Notice that the pre-money valuation doubled in this scenario, but not as a function of its current inherent “worth.” Rather, it was an outcome of identifying the optimum exit scenario and creating a funding strategy to get there. 

Using VC investing norms, we can test different scenarios that ultimately lead back to a Series A offer that includes an investment size, premoney valuation and option pool. 

## Due Diligence Advice for Students: Return Analysis 

Apply all the norms you’ve learned about venture investing to the current startup and project a path to exit (a story) to include funding for milestones (money in), an exit strategy (money out) and a summary return statement, such as, “10X return on a $8M investment over 7 years.” 

Iterate, iterate, iterate! If the math does not work on the first try, change some assumptions to see if you can make it work. 

Return analysis includes your offer on a Series A investment, which should include a premoney valuation, investment size and option pool, all of which should reflect your analysis of the opportunity. 

A lot of my students get hung up on “problems” with the startup or play “gotcha” with assertions the founders have made. Translate those value judgments into numbers. If a startup doesn’t have any traction or its team is young, those are not “problems.” They are opportunities to identify milestones that we could fund, thereby increasing the valuation. 

A “problem” in any section of the Razor (such as low traction, in experienced team, no secret sauce…) should equate to a lower premoney. Those are all opportunities. If we can identify a milestone that “de-risks” a “problem,” we will create value. 

The job of the VC is to price that extra risk and provide the funding to hit the milestones that will increase the valuation for the next round. It takes years of experience to learn how to identify and price these risks/opportunities. That is why 

venture capital is considered an apprentice vocation. Students should make best your effort based on of the your understanding underlying principles. 

Also, get comfortable with heading in the direction versus for right looking right and wrong answers. Students aren’t expected to come up with a realistic exit strategy, as the nuances of different industries can be to for an impossible decipher outsider. However, your logic and research can be in the right direction. 

And I’ll repeat: iterate, iterate, iterate! If you make a set of assumptions and the math does not work, see if there is a way to make it work by changing some assumptions that will still be true to your analysis of other sections of the Razor. 

It is okay (in fact, normal) to conclude that the math does not work, but it is the VC’s job to see if there is any way to make it work. Is there an alternative strategic approach that could make this work? For example, perhaps a smaller seed investment to hit a specific early milestone could de-risk the deal enough to position it for a Series A next time.

## Due Diligence Advice 

The advice below is reprinted from the boxes at the end of each chapter. 

## Due Diligence: Value Proposition 

Do not take the founders’ word for it! Push back on assertions of value creation. Find your own words to describe the value proposition. Be very granular in your search for specific pain points tied to specific target customer segments. Prioritize customers segments (and size each of them in the next chapter). You may need a separate value prop statement for each segment. Be prepared to revisit value prop after an in-depth investigation of the competition in chapter 5. 

Be prepared to size each segment you identify (next chapter). 

You also may need to address the competition in this section to answer the questions better/faster/cheaper. That is, you may need to include specifics about what the competition is offering and how this startup is differentiated. As much as possible, you need to become an expert in this space so that you understand how value is created. 

## Due Diligence: Market Size 

Do not take the founders word for it! Use your assumptions and your research, not the founders’. You need to size each segment identified in the previous chapter, including a narrative with multiple numerical data points (TAMs, current market trends, competitors’ sales). Focus on bottom-up efforts (TAM: X customers x Y price) because this demonstrate your expertise in the space. Calculate several TAMs for each segment using different methodologies and sources. Tie it all together. Cite your sources. 

## Due Diligence: Traction 

Traction is where many VCs spend the most time in due diligence, specifically talking to current, past and potential future customers. This is where the rubber meets the road for product/market fit. Get granular! We need a very robust story around any numbers. If the startup has customers, we want to know everything we can about them. 

Students should similarly focus their due diligence efforts here. Unfortunately, students performing due diligence as part of an exercise are somewhat hampered because we do not want to disrupt a startup’s business by contacting their customers, particularly if the startup is B2B. One exception is if you already know people in your network whose behavior would not be disrupted by your questions. 

Since we cannot rely on customer interviews, we must get creative with online research. Google the startup name along with adjectives that might highlight successes or failures. Some examples: startup name + reviews / my favorite new company / problems / made me sick / saved my life / happy customers. 

Note that the founders may be our only source of information about current customers. Take advantage of any Q&A you have with founders to find out everything you can about customers. You need to be assertive in these Q&A sessions, as founders will tend to gloss over some important details if they don’t reflect well on the startup’s offerings. Dig, dig, dig. In later chapters, you’ll find that the founders are probably not our best authority on the competition or growth milestones, for example. However, for their own current customers, they may be the best source. 

## Due Diligence: Team 

Normally, VCs would spend scores of hours with founders as well as talk to customers, employees, strategic partners and former employers as part of the due diligence process. Since none of these are possible, students will have to make some educated guesses based on limited time together. Ask questions that help you gauge what type of people you are working with. In a limited amount of time, you’ll have to assess whether you could make good partners. 

In your analysis, start with and focus on the CEO. The rest of the team is very important, but the CEO plays the most critical role. Check bios on the company website. Check everyone’s LinkedIn profiles. Google individuals along with some keywords like “problems” or “complaints” to see if you can uncover any issues. Point out any holes on the team and go the extra mile to see if you can find suitable candidates to fill those holes. Remember, real VCs add value with their extensive networks. We 

are simulating that competency here. Bring solutions, not just observations. 

## Due Diligence: Secret Sauce 

Competition: Don’t take your founders’ word for it! Founders are often surprisingly lacking in their understanding of the competition. Put on your Sherlock Holmes hat and Google, Google, Google. Use creative keyword searches to find potential competitors. Perhaps use a keyword suggestion tool. See if there is a trade organization that has industry overview information. Learn the lexicon of the industry. Demonstrate a macro level understanding of the space as well as depth of knowledge about key players. Use 2x2, Venn or product attribute chart to summarize findings. Make sure to revisit your product/market fit after you better understand the competitive landscape. 

Secret sauce: Assess both the current situation and the future potential. If the startup has a competitive advantage today, is it sustainable over time? What investment and strategy will that require? Alternatively, if a startup does not currently have an advantage, is there a way we can create and maintain one with our investment? This is an area of analysis in which we need to be speculative and assertive. Remember, this is barriers to entry, not differentiation! 

## Due Diligence: Business Model 

Focus on identifying and removing barriers to scale, both current and future. Use numbers when appropriate but focus on the narrative. Do not count on founders to understand our exit-driven strategy, meaning be strategic and assertive about the kind of growth-oriented business model a VC fund needs to see (whether or not the founders are pitching that model). Reconnect with product/market fit, a major determiner of customer adoption, as well as competitive threats. 

## Due Diligence: Growth Milestones 

From the perspective of a Series A investor, we are looking for future milestones, the ones we hope a startup can achieve with our investment. Any previous milestones should be noted under Traction and will have increased the pre-money valuation of this round. Milestones noted here will be achieved with the “use of funds” of our investment. This will be our “money in” component of the return analysis. 

Our founders not be the best authorities on the may (again) growth trajectory we need to achieve on our way to exit. That means VCs often must come up with their own plan here for milestones. These should be tied back to risks or directly any opportunities you noted in your analysis in earlier sections. If you identified a new compelling market in product/market fit, you need to create milestones to prove out that market. If noted a lead you technology in your team/industry analysis, you likely need to create a milestone for maintaining that lead. 

Some milestones be for the current round. Others should be may planned for future rounds. Be assertive: you might pick a different amount of funding to hit fewer or more milestones each round than the founders pitched. 

As we are looking at a startup from the VC fund perspective, it may not be surprising that many founders have not deeply considered this point of view. It is imperative that the milestones you choose here are in sync with your analysis above, not necessarily with the founders, though you do need to whether the founders on board with the direction gauge could get you are suggesting they take. 

Remember we are in the Fund Fit section. We have to be able to make the math work. However, the calculus we employ to do so, along with the changes in strategy our new numbers may imply, those also have to work for the founders. It’s a dance. 

Lastly, be prepared to revisit this section after you have worked on Return Analysis. You may need to adjust your investment size, which implies you may be going after different milestones. Be prepared to iterate! 

## Due Diligence: Exit Strategy 

Our founders may again not be the best authorities on the exit strategy. VCs must do their own diligence here. We need two things: 1) named potential acquirers; and 2) an estimate of an exit valuation. Like Market Size, we hope to create a story about a number here with multiple data points. This will be the “money out” aspect of the Return Analysis. 

To identify potential acquirers: get specific and name the companies you think might acquire this startup. Identify big companies in the 

industry. Research their M&A activity. Google the company name along with “acquires” and “acquisition” and “M&A” to see if you can learn their acquisition trends. Is there a range of acquisition prices? Is there a common strategic goal? Speculate as to what kind of company we’d need to build for them (milestones we need to hit) and what range of price they the the new might pay. Revisit previous chapter’s analysis using information you’ve gathered here, adjusting your growth milestones and funding to match your exit strategy. 

To we have identify comps: two challenges: finding startups that could be and then valuations to estimate an exit comps finding the valuation. Google industry keywords to try to identify other that could be a companies/startups comps. Once you have startup name, find out everything you can about its funding. How much did it raise? At what valuations? How much was it acquired for. Much of this information may be unobtainable, but you may get lucky if you look it up on Crunchbase. See if your university subscribes to Pitchbook, Capital IQ or Thomson ONE. Google the company name along with “acquired” or “acquisition.” 

## Due Diligence: Return Analysis 

Apply all the norms you’ve learned about venture investing to the current startup and project a path to exit (a story) to include funding for milestones (money in), an exit strategy (money out) and a summary return statement, such as, “10X return on a $8M investment over 7 years.” 

Iterate, iterate, iterate! If the math does not work on the first try, change some assumptions to see if you can make it work. 

Return analysis includes your offer on a Series A investment, which should include a pre-money valuation, investment size and option pool, all of which should reflect your analysis of the opportunity. 

A lot of my students get hung up on “problems” with the startup or play “gotcha” with assertions the founders have made. Translate those value judgments into numbers. If a startup doesn’t have any traction or its team is young, those are not “problems.” They are opportunities to identify milestones that we could fund, thereby increasing the valuation. 

A “problem” in any section of the Razor (such as low traction, in experienced team, no secret sauce…) should equate to a lower pre-money. Those are all we milestone that “de-risks” opportunities. If can identify a a “problem,” we will create value. 

The job of the VC is to price that extra risk and provide the funding to hit the milestones that will increase the valuation for the next round. It takes years of experience to learn how to identify and price these risks/opportunities. That is why venture capital is considered an apprentice vocation. Students should make your best effort based on your understanding of the underlying principles. 

Also, get comfortable with heading in the right direction versus looking for right and wrong answers. Students aren’t expected to come up with a realistic exit strategy, as the nuances of different industries can be impossible to decipher for an outsider. However, your logic and research can be in the right direction. 

And I’ll repeat: iterate, iterate, iterate! If you make a set of assumptions and the math does not work, see if there is a way to make it work by changing some assumptions that will still be true to your analysis of other sections of the Razor. 

It is okay (in fact, normal) to conclude that the math does not work, but it is the VC’s job to see if there is any way to make it work. Is there an alternative strategic approach that could make this work? For example, perhaps a smaller seed investment to hit a specific early milestone could de-risk the deal enough to position it for a Series A next time. 

## Glossary 

- ADOPTION RISK The risk that customers will not adopt a new solution. In the Razor, we mitigate this risk by demonstrating traction. 

- ANCHOR LP A limited partner with a large (more than 10%) stake in a VC fund. 

- ANGEL INVESTORS Individuals or groups who, like VCs, make equity investments in early stage startups, but unlike VCs, do not have LPs. 

- BARRIER TO ENTRY Something that inhibits competitors from getting into a market space (like very high development costs, exclusive contracts, entrenched infrastructure…). This can be a competitive advantage. 

- BOARD OF DIRECTORS The governing body of a company; the CEO’s boss, though often the CEO is also on the board and sometimes is chairman. Unlike an advisory board, this board has a legal (fiduciary) duty to the shareholders. In new ventures it is best to keep the board small and an odd number (Series A is often 3 or 5 members). 

- BOARD OF ADVISORS A group of individuals recruited by the entrepreneur for advice. They have no fiduciary duties and may meet as formally or informally as the entrepreneur desires. 

- BURN RATE The speed at which a startup is spending (“burning through”) cash, generally monthly or weekly. 

- CAP EX Capital expenditures: big ticket items needed, like factories, real estate, large equipment. 

- CAP TABLE Capitalization table: a spreadsheet that shows % ownership in a venture, including # and % of shares held by founders, investors, key employees, as well as allocated to the option pool for future key employees. 

- CAPITAL CALL When VCs request capital from LPs to invest in a startup. 

- CARRY The % of proceeds that a VC firm receives after the LPs have recouped their investment, most commonly 20%. 

- CLOSED (A FUND) Finished fundraising and able to invest in startups 

- CONVERTIBLE DEBT A loan that can convert to equity in the next round; a creative way to do an angel deal without pricing the round 

- CUSTOMER PAIN The metaphorical discomfort that customers must suffer without our products, which indicates potential customer demand. The higher the pain, the lower the “adoption risk.” 

   - COMPETITIVE Something that will give the venture a leg up on competitors, ADVANTAGE often related to IP. It usually requires a significant competitive advantage to attract VC funding. 

   - DEAL A round of financing. For venture capital, these are labeled Series A, Series B, etc. 

   - DECACORN Startup that reaches a valuation of $10B (10X a unicorn). DILUTION The decrease in percent ownership suffered by early stakeholders as new equity is issued. 

   - DOWN ROUND An equity round in which the pre-money valuation is lower than the post-money from the last round. 

   - DRY POWDER Capital that VCs reserve for follow-on rounds, for example, often 2-3X of a Series A investment. 

- EARLY STAGE, LATER Refers to the maturity of a new venture, generally associated with STAGE rounds of VC funding. The latest stage can be an IPO or an acquisition (venture gets bought by another firm). 

- ELEVATOR PITCH A 2-3 minute verbal presentation that an entrepreneur should be prepared to give in case there happens to be an investor in the elevator with you. 

      - EQUITY Ownership in the form of shares of stock in a venture. EXIT An event that allows founders and private investors the opportunity to cash out by selling their shares in the venture, either through an acquisition or, much rarer, an IPO. 

   - EXIT STRATEGY Tactics employed to maximize value of the startup at exit. FATAL FLAW Something in (or missing from) the business plan that indicates to the reader that the venture will definitely not work – immediately eliciting a NO-GO decision. 

- FEE STRUCTURE Fees charged by the VC fund: management fees and carry, most commonly 2/20 (2% fees, 20% carry). 

- FOLLOW-ON ROUNDS Subsequent rounds of funding in a startup after an initial institutional round, i.e., Series B, C, etc. 

- FUND OF FUNDS An LP that aggregates capital from other, larger LPs to invest in VC funds. 

- GENERAL PARTNERS The people who actively manage the partnership. In a venture (GPS) fund, the general partners (GPs) are the VCs who do the investing. 

- ILLIQUID ASSET Something that is not easy to transfer, such as a startup or house. 

- INSTITUTIONAL ROUND A round of equity investment by institutional investors (VCs), not angels. The Series A is the first institutional round. 

- IP, INTELLECTUAL Non-tangible assets that have value. Includes patents, trademarks, PROPERTY copyrights and trade secrets. Often used in the context of a sustainable competitive advantage. 

- IPO Initial public offering, aka, “going public,” refers to selling shares of the company on the open market, usually with NASDAQ or NYSE (New York Stock Exchange). See Exit. 

- IRR Internal Rate of Return: similar to ROI, to measure return on investment. Given as an annual percentage. 

- LIMITED PARTNERS OR The institutions and high net worth individuals who put up the LPS money for VCs; they do not have an active role in the firm. 

- LIQUID ASSET Something that can be very easily transferred (like cash) 

- LIQUIDITY EVENT See exit. 

- MANAGEMENT FEE T he amount a VC firm charges the LPs to run the firm (salaries, rent, travel, etc.), most commonly 2% annually. 

- MEZZANINE FUND A later stage VC fund that invests later stage, just before exit. MILESTONES Value-creating goals tied to rounds of investing that reduce risk and increase valuation. 

- OPPORTUNITY COST When choosing between two options, the opportunity cost is the value of the option you do not choose. 

- OPTION POOL A percentage of ownership in the venture that is set aside to be given as incentives to future employees. 

- OVER SUBSCRIBE Raise more money than targeted. 

- PAIN POINT Refers to a very specific need that would motivate a customer to make a purchase decision. 

- PARTIAL CLOSE In fundraising, an announcement that some goal has been achieved, though the final objective has not been met. 

- POST-MONEY The negotiated value of the startup just after investment. VALUATION Investment + pre-money = post-money. 

- PRE-MONEY The negotiated value of the startup just before an equity VALUATION investment. 

- PREFERRED SHARES Like friends with benefits, “preferred” shares are a class of stock that have special rights, like liquidation preferences, anti-dilution, dividends, voting rights, etc. 

- PRICE ELASTICITY A measure of how sensitive customers are to price changes. Inelastic implies that demand doesn’t change much even when the price does (like gasoline). 

- PRICING THE ROUND Negotiating a pre-money valuation for a VC investment. PRO RATA Maintain the same ownership % through future rounds, i.e., don’t get diluted. 

- REVENUE MODEL Part of the business model that deals with how customers and the company transact. Examples include subscription model, simple transactions, service contract, etc. 

- PROOF OF CONCEPT A way of mitigating the risk that something might not work. Closely related to milestones and value creation. 

- PRO FORMA In a business plan, this means projected financial statements, generally done by month for the first 2-3 years and by year for 5 years. 

- RISK PROFILE Willingness to take risk, and correspondingly the amount of potential reward expected from the risk. For example, if one is highly risk averse, one would need a very large potential payoff to be willing to take a risk. 

- ROUND (OF INVESTING) The total amount of money given to a new venture (in exchange for equity) at a given time. Rounds often have names, like: “friends and family round”, pre-seed, seed, angel, Series A, Series B, etc. 

- SCALABILITY The ability of a startup to grow quickly with enough stability to make it to exit. 

- SERIES A The first round of institutional financing into a startup (from venture capitalists). 

- SERIES B, C, D… Subsequent rounds of equity financing from VCs. SWEAT EQUITY The value of the founders’ labor while starting the venture, often used in the context of negotiating pre-money valuation. 

- SYNDICATE Noun: A group of investment firms who are all participating in the same round of investing in a venture. Verb: to create a syndicate. 

- TERM SHEET A contract that lays out the terms of the venture capital investment. Part of the process, not the end. 

- UNICORN Startup that reaches a valuation of $1B. 

- UP ROUND An equity round in which the pre-money valuation is higher than the post-money from the previous round. Opposite: down round. 

- USE OF FUNDS This is the term of art for the startup’s plan to spend the capital it raises from VCs. 

- VALUE NETWORK The network of constituencies that may be affected by a startup, including customers, buyers, users, suppliers, competitors, employees, general public. 

- VALUE PROPOSITION A brief statement that quickly summarizes a startup’s differentiated solution addressing customer pain points. 

- VCIC Venture Capital Investment Competition; a reality-based competition in which startups pitch to students playing the role of VCs. 

- VENTURE CAPITAL The legal umbrella/entity/partnership under which a group of FIRM venture capitalists organize for the purpose of raising VC funds. 

- VENTURE CAPITAL A partnership through which the GPs (general partners, VCs) FUND raise funds from LPs (limited partners, institutions) for the purpose of investing in startups. Funds generally have 10-year lives. VC firms may have multiple VC funds.

## About the Author 

Patrick Vernon has been directing the world’s largest venture capital competition since he was the president of the MBA Entrepreneurship Club at the University of North Carolina’s Kenan-Flagler Business School in 2003. After graduating, Patrick joined UNC Kenan-Flagler, largely to run the Venture Capital Investment Competition (VCIC), which has grown to include over 80 universities representing 13 countries on 3 continents. 

In 2006, Patrick began teaching at UNC, and by 2008 had a full course load of entrepreneurship and venture capital classes for undergraduate and graduate business students. The first iterations of this book were used as the course pack for early versions of his BUSI 506 class, renamed Entrepreneurial Strategy: How to Think Like a Venture Capitalist. 

Prior to business school, Patrick spent a decade in Los Angeles as an artistic entrepreneur. With his folkadelic party rock band, the Zookeepers, Patrick performed over 500 shows across the American west. The Zookeepers recorded and released four CDs.
